Rule of the conditions of the four figures: (predication) on the major is affirmative; for negation is negation of predication, and predication is only affirmation. That is as in the major premise of the first, second, third, and eighth moods of the fourth figure. So the first two moods have fallen under both parts of the second disjunction, and it is also on the basis of mutual exclusion (man' al-khuluww) like the first. And here the indication to the conditions of productivity is completed with (affirmative) predication on the major, so what Mirza Jan said is repelled: that it would be better to say 'or its affirmation of the major' etc.; for predication in common usage is more general than being affirmative or negative, so it does not benefit affirmative only, unlike affirmation; for it is only for affirmation. 'For' etc. the fa is for causation, meaning we only said 'affirmatively' because negation etc. (Burhan) And predication is only affirmation: that is in reality; for the meaning of predication is the union of two differing things in some manner, and applying the term 'predicative' to the negative is for similarity. (Burhan) And the eighth (mood) without the fourth and seventh; for their major premises are negative, without the sixth; for its major premise, though affirmative, its minor premise is negative particular, so it does not enter under the universality of the middle term's subject, without the fifth; for its major premise is also negative. (Burhan) So the first two moods: for the first is composed of two universal affirmatives, so in its minor is found the meeting with the minor (i.e., predication of the minor on the middle actually), and in its major the predication on the major, and likewise the second mood, which is composed of a universal affirmative and a particular affirmative, unlike the rest; for in them universality of the middle term's subject is not found except with one of the two parts of the second disjunction, as is not hidden. So the first two moods have fallen, meaning that the first and second moods of this figure fall under his statement: 'with its meeting with the minor' and also under his statement: 'or its predication on the major', so the disjunction between the two statements is on the basis of mutual exclusion, not mutual inclusion, just as the first disjunction is also on the basis of mutual exclusion, not mutual inclusion; because its two parts unite in the third and fourth moods as will appear to you, so there is no harm in their uniting under them. (Isma'il) So it (i.e., the second disjunction) is on the basis of mutual exclusion, just as the first disjunction was, meaning his statement: 'either from universality of the middle term's subject or from universality of the major's subject.' Like the first: meaning just as the first disjunction, i.e., his statement: 'either from universality of the major's subject' is on the basis of mutual exclusion, so there is no harm in its two parts uniting as in the third and fourth moods of the fourth figure. (Burhan) And here the indication is completed, meaning that in his statement: 'either from universality of the middle term's subject' the indication is incomplete, and when he said: 'or its predication on the major' the indication is completed. 'With its actual meeting with the minor' — it included all moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure. So memorise. And know that he did not say: 'or to the major' i.e., or with its meeting with the major, despite it being more concise; because 'meeting' includes position and predication as previously mentioned, so it would follow that the syllogism arranged in the form of the first figure with a universal affirmative major and a negative minor would be productive, and it would also follow that the syllogism arranged in the form of the third figure with a negative minor and an affirmative major with universality of one of its premises would be productive. All moods of the first figure etc.: For the conditions of productivity in all moods of the first figure are affirmation of the minor and its actuality and universality of the major, and likewise the conditions of productivity of all moods of the third figure are only affirmation of the minor and its actuality and universality of one of the two premises. So he indicated to affirmation of the minor and its actuality in both parts by his statement: 'meeting of the middle with the minor actually,' and he indicated by his statement: 'universality of the middle term's subject' to universality of the major in the first figure and universality of one of the two premises in the third figure. As for the six moods of the fourth figure, i.e., the first, second, third, fourth, seventh, and eighth, it appears from his statement: 'universality of the middle term's subject' universality of the minor in all of them, and he indicated by his statement: 'with its meeting with the minor' to affirmation of the minor and its actuality in four of these six, which are the first, second, fourth, and seventh. So it appeared from his statement: 'universality of the middle term's subject, its meeting with the minor, and its predication on the major' all conditions of the first and third and also some conditions of the fourth. He did not say 'or to the major' — an objection, its explanation: that the author, intending conciseness, should not have said 'or its predication on the major' but rather should have said 'or the major' by coordinating it with the minor so that the meaning would be: or with its meeting with the major. And his statement: 'because meeting etc.' is its answer, and its explanation is clear. So understand. (Isma'il) So it follows: for universality of the middle term's subject is realized here because the major is universal with its meeting with the major; for the major is predicated in the major premise of this syllogism, and its meeting with the major is more general than the major being predicated on it or vice versa as passed. (Isma'il) And it follows etc.: for universality of the middle term's subject with its predication on the major is realized here. As for the first, because of the assumption of universality of one of the two premises. And as for the second, because the major is affirmative; for the middle is united with the major by its predication on it, and meeting includes position and predication. (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (hāshiya) on the rule (ḍābiṭa) stipulating the conditions for productivity of syllogisms in the four figures. The text begins by stating the rule: the major premise must be affirmative because negation is the negation of predication, and predication is essentially affirmation. This is exemplified by the first, second, third, and eighth moods of the fourth figure, whose major premises are affirmative. The commentator (Burhān) then explains that the first two moods of the fourth figure (both affirmative) fall under both parts of the second disjunction of the rule: 'either universality of the middle term's subject or predication of the middle on the major.' This disjunction is exclusive (manʿ al-khuluww), meaning both conditions cannot be absent simultaneously, but they can both be present. The second disjunction mirrors the first (which concerned universality of the major's subject). The commentator Ismāʿīl clarifies that the first two moods of the fourth figure satisfy both 'meeting with the minor' and 'predication on the major', so they are covered by both branches. The text then notes that with the completion of the rule (including 'predication on the major'), all moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure are productive. The author did not say 'or with its meeting with the major' to avoid implying productivity of invalid syllogisms (e.g., first figure with negative minor). The objection by Mīrzā Jān is addressed: the phrasing 'predication on the major' is better because 'predication' in common usage includes both affirmation and negation, whereas 'affirmation' is only for affirmative, so the rule explicitly requires affirmative major. The commentary then details how the three conditions (universality of the middle, actual meeting with the minor, predication on the major) cover different figures and moods. For the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and actual, and the major universal. For the third figure, the minor must be affirmative and actual, and one premise universal. For the fourth figure, the six productive moods are listed, with the condition of universality of the middle applying to all, and actual meeting with the minor applying to four of them. Finally, the objection about not using the shorter expression 'or to the major' is refuted because 'meeting' includes both position and predication, which would wrongly include invalid forms. The entire discussion is a precise examination of syllogistic validity conditions from the perspective of post-classical logic commentators.

A) It must be universal.

Key Takeaway
The major premise in a productive syllogism must be affirmative because predication is fundamentally an affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid categorical syllogism about a fiqh ruling (e.g., 'All intoxicants are forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden') and verify that the major premise is affirmative.
?
Explain why a syllogism with a negative major (e.g., 'No temporally originated thing is without a cause; the world is temporally originated; therefore the world has a cause') is not technically in the first figure because the major is negative, and thus does not meet the condition for productivity in that figure.

The Two Disjunctions and Mutual Exclusivity
Explanation of the two disjunctions in the rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two disjunctions in the rule and explain why they are exclusive (man' al-khuluww) rather than inclusive.

Core Idea
The disjunctions are exclusive: both conditions cannot be absent, but both can be present.

This lesson focuses on the two disjunctions presented in the rule: one concerning universality of the subject, and the other concerning predication on the major. The commentators clarify that these disjunctions are on the basis of 'man' al-khuluww' (prohibition of emptiness), meaning at least one condition must hold, but both may hold simultaneously. This nuance is crucial for understanding which moods are productive.

Guided Path
1
Locate the first disjunction
Find the phrase 'إما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر' (either from universality of the major's subject) and its counterpart. This is the first disjunction.
2
Locate the second disjunction
The second disjunction is between 'ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر' and 'حمل الأوسط على الأكبر'. Confirm from text.
3
Understand 'man' al-khuluww'
'Man' al-khuluww' means that the two parts cannot both be false simultaneously; at least one must be true. The text explicitly says 'على سبيل منع الخلو لا منع الجمع'.
In logic, this is an inclusive disjunction (OR) with the sense that at least one holds.
4
Apply to moods
The first two moods of the fourth figure satisfy both parts of the second disjunction. Other moods satisfy only one part. This confirms the disjunction is exclusive only in the sense of not allowing both to be absent.
The text gives examples: the third and fourth moods have both parts present, which is allowed.

Rule of the conditions of the four figures: (predication) on the major is affirmative; for negation is negation of predication, and predication is only affirmation. That is as in the major premise of the first, second, third, and eighth moods of the fourth figure. 'For' etc. the fa is for causation, meaning we only said 'affirmatively' because negation etc. (Burhan) And predication is only affirmation: that is in reality; for the meaning of predication is the union of two differing things in some manner, and applying the term 'predicative' to the negative is for similarity. Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (hāshiya) on the rule (ḍābiṭa) stipulating the conditions for productivity of syllogisms in the four figures. The text begins by stating the rule: the major premise must be affirmative because negation is the negation of predication, and predication is essentially affirmation. This is exemplified by the first, second, third, and eighth moods of the fourth figure, whose major premises are affirmative. The commentator (Burhān) then explains that the first two moods of the fourth figure (both affirmative) fall under both parts of the second disjunction of the rule: 'either universality of the middle term's subject or predication of the middle on the major.' This disjunction is exclusive (manʿ al-khuluww), meaning both conditions cannot be absent simultaneously, but they can both be present. The second disjunction mirrors the first (which concerned universality of the major's subject). The commentator Ismāʿīl clarifies that the first two moods of the fourth figure satisfy both 'meeting with the minor' and 'predication on the major', so they are covered by both branches. The text then notes that with the completion of the rule (including 'predication on the major'), all moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure are productive. The author did not say 'or with its meeting with the major' to avoid implying productivity of invalid syllogisms (e.g., first figure with negative minor). The objection by Mīrzā Jān is addressed: the phrasing 'predication on the major' is better because 'predication' in common usage includes both affirmation and negation, whereas 'affirmation' is only for affirmative, so the rule explicitly requires affirmative major. The commentary then details how the three conditions (universality of the middle, actual meeting with the minor, predication on the major) cover different figures and moods. For the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and actual, and the major universal. For the third figure, the minor must be affirmative and actual, and one premise universal. For the fourth figure, the six productive moods are listed, with the condition of universality of the middle applying to all, and actual meeting with the minor applying to four of them. Finally, the objection about not using the shorter expression 'or to the major' is refuted because 'meeting' includes both position and predication, which would wrongly include invalid forms. The entire discussion is a precise examination of syllogistic validity conditions from the perspective of post-classical logic commentators.

What is the nature of the first disjunction in the rule?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Mapping Conditions to Productive Moods
Application of the three conditions to different figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map the three conditions (universality of middle, actual meeting with minor, predication on major) to the productive moods of the first, third, and fourth figures.

Core Idea
The three conditions together define the productive moods of the first, third, and part of the fourth figure.

This lesson synthesizes the three conditions and shows how they apply to the productive moods of the first, third, and fourth figures. The passage explains which moods satisfy which conditions, providing a complete map of syllogistic productivity.

Guided Path
1
List the three conditions
From the rule, extract: 'عموم موضوعية الأوسط' (universality of the middle's subject), 'ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل' (actual meeting with the minor), and 'حمل الأوسط على الأكبر' (predication on the major).
Some conditions are disjunctively required.
2
Apply to the first figure
All moods of the first figure require: minor affirmative and actual (meeting with minor), and major universal (universality of middle's subject refers to major universality in first figure).
3
Apply to the third figure
Third figure requires: minor affirmative and actual, and universality of one premise (middle universality).
4
Apply to the fourth figure
Six moods are productive: 1,2,3,4,7,8. All require universality of minor (from universality of middle's subject). Four (1,2,4,7) also require actual meeting with minor. The condition of predication on major applies to those with affirmative major (1,2,3,8).
The text details which moods satisfy which conditions.

ضابطة شرائط الأشكال الأربعة على الأكبر إيجابا؛ فإن السلب سلب الحمل، وإنما الحمل هو الإيجاب، وذلك كما في كبرى الضرب الأول والثاني والثالث والثامن من الشكل الرابع فالضربان الأولان قد اندرجا تحت كلا شقي الترديد الثاني فهو أيضا على سبيل منع الخلو كالأول. و ههنا تمت الإشارة إلى شرائط إنتاج على الأكبر إيجابا فاندفع ما قال ميرزا جان من أن الأولى أن يقول: "أو إثباته للأكبر" إلخ ؛ إذ الحمل في العرف أعم من أن يكون إيجابا أو سلبا، فلا يفيد الإيجاب فقط بخلاف الإثبات؛ فإنه للإيجاب فقط. فإن إلخ الفاء للتعليل يعني إنما قلنا: إيجاباً؛ لأن السلب إلخ. (برهان) وإنما الحمل هو الإيجاب: أي في الحقيقة؛ إذ معنى الحمل اتحاد المتغايرين بنحو، وإطلاق الحملية على السالبة للمشاكلة. (برهان) والثامن دون الرابع والسابع؛ إذ كبراهما سالبة دون السادس؛ إذ كبراه وإن كانت موجبة لكن صغراه سالبة جزئية، فلا يدخل عموم موضوعية الأوسط دون الخامس؛ فإن كبراه أيضاً سالبة. (برهان) فالضربان :الأولان إذ الأول مركب من موجبتين كليتين فوجد في صغراه الملاقاة للأصغر يعني حمل الأصغر على الأوسط بالفعل، وفي كبراه الحمل على الأكبر، وكذا الضرب الثاني وهو مركب من موجبة كلية وموجبة جزئية بخلاف البواقي؛ فإنه لم يوجد فيها عموم موضوعية الأوسط إلا مع واحد من شقي الترديد الثاني كما لا يخفى. فالضربان الأولان قد اندرجا يعني أن الضرب الأول والثاني من هذا الشكل مندرجان تحت قوله: "مع ملاقاته للأصغر" وكذا تحت قوله : "أو حمله على الأكبر" أيضاً، فالترديد بين القولين على سبيل منع الخلو، لا منع الجمع كما أن الترديد الأول أيضاً على سبيل منع الخلو لا منع الجمع؛ لاجتماع شقيه في الضرب الثالث والرابع كما سيظهر لك، فلا بأس باجتماعهما تحتهما. (إسماعيل) فهو أي الترديد الثاني على سبيل منع الخلو كما كان الترديد الأول أعني قوله: "إما من عموم موضوعية الأوسط وإما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر. كالأول: أي كما أن الترديد الأول أعني قوله: "إما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر على سبيل منع الخلو، فلا بأس باجتماع شقيه كما في الضرب الثالث والرابع من الشكل الرابع. (برهان) و ههنا تمت الإشارة يعني أن في قوله: "إما من عموم موضوعية الأوسط إشارة ناقصة ولما قال: "أو حمله على الأكبر" تمت الإشارة.
مع ملاقاته للأصغر بالفعل" كانت
جميع ضروب الشكل الأول والثالث وستة ضروب من الشكل الرابع. فاحفظ. واعلم أنه لم يقل: "أو للأكبر" أي أو مع ملاقاته للأكبر مع كونه أخصر؛ لأن الملاقاة تشمل الوضع والحمل كما تقدم، فيلزم كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الأول من كبرى موجبة كلية مع صغرى سالبة منتجا، ويلزم أيضاً كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الثالث من صغرى سالبة وكبرى موجبة مع كلية إحدى مقدمتيه
جميع ضروب الشكل الأول إلخ : فإن شرائط الإنتاج في جميع ضروب الشكل الأول إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها وكلية الكبرى، وكذلك شرائط إنتاج جميع ضروب الشكل الثالث أيضاً ليست إلا إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها وكلية إحدى المقدمتين، فأشار إلى إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها في كلا الشقين بقوله: "ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل" وأشار بقوله: عموم موضوعية الأوسط إلى كلية الكبرى في الشكل الأول وكلية إحدى المقدمتين في الشكل الثالث. وأما الضروب الستة من الشكل الرابع أي الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع والسابع والثامن، فيظهر من قوله: "عموم موضوعية "الأوسط" كلية الصغرى في كلها، وأشار بقوله : "مع ملاقاته للأصغر" إلى إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها في أربعة من هذه الستة، وهي الأول والثاني والرابع والسابع، فظهر من قوله: "عموم موضوعية الأوسط ملاقاته للأصغر وحمله على الأكبر" جميع شرائط الأول والثالث وبعض شرائط الرابع أيضاً. لم يقل أو للأكبر ،اعتراض تقريره: أن المصنف في صدد الاختصار فكان عليه أن لا يقول: "أو حمله على الأكبر" بل أن يقول: "أو "الأكبر" بعطفه على الأصغر حتى يكون المعنى: أو مع ملاقاته للأكبر وقوله: "لأن الملاقاة إلخ" جوابه، وتقريره واضح.

فافهم. إسماعيل)
فيلزم فإن عموم موضوعية الأوسط يتحقق ههنا؛ لكون الكبرى كلية مع ملاقاته للأكبر؛ إذ الأكبر محمول في
كبرى هذا القياس وملاقاته للأكبر أعم من أن يكون الأكبر محمولا عليه أو بالعكس كما مر . ( إسماعيل)
ويلزم إلخ : فإن عموم موضوعية الأوسط مع حمله على الأكبر يتحقق ههنا. أما الأول فلفرض كلية إحدى المقدمتين. وأما الثاني فلكون الكبرى موجبة؛ فإن الأوسط متحد مع الأكبر بحمله عليه، والملاقاة شامل للوضع
والحمل. ( إسماعيل)
Rule of the conditions of the four figures: (predication) on the major is affirmative; for negation is negation of predication, and predication is only affirmation. That is as in the major premise of the first, second, third, and eighth moods of the fourth figure. So the first two moods have fallen under both parts of the second disjunction, and it is also on the basis of mutual exclusion (man' al-khuluww) like the first. And here the indication to the conditions of productivity is completed with (affirmative) predication on the major, so what Mirza Jan said is repelled: that it would be better to say 'or its affirmation of the major' etc.; for predication in common usage is more general than being affirmative or negative, so it does not benefit affirmative only, unlike affirmation; for it is only for affirmation. 'For' etc. the fa is for causation, meaning we only said 'affirmatively' because negation etc. (Burhan) And predication is only affirmation: that is in reality; for the meaning of predication is the union of two differing things in some manner, and applying the term 'predicative' to the negative is for similarity. (Burhan) And the eighth (mood) without the fourth and seventh; for their major premises are negative, without the sixth; for its major premise, though affirmative, its minor premise is negative particular, so it does not enter under the universality of the middle term's subject, without the fifth; for its major premise is also negative. (Burhan) So the first two moods: for the first is composed of two universal affirmatives, so in its minor is found the meeting with the minor (i.e., predication of the minor on the middle actually), and in its major the predication on the major, and likewise the second mood, which is composed of a universal affirmative and a particular affirmative, unlike the rest; for in them universality of the middle term's subject is not found except with one of the two parts of the second disjunction, as is not hidden. So the first two moods have fallen, meaning that the first and second moods of this figure fall under his statement: 'with its meeting with the minor' and also under his statement: 'or its predication on the major', so the disjunction between the two statements is on the basis of mutual exclusion, not mutual inclusion, just as the first disjunction is also on the basis of mutual exclusion, not mutual inclusion; because its two parts unite in the third and fourth moods as will appear to you, so there is no harm in their uniting under them. (Isma'il) So it (i.e., the second disjunction) is on the basis of mutual exclusion, just as the first disjunction was, meaning his statement: 'either from universality of the middle term's subject or from universality of the major's subject.' Like the first: meaning just as the first disjunction, i.e., his statement: 'either from universality of the major's subject' is on the basis of mutual exclusion, so there is no harm in its two parts uniting as in the third and fourth moods of the fourth figure. (Burhan) And here the indication is completed, meaning that in his statement: 'either from universality of the middle term's subject' the indication is incomplete, and when he said: 'or its predication on the major' the indication is completed. 'With its actual meeting with the minor' — it included all moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure. So memorise. And know that he did not say: 'or to the major' i.e., or with its meeting with the major, despite it being more concise; because 'meeting' includes position and predication as previously mentioned, so it would follow that the syllogism arranged in the form of the first figure with a universal affirmative major and a negative minor would be productive, and it would also follow that the syllogism arranged in the form of the third figure with a negative minor and an affirmative major with universality of one of its premises would be productive. All moods of the first figure etc.: For the conditions of productivity in all moods of the first figure are affirmation of the minor and its actuality and universality of the major, and likewise the conditions of productivity of all moods of the third figure are only affirmation of the minor and its actuality and universality of one of the two premises. So he indicated to affirmation of the minor and its actuality in both parts by his statement: 'meeting of the middle with the minor actually,' and he indicated by his statement: 'universality of the middle term's subject' to universality of the major in the first figure and universality of one of the two premises in the third figure. As for the six moods of the fourth figure, i.e., the first, second, third, fourth, seventh, and eighth, it appears from his statement: 'universality of the middle term's subject' universality of the minor in all of them, and he indicated by his statement: 'with its meeting with the minor' to affirmation of the minor and its actuality in four of these six, which are the first, second, fourth, and seventh. So it appeared from his statement: 'universality of the middle term's subject, its meeting with the minor, and its predication on the major' all conditions of the first and third and also some conditions of the fourth. He did not say 'or to the major' — an objection, its explanation: that the author, intending conciseness, should not have said 'or its predication on the major' but rather should have said 'or the major' by coordinating it with the minor so that the meaning would be: or with its meeting with the major. And his statement: 'because meeting etc.' is its answer, and its explanation is clear. So understand. (Isma'il) So it follows: for universality of the middle term's subject is realized here because the major is universal with its meeting with the major; for the major is predicated in the major premise of this syllogism, and its meeting with the major is more general than the major being predicated on it or vice versa as passed. (Isma'il) And it follows etc.: for universality of the middle term's subject with its predication on the major is realized here. As for the first, because of the assumption of universality of one of the two premises. And as for the second, because the major is affirmative; for the middle is united with the major by its predication on it, and meeting includes position and predication. (Isma'il)

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (hāshiya) on the rule (ḍābiṭa) stipulating the conditions for productivity of syllogisms in the four figures. The text begins by stating the rule: the major premise must be affirmative because negation is the negation of predication, and predication is essentially affirmation. This is exemplified by the first, second, third, and eighth moods of the fourth figure, whose major premises are affirmative. The commentator (Burhān) then explains that the first two moods of the fourth figure (both affirmative) fall under both parts of the second disjunction of the rule: 'either universality of the middle term's subject or predication of the middle on the major.' This disjunction is exclusive (manʿ al-khuluww), meaning both conditions cannot be absent simultaneously, but they can both be present. The second disjunction mirrors the first (which concerned universality of the major's subject). The commentator Ismāʿīl clarifies that the first two moods of the fourth figure satisfy both 'meeting with the minor' and 'predication on the major', so they are covered by both branches. The text then notes that with the completion of the rule (including 'predication on the major'), all moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure are productive. The author did not say 'or with its meeting with the major' to avoid implying productivity of invalid syllogisms (e.g., first figure with negative minor). The objection by Mīrzā Jān is addressed: the phrasing 'predication on the major' is better because 'predication' in common usage includes both affirmation and negation, whereas 'affirmation' is only for affirmative, so the rule explicitly requires affirmative major. The commentary then details how the three conditions (universality of the middle, actual meeting with the minor, predication on the major) cover different figures and moods. For the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and actual, and the major universal. For the third figure, the minor must be affirmative and actual, and one premise universal. For the fourth figure, the six productive moods are listed, with the condition of universality of the middle applying to all, and actual meeting with the minor applying to four of them. Finally, the objection about not using the shorter expression 'or to the major' is refuted because 'meeting' includes both position and predication, which would wrongly include invalid forms. The entire discussion is a precise examination of syllogistic validity conditions from the perspective of post-classical logic commentators.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many moods of the fourth figure require the minor to be both universal and affirmative (actual meeting with the minor)?
A) 2
B) 4
C) 6
D) All 6

Key Takeaway
The three conditions jointly determine the productive moods of the first, third, and fourth figures, with each figure having a specific combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the syllogism: 'All humans are mortal; all Muslims are human; therefore all Muslims are mortal.' Identify its figure and check if it satisfies the conditions for that figure.
?
For the fourth figure mood: 'Some animals are mammals; all mammals are warm-blooded; therefore some warm-blooded are animals' (mood 4). Verify if it meets the conditions of universality of the middle's subject and actual meeting with the minor.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Conditions for Productive Syllogisms in the First and Third Figures
Commentary on conditions for first and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the two necessary conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism (minor affirmative and actual, major universal) and for a third-figure syllogism (minor affirmative and actual, at least one premise universal).

Core Idea
The first and third figures require the minor to be affirmative and actual, but differ in where universality must reside.

The commentator begins by summarizing that all moods of the first and third figures are productive. He then unpacks the specific conditions: for the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and actual (ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل) and the major must be universal (عموم موضوعية الأوسط); for the third figure, the conditions are similar except the universality can be in either premise. In this lesson, we will dissect these conditions and apply them to sample syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions from the passage
Find the phrases 'ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل' (meeting of middle with minor in actuality) and 'عموم موضوعية الأوسط' (universal subject-membership of the middle term).
2
Explain 'meeting the minor in actuality'
This means the minor premise must be affirmative (so the middle term actually connects to the minor) and the judgment must be actual, not potential.
For the first and third figures, the minor must be affirmative and actual.
3
Explain 'universal subject-membership of the middle term'
In the first figure, this means the major premise must be universal. In the third figure, it means at least one premise must be universal.
The middle term is universal as a subject in the required premise.
4
Apply to a valid first-figure syllogism
Consider: 'Every intoxicant is haram; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is haram.' Check that the minor (khamr is an intoxicant) is affirmative and actual, and the major (every intoxicant is haram) is universal.
5
Apply to an invalid first-figure syllogism
Consider: 'Some human is not a stone; every stone is a body; therefore some human is not a body.' The minor is negative, violating the first condition, so the conclusion is false even though the premises are true.
Passage provides this example.

ضابطة شرائط الأشكال الأربعة جميع ضروب الشكل الأول والثالث وستة ضروب من الشكل الرابع. فاحفظ. واعلم أنه لم يقل: "أو للأكبر" أي أو مع ملاقاته للأكبر مع كونه أخصر؛ لأن الملاقاة تشمل الوضع والحمل كما تقدم، فيلزم كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الأول من كبرى موجبة كلية مع صغرى سالبة منتجا، ويلزم أيضاً كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الثالث من صغرى سالبة وكبرى موجبة مع كلية إحدى مقدمتيه. جميع ضروب الشكل الأول إلخ : فإن شرائط الإنتاج في جميع ضروب الشكل الأول إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها وكلية الكبرى، وكذلك شرائط إنتاج جميع ضروب الشكل الثالث أيضاً ليست إلا إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها وكلية إحدى المقدمتين، فأشار إلى إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها في كلا الشقين بقوله: "ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل" وأشار بقوله: عموم موضوعية الأوسط إلى كلية الكبرى في الشكل الأول وكلية إحدى المقدمتين في الشكل الثالث. وأما الضروب الستة من الشكل الرابع أي الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع والسابع والثامن، فيظهر من قوله: "عموم موضوعية الأوسط" كلية الصغرى في كلها، وأشار بقوله: "مع ملاقاته للأصغر" إلى إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها في أربعة من هذه الستة، وهي الأول والثاني والرابع والسابع، فظهر من قوله: "عموم موضوعية الأوسط ملاقاته للأصغر وحمله على الأكبر" جميع شرائط الأول والثالث وبعض شرائط الرابع أيضاً. لم يقل أو للأكبر ،اعتراض تقريره: أن المصنف في صدد الاختصار فكان عليه أن لا يقول: "أو حمله على الأكبر" بل أن يقول: "أو الأكبر" بعطفه على الأصغر حتى يكون المعنى: أو مع ملاقاته للأكبر وقوله: "لأن الملاقاة إلخ" جوابه، وتقريره واضح. مع فافهم. إسماعيل) فيلزم فإن عموم موضوعية الأوسط يتحقق ههنا؛ لكون الكبرى كلية مع ملاقاته للأكبر؛ إذ الأكبر محمول في كبرى هذا القياس وملاقاته للأكبر أعم من أن يكون الأكبر محمولا عليه أو بالعكس كما مر . ( إسماعيل) ويلزم إلخ : فإن عموم موضوعية الأوسط مع حمله على الأكبر يتحقق ههنا. أما الأول فلفرض كلية إحدى المقدمتين. وأما الثاني فلكون الكبرى موجبة؛ فإن الأوسط متحد مع الأكبر بحمله عليه، والملاقاة شامل للوضع والحمل. ( إسماعيل) وإما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر مع الاختلاف في الكيف هو منتجا، وقد اشتبه ذلك على بعض الفحول فاعرفه قوله وإما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر: هذا الأمر الثاني من الأمرين اللذين ذكرنا أنه لابد في إنتاج القياس من أحدهما. وحاصله كلية كبرى يكون الأكبر موضوعا فيها مع اختلاف المقدمتين في الكيف، وذلك كما في جميع ضروب الشكل الثاني وكما في الضرب الثالث والرابع والخامس والسادس من الشكل الرابع، فقد اشتمل الضرب الثالث والرابع منه على كلا الأمرين؛ ولذا حملنا الترديد الأول على منع الخلو، فقد أشير إلى جميع شرائط الشكل الأول والثالث كما وكيفا ،وجهةً وإلى شرائط الشكل الثاني والرابع كماً وكيفاً. منتجا لكونه مشتملا على الشروط المعلومة من الضابطة؛ فإن كبرى هذا القياس المرتب لما كانت موجبة كلية فوجد منه عمومية موضوعية الأوسط ملاقاته للأصغر بالفعل، فيلزم أن يكون مشتملا على الشروط المذكورة في ما سبق وليس كذلك؛ لأنه ليس قد وجد إيجاب الصغرى مع أنه ليس بمنتج، ألا ترى أن بعض الإنسان ليس بحجر، وكل حجر جسم صادق والنتيجة الحاصلة أعني بعض الإنسان ليس بجسم كاذب، وقس عليه قوله: "ويلزم أيضاً كون القياس المرتب إلخ. وقد اشتبه ذلك: أي وجه أن المصنف عليه قال: "وحمله على الأكبر" ولم يقل: "أو الأكبر" و لم يكشف غطاؤه على بعض الفحول؛ لكونهم أجانب. أن الفحول: جمع "فحل" هو ضد الأنثى، يقال: للعالم المتبحر، والمراد به العارف الحاجي وتبعه الفاضل الياغنوي. جميع ضروب الشكل الثاني: فإن الأكبر موضوع في كبراه فكليتها واجبة مع الاختلاف في الكيف، وأيضاً الأكبر في كبرى الشكل الرابع موضوع، فكليتها في هذه الضروب موجودة مع الاختلاف.(برهان) فقد اشتمل إلخ فقد اجتمع الأمران في الضرب الثالث والرابع من الشكل الرابع ؛ فإنك قد علمت مما سبق عموم موضوعية الأوسط مع حمله على الأكبر يتحقق في الثالث، وعموم موضوعية الأوسط ملاقاته للأصغر مع يتحقق في الرابع فافهم. إسماعيل) ولذا أي ولهذا الاشتمال المفضي إلى اجتماع الأمرين المرادين أو لا. فقد أشير : أي من قوله: "إما من عموم موضوعية الأوسط إلى قوله: "وإما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر مع الاختلاف في الكيف". والرابع وقد علمت شرائط الضروب الستة من الشكل الرابع سابقا، فيظهر من قوله: "عموم موضوعية الأكبر مع الاختلاف في "الكيف" شرائط الضربين الباقيين من الثمانية أيضاً، فتم شرائطه أيضا بحسب الكيف والكم.
The rule of conditions for the four figures: All moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure. So memorize. And know that he did not say: 'or for the major', meaning 'or with its meeting the major while it is more concise'; because meeting includes both subsumption and predication as previously, so it would follow that the syllogism arranged in the form of the first figure from a universal affirmative major with a negative minor is productive, and it would also follow that the syllogism arranged in the form of the third figure from a negative minor and affirmative major with universality of one of the two premises. All moods of the first figure etc.: For the conditions of productivity in all moods of the first figure are the affirmation of the minor and its actuality and the universality of the major, and likewise the conditions of productivity of all moods of the third figure are only the affirmation of the minor and its actuality and the universality of one of the two premises. So he pointed to the affirmation of the minor and its actuality in both parts by his saying: 'the middle term's meeting the minor in actuality', and he pointed by his saying: 'universal subject-membership of the middle term' to the universality of the major in the first figure and the universality of one of the two premises in the third figure. As for the six moods of the fourth figure, i.e., the first, second, third, fourth, seventh, and eighth, it appears from his saying: 'universal subject-membership of the middle term' the universality of the minor in all of them, and he pointed by his saying: 'with its meeting the minor' to the affirmation of the minor and its actuality in four of these six, which are the first, second, fourth, and seventh. So it appeared from his saying: 'universal subject-membership of the middle term, its meeting the minor, and its predication on the major' all the conditions of the first and third figures and some conditions of the fourth as well. He did not say 'or for the major', an objection whose elucidation is: that the author, in the context of brevity, should not have said 'or its predication on the major' but rather should have said 'or the major' by conjoining it to the minor so that the meaning would be 'or with its meeting the major', and his saying 'because meeting etc.' is its answer, and its elucidation is clear. With (so understand. Isma'il). So it follows: for universal subject-membership of the middle term is realized here because of the universality of the major with its meeting the major, since the major is predicated in the major premise of this syllogism, and its meeting the major is more general than whether the major is predicated on it or vice versa as previously. (Isma'il) And it follows etc.: for universal subject-membership of the middle term with its predication on the major is realized here. As for the first, because of the assumption of universality of one of the two premises. As for the second, because the major is affirmative; so the middle term is united with the major by its predication on it, and meeting includes both subsumption and predication. (Isma'il) And either from universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality it is productive, and that has been obscure to some eminent scholars, so know it. His saying 'and either from universal subject-membership of the major': this is the second of the two matters that we mentioned that a syllogism must necessarily have one of them for productivity. Its summary: universality of a major in which the major term is subject with difference in quality of the two premises, as in all moods of the second figure and as in the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods of the fourth figure. Indeed the third and fourth moods of it combine both matters; therefore we interpreted the first disjunction as prevention of emptiness. So it was pointed to all conditions of the first and third figures in terms of quantity and quality and modality, and to the conditions of the second and fourth figures in terms of quantity and quality. Productive because it includes the conditions known from the rule; for the major of this arranged syllogism, since it was universal affirmative, there occurred from it universality of subject-membership of the middle term meeting the minor in actuality, so it would follow that it includes the conditions mentioned previously, but it is not so; because the affirmation of the minor is not found, while it is not productive. Do you not see that 'some human is not a stone', and 'every stone is a body' is true, but the resulting conclusion, namely 'some human is not a body', is false. And analogize to it his saying: 'and it would also follow that the syllogism arranged etc.' And that has been obscure: i.e., the aspect that the author said 'and its predication on the major' and did not say 'or the major' and its cover was not removed to some eminent scholars because they are outsiders. That 'al-fuhul' (the eminent) is the plural of 'fahl', which is the opposite of female; it is said of a profoundly learned scholar, and what is meant here is al-‘Arif al-Hajji and al-Fadil al-Yaghnowi followed him. All moods of the second figure: for the major term is subject in its major premise, so its universality is obligatory with difference in quality, and also the major term in the major of the fourth figure is subject, so its universality in these moods exists with difference. (Burhan) Indeed it combines etc.: indeed the two matters combine in the third and fourth moods of the fourth figure; for you have known from what preceded that universal subject-membership of the middle term with its predication on the major is realized in the third, and universal subject-membership of the middle term meeting the minor is realized in the fourth, so understand. (Isma'il) Therefore: i.e., because of this combination leading to the conjunction of the two intended matters either first or last. 'It was pointed': i.e., from his saying 'either from universal subject-membership of the middle term' to his saying 'and either from universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality'. And the fourth: and you have already known the conditions of the six moods of the fourth figure previously, so it appears from his saying: 'universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality' the conditions of the remaining two of the eight as well, so its conditions are also completed in terms of quality and quantity.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the rule (ضابطة) governing the conditions for productive syllogisms in the four figures. The commentator begins by stating that all moods of the first and third figures are productive, along with six specific moods of the fourth figure, and instructs the student to memorize this. He then addresses a linguistic point: why the author of the matn said 'and its predication on the major' (وحَمْلِهِ على الأكبر) instead of a shorter 'or the major' (أو الأكبر). The reason, the commentator explains, is that 'meeting' (ملاقاة) includes both subsumption (وضع) and predication (حمل), so using 'or the major' would wrongly imply that merely meeting the major (without specifying predication) is sufficient, which would make certain invalid syllogisms appear productive. For example, a syllogism in the first figure with a universal affirmative major and a negative minor would wrongly seem productive. The commentator then unpacks the conditions. For the first figure, the conditions are: the minor must be affirmative and actual (فعلية), and the major must be universal. These are indicated by the phrases 'the middle term's meeting the minor in actuality' (ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل) and 'universal subject-membership of the middle term' (عموم موضوعية الأوسط). For the third figure, the conditions are similar except the universality can be in either premise. For the fourth figure, the six productive moods require the minor to be universal in all, and additionally the minor must be affirmative and actual in four specific moods (first, second, fourth, seventh). The phrase 'universal subject-membership of the middle term, its meeting the minor, and its predication on the major' thus covers all conditions for the first and third figures and some for the fourth. The commentator then introduces a second set of conditions: syllogisms can also be productive from 'universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality'. This applies to all moods of the second figure and to the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods of the fourth figure. In these cases, the major premise is universal and the major term is its subject (not predicate), and the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The commentator notes that the third and fourth moods of the fourth figure actually combine both sets of conditions, which is why they are productive under both. He further clarifies that the first disjunction (either from the middle term's universality or from the major's universality) is exclusive but not exhaustive; the 'or' is meant to prevent simultaneous absence (منع الخلو), not to imply that both cannot occur together. Finally, the commentator provides a concrete example to illustrate the necessity of the minor's affirmation: 'Some human is not a stone, and every stone is a body' yields a false conclusion, showing that a negative minor with a universal affirmative major does not produce a valid syllogism. He concludes by explaining that the phrase 'universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality' covers the remaining two moods of the fourth figure (the fifth and sixth?), completing the conditions for all eight moods of the fourth figure.

Key Terms
عموم موضوعية الأوسط
ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل
حمل الأوسط على الأكبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first figure of categorical syllogism, which of the following is a necessary condition for productivity according to the rule?
A) The minor premise must be affirmative and actual, and the major premise must be universal.
B) The minor premise must be universal, and the major premise must be affirmative.
C) Both premises must be affirmative and universal.
D) The minor premise must be particular, and the major premise must be universal.

Key Takeaway
For a first-figure syllogism to be productive, the minor must be affirmative and actual, and the major must be universal; for the third figure, the minor is also affirmative and actual, and at least one premise must be universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid first-figure categorical syllogism about a fiqh ruling (e.g., on riba or zakat) that satisfies the conditions for productivity. Identify each condition explicitly.
?
Check whether the following is a valid first-figure syllogism: 'Some animals are not rational; every human is rational; therefore some animals are not rational.' Which condition is violated? Explain using the terminology from the lesson.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Conditions for Productive Syllogisms: Universal Major Subject and Difference in Quality
Commentary on second set of conditions for productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for productive syllogisms in the second figure and the remaining moods of the fourth figure: the major premise must be universal with the major term as subject, and the premises must differ in quality.

Core Idea
When the major term is subject in a universal major premise and the premises differ in quality, the syllogism is productive.

After covering the first set of conditions centered on the middle term, the commentator introduces a second set: universal subject-membership of the major term (عموم موضوعية الأكبر) combined with difference in quality (الاختلاف في الكيف) between the premises. This applies to all moods of the second figure and to four moods of the fourth figure. In this lesson, we will analyze this condition and see how it works with examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second condition from the passage
Find the phrase 'إما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر مع الاختلاف في الكيف' (either from universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality).
2
Understand the condition
The major premise must be universal, and the major term must be the subject of that premise (not the predicate). Additionally, the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative).
This condition is necessary for the second figure and some fourth-figure moods.
3
Apply to the second figure
In all moods of the second figure, the major term is the subject of the major premise, and the two premises always differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Therefore, all second-figure moods satisfy this condition.
4
Apply to the fourth figure
The fourth figure has eight moods, but only moods 3, 4, 5, and 6 satisfy this condition. Moods 3 and 4 actually combine both sets of conditions.
The passage explains that the third and fourth moods of the fourth figure have both conditions.
5
Test a second-figure example
Consider: 'No human is a stone; every marble is a stone; therefore no marble is a human.' Check that the major premise (No human is a stone) is universal with 'human' (major term) as subject, and the premises are negative and affirmative respectively (differ in quality).
Provide an Islamic example: 'No believer is an idol worshiper; every mushrik is an idol worshiper; therefore no mushrik is a believer.'

ضابطة شرائط الأشكال الأربعة جميع ضروب الشكل الأول والثالث وستة ضروب من الشكل الرابع. فاحفظ. واعلم أنه لم يقل: "أو للأكبر" أي أو مع ملاقاته للأكبر مع كونه أخصر؛ لأن الملاقاة تشمل الوضع والحمل كما تقدم، فيلزم كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الأول من كبرى موجبة كلية مع صغرى سالبة منتجا، ويلزم أيضاً كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الثالث من صغرى سالبة وكبرى موجبة مع كلية إحدى مقدمتيه. جميع ضروب الشكل الأول إلخ : فإن شرائط الإنتاج في جميع ضروب الشكل الأول إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها وكلية الكبرى، وكذلك شرائط إنتاج جميع ضروب الشكل الثالث أيضاً ليست إلا إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها وكلية إحدى المقدمتين، فأشار إلى إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها في كلا الشقين بقوله: "ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل" وأشار بقوله: عموم موضوعية الأوسط إلى كلية الكبرى في الشكل الأول وكلية إحدى المقدمتين في الشكل الثالث. وأما الضروب الستة من الشكل الرابع أي الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع والسابع والثامن، فيظهر من قوله: "عموم موضوعية الأوسط" كلية الصغرى في كلها، وأشار بقوله: "مع ملاقاته للأصغر" إلى إيجاب الصغرى وفعليتها في أربعة من هذه الستة، وهي الأول والثاني والرابع والسابع، فظهر من قوله: "عموم موضوعية الأوسط ملاقاته للأصغر وحمله على الأكبر" جميع شرائط الأول والثالث وبعض شرائط الرابع أيضاً. لم يقل أو للأكبر ،اعتراض تقريره: أن المصنف في صدد الاختصار فكان عليه أن لا يقول: "أو حمله على الأكبر" بل أن يقول: "أو الأكبر" بعطفه على الأصغر حتى يكون المعنى: أو مع ملاقاته للأكبر وقوله: "لأن الملاقاة إلخ" جوابه، وتقريره واضح. مع فافهم. إسماعيل) فيلزم فإن عموم موضوعية الأوسط يتحقق ههنا؛ لكون الكبرى كلية مع ملاقاته للأكبر؛ إذ الأكبر محمول في كبرى هذا القياس وملاقاته للأكبر أعم من أن يكون الأكبر محمولا عليه أو بالعكس كما مر . ( إسماعيل) ويلزم إلخ : فإن عموم موضوعية الأوسط مع حمله على الأكبر يتحقق ههنا. أما الأول فلفرض كلية إحدى المقدمتين. وأما الثاني فلكون الكبرى موجبة؛ فإن الأوسط متحد مع الأكبر بحمله عليه، والملاقاة شامل للوضع والحمل. ( إسماعيل) وإما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر مع الاختلاف في الكيف هو منتجا، وقد اشتبه ذلك على بعض الفحول فاعرفه قوله وإما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر: هذا الأمر الثاني من الأمرين اللذين ذكرنا أنه لابد في إنتاج القياس من أحدهما. وحاصله كلية كبرى يكون الأكبر موضوعا فيها مع اختلاف المقدمتين في الكيف، وذلك كما في جميع ضروب الشكل الثاني وكما في الضرب الثالث والرابع والخامس والسادس من الشكل الرابع، فقد اشتمل الضرب الثالث والرابع منه على كلا الأمرين؛ ولذا حملنا الترديد الأول على منع الخلو، فقد أشير إلى جميع شرائط الشكل الأول والثالث كما وكيفا ،وجهةً وإلى شرائط الشكل الثاني والرابع كماً وكيفاً. منتجا لكونه مشتملا على الشروط المعلومة من الضابطة؛ فإن كبرى هذا القياس المرتب لما كانت موجبة كلية فوجد منه عمومية موضوعية الأوسط ملاقاته للأصغر بالفعل، فيلزم أن يكون مشتملا على الشروط المذكورة في ما سبق وليس كذلك؛ لأنه ليس قد وجد إيجاب الصغرى مع أنه ليس بمنتج، ألا ترى أن بعض الإنسان ليس بحجر، وكل حجر جسم صادق والنتيجة الحاصلة أعني بعض الإنسان ليس بجسم كاذب، وقس عليه قوله: "ويلزم أيضاً كون القياس المرتب إلخ. وقد اشتبه ذلك: أي وجه أن المصنف عليه قال: "وحمله على الأكبر" ولم يقل: "أو الأكبر" و لم يكشف غطاؤه على بعض الفحول؛ لكونهم أجانب. أن الفحول: جمع "فحل" هو ضد الأنثى، يقال: للعالم المتبحر، والمراد به العارف الحاجي وتبعه الفاضل الياغنوي. جميع ضروب الشكل الثاني: فإن الأكبر موضوع في كبراه فكليتها واجبة مع الاختلاف في الكيف، وأيضاً الأكبر في كبرى الشكل الرابع موضوع، فكليتها في هذه الضروب موجودة مع الاختلاف.(برهان) فقد اشتمل إلخ فقد اجتمع الأمران في الضرب الثالث والرابع من الشكل الرابع ؛ فإنك قد علمت مما سبق عموم موضوعية الأوسط مع حمله على الأكبر يتحقق في الثالث، وعموم موضوعية الأوسط ملاقاته للأصغر مع يتحقق في الرابع فافهم. إسماعيل) ولذا أي ولهذا الاشتمال المفضي إلى اجتماع الأمرين المرادين أو لا. فقد أشير : أي من قوله: "إما من عموم موضوعية الأوسط إلى قوله: "وإما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر مع الاختلاف في الكيف". والرابع وقد علمت شرائط الضروب الستة من الشكل الرابع سابقا، فيظهر من قوله: "عموم موضوعية الأكبر مع الاختلاف في "الكيف" شرائط الضربين الباقيين من الثمانية أيضاً، فتم شرائطه أيضا بحسب الكيف والكم.
The rule of conditions for the four figures: All moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure. So memorize. And know that he did not say: 'or for the major', meaning 'or with its meeting the major while it is more concise'; because meeting includes both subsumption and predication as previously, so it would follow that the syllogism arranged in the form of the first figure from a universal affirmative major with a negative minor is productive, and it would also follow that the syllogism arranged in the form of the third figure from a negative minor and affirmative major with universality of one of the two premises. All moods of the first figure etc.: For the conditions of productivity in all moods of the first figure are the affirmation of the minor and its actuality and the universality of the major, and likewise the conditions of productivity of all moods of the third figure are only the affirmation of the minor and its actuality and the universality of one of the two premises. So he pointed to the affirmation of the minor and its actuality in both parts by his saying: 'the middle term's meeting the minor in actuality', and he pointed by his saying: 'universal subject-membership of the middle term' to the universality of the major in the first figure and the universality of one of the two premises in the third figure. As for the six moods of the fourth figure, i.e., the first, second, third, fourth, seventh, and eighth, it appears from his saying: 'universal subject-membership of the middle term' the universality of the minor in all of them, and he pointed by his saying: 'with its meeting the minor' to the affirmation of the minor and its actuality in four of these six, which are the first, second, fourth, and seventh. So it appeared from his saying: 'universal subject-membership of the middle term, its meeting the minor, and its predication on the major' all the conditions of the first and third figures and some conditions of the fourth as well. He did not say 'or for the major', an objection whose elucidation is: that the author, in the context of brevity, should not have said 'or its predication on the major' but rather should have said 'or the major' by conjoining it to the minor so that the meaning would be 'or with its meeting the major', and his saying 'because meeting etc.' is its answer, and its elucidation is clear. With (so understand. Isma'il). So it follows: for universal subject-membership of the middle term is realized here because of the universality of the major with its meeting the major, since the major is predicated in the major premise of this syllogism, and its meeting the major is more general than whether the major is predicated on it or vice versa as previously. (Isma'il) And it follows etc.: for universal subject-membership of the middle term with its predication on the major is realized here. As for the first, because of the assumption of universality of one of the two premises. As for the second, because the major is affirmative; so the middle term is united with the major by its predication on it, and meeting includes both subsumption and predication. (Isma'il) And either from universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality it is productive, and that has been obscure to some eminent scholars, so know it. His saying 'and either from universal subject-membership of the major': this is the second of the two matters that we mentioned that a syllogism must necessarily have one of them for productivity. Its summary: universality of a major in which the major term is subject with difference in quality of the two premises, as in all moods of the second figure and as in the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods of the fourth figure. Indeed the third and fourth moods of it combine both matters; therefore we interpreted the first disjunction as prevention of emptiness. So it was pointed to all conditions of the first and third figures in terms of quantity and quality and modality, and to the conditions of the second and fourth figures in terms of quantity and quality. Productive because it includes the conditions known from the rule; for the major of this arranged syllogism, since it was universal affirmative, there occurred from it universality of subject-membership of the middle term meeting the minor in actuality, so it would follow that it includes the conditions mentioned previously, but it is not so; because the affirmation of the minor is not found, while it is not productive. Do you not see that 'some human is not a stone', and 'every stone is a body' is true, but the resulting conclusion, namely 'some human is not a body', is false. And analogize to it his saying: 'and it would also follow that the syllogism arranged etc.' And that has been obscure: i.e., the aspect that the author said 'and its predication on the major' and did not say 'or the major' and its cover was not removed to some eminent scholars because they are outsiders. That 'al-fuhul' (the eminent) is the plural of 'fahl', which is the opposite of female; it is said of a profoundly learned scholar, and what is meant here is al-‘Arif al-Hajji and al-Fadil al-Yaghnowi followed him. All moods of the second figure: for the major term is subject in its major premise, so its universality is obligatory with difference in quality, and also the major term in the major of the fourth figure is subject, so its universality in these moods exists with difference. (Burhan) Indeed it combines etc.: indeed the two matters combine in the third and fourth moods of the fourth figure; for you have known from what preceded that universal subject-membership of the middle term with its predication on the major is realized in the third, and universal subject-membership of the middle term meeting the minor is realized in the fourth, so understand. (Isma'il) Therefore: i.e., because of this combination leading to the conjunction of the two intended matters either first or last. 'It was pointed': i.e., from his saying 'either from universal subject-membership of the middle term' to his saying 'and either from universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality'. And the fourth: and you have already known the conditions of the six moods of the fourth figure previously, so it appears from his saying: 'universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality' the conditions of the remaining two of the eight as well, so its conditions are also completed in terms of quality and quantity.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the rule (ضابطة) governing the conditions for productive syllogisms in the four figures. The commentator begins by stating that all moods of the first and third figures are productive, along with six specific moods of the fourth figure, and instructs the student to memorize this. He then addresses a linguistic point: why the author of the matn said 'and its predication on the major' (وحَمْلِهِ على الأكبر) instead of a shorter 'or the major' (أو الأكبر). The reason, the commentator explains, is that 'meeting' (ملاقاة) includes both subsumption (وضع) and predication (حمل), so using 'or the major' would wrongly imply that merely meeting the major (without specifying predication) is sufficient, which would make certain invalid syllogisms appear productive. For example, a syllogism in the first figure with a universal affirmative major and a negative minor would wrongly seem productive. The commentator then unpacks the conditions. For the first figure, the conditions are: the minor must be affirmative and actual (فعلية), and the major must be universal. These are indicated by the phrases 'the middle term's meeting the minor in actuality' (ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل) and 'universal subject-membership of the middle term' (عموم موضوعية الأوسط). For the third figure, the conditions are similar except the universality can be in either premise. For the fourth figure, the six productive moods require the minor to be universal in all, and additionally the minor must be affirmative and actual in four specific moods (first, second, fourth, seventh). The phrase 'universal subject-membership of the middle term, its meeting the minor, and its predication on the major' thus covers all conditions for the first and third figures and some for the fourth. The commentator then introduces a second set of conditions: syllogisms can also be productive from 'universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality'. This applies to all moods of the second figure and to the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods of the fourth figure. In these cases, the major premise is universal and the major term is its subject (not predicate), and the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The commentator notes that the third and fourth moods of the fourth figure actually combine both sets of conditions, which is why they are productive under both. He further clarifies that the first disjunction (either from the middle term's universality or from the major's universality) is exclusive but not exhaustive; the 'or' is meant to prevent simultaneous absence (منع الخلو), not to imply that both cannot occur together. Finally, the commentator provides a concrete example to illustrate the necessity of the minor's affirmation: 'Some human is not a stone, and every stone is a body' yields a false conclusion, showing that a negative minor with a universal affirmative major does not produce a valid syllogism. He concludes by explaining that the phrase 'universal subject-membership of the major with difference in quality' covers the remaining two moods of the fourth figure (the fifth and sixth?), completing the conditions for all eight moods of the fourth figure.

Key Terms
عموم موضوعية الأكبر
الاختلاف في الكيف
ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second figure of categorical syllogism, which of the following is a necessary condition for productivity according to the rule?
A) The major premise must be universal with the major term as subject, and the two premises must differ in quality.
B) The minor premise must be affirmative and actual, and the major premise must be universal.
C) The middle term must be subject in both premises.
D) The conclusion must be universal.

Key Takeaway
For the second figure and some fourth-figure moods, a productive syllogism requires the major premise to be universal with the major term as subject, and the premises must differ in quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid second-figure categorical syllogism using an Islamic example (e.g., from fiqh or kalam). Identify the major term, minor term, middle term, and show that the condition of universal major subject and difference in quality is satisfied.
?
The following syllogism is supposed to be in the second figure: 'Every human is rational; every animal is rational; therefore every human is animal.' Why is this invalid? Which condition(s) does it violate?

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Second Condition: Universal Major Subject with Difference in Quality
Commentator explains the second condition for productive syllogism and applies it to second and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a syllogism satisfies the second condition (universal major subject with difference in quality) and evaluate its validity based on this condition.

Core Idea
A productive syllogism needs either a universal middle subject or a universal major subject with premises differing in quality.

In this lesson, we explore the second condition for a productive categorical syllogism as explained by the commentator. While the first condition focused on the middle term, this one focuses on the major term: the major premise must be universal and have the major term as its subject, and the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This condition is essential for the second figure and some moods of the fourth figure. The commentator provides a counterexample to illustrate a common mistake.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise and its term position
Locate the premise that contains the predicate of the conclusion (the major term). Check whether the major term is the subject of that premise.
In the counterexample 'Every stone is a body', the major term 'body' is predicate, not subject.
2
Check universality of the major premise
Verify that the major premise is universal (either A: all, or E: no). It cannot be particular (I or O).
3
Check difference in quality between premises
Ensure one premise is affirmative and the other negative. Both affirmative or both negative fails this condition.
In the counterexample, the premises are A (affirmative) and O (negative) – they do differ in quality, but the major term is not subject, so condition fails.
4
Apply to the given syllogism
Test the counterexample: 'Some human is not a stone' (O), 'Every stone is a body' (A), therefore 'Some human is not a body' (O). Despite difference in quality, the major term 'body' is not subject, so the conclusion is false.
This shows the condition is necessary for validity.
5
Recognize the figures that satisfy this condition
All moods of the second figure and moods 3-6 of the fourth figure have the major term as subject in a universal premise and premises differing in quality.

And the second is from the universality of the subject of the major with difference in quality, it is productive, and that has been confused for some of the great scholars, so know it. His statement: 'And the second is from the universality of the subject of the major': This is the second of the two matters which we mentioned that one of them is necessary in the production of the syllogism. Commentary
This passage is an expansion by the commentator on the second condition required for a productive syllogism. The author (of the matn) had stated that one of two conditions is necessary: either (1) universality of the subject of the middle term, or (2) universality of the subject of the major term with difference in quality between the premises. The commentator clarifies that this second condition is that the major premise must be universal and have the major term as subject, while the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This applies to all moods of the second figure and to moods 3-6 of the fourth figure. The commentator warns that some great scholars have been confused about this point, thinking that a syllogism with a universal affirmative major and a particular affirmative minor would be productive, but he gives a counterexample: 'Some human is not a stone' (true) and 'Every stone is a body' (true) but the conclusion 'Some human is not a body' is false. This demonstrates that the condition is not sufficient without the difference in quality. He then explains that in the third and fourth moods of the fourth figure, both conditions are present. The commentator also notes that the author's phrasing ('with predication upon the major' instead of 'or the major') caused confusion, but he justifies it. He concludes that from the stated condition, the conditions for the remaining two moods of the fourth figure (out of eight) become clear, completing the quantitative and qualitative conditions for that figure.

Third Condition for Second Figure: Incompatibility of Modalities
Commentator explains the third condition for productive syllogism in the second figure regarding modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the incompatibility-of-modalities condition is met for a second-figure syllogism and test whether a modal pair satisfies it.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the modalities of the two premises must be incompatible— they cannot both be true simultaneously.

The second figure of categorical syllogism requires not only the universality of the major subject with a difference in quality, but also a third condition concerning modality. The commentator explains that when the middle term is predicated in both premises (as in the second figure), the relation of the middle to the major in the major premise and the relation of the middle to the minor in the minor premise must be modalized in such a way that the two relations cannot both be true. This condition ensures that the syllogism is productive despite the difference in quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being explained
In the passage, the commentator introduces the condition of 'incompatibility' (munafah) as a third requirement for the second figure. Read the Arabic and translation to grasp that it concerns the modalities of the two relations involving the middle term.
Focus on the phrase 'مع منافاة' (with incompatibility).
2
Understand the restriction to the second figure
The condition applies only when the middle term is predicated in both premises (as in the second figure). The commentator explicitly excludes the four moods of the fourth figure that also have the second condition. Note how the phrase 'إذا كان الأوسط منسوبا ومحمولا' restricts the scope.
The middle is predicated in both premises in the second figure, making it a 'description' (wasf).
3
Grasp the equivalence to earlier conditions
The commentator states that this incompatibility condition is mutually necessary and sufficient with the two modality conditions mentioned earlier for the second figure. This means that meeting one implies meeting the others. Trace the logical 'lazim' (necessary consequence) relationship.
This is a meta-logical insight connecting different formulations.
4
Work through the example
The commentator gives the example: 'Zayd is always standing' and 'Zayd is not actually standing'. These two propositions differ in quality (affirmative/negative) and have incompatible modalities (perpetuity vs. actuality). Verify that they cannot both be true together, fulfilling the incompatibility condition.
The example illustrates how the condition works with a concrete subject (Zayd) and a real predicate (standing).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقيت شرائط الشكل الثاني بحسب الجهة، فأشار إليها بقوله : مع منافاة إلخ. قوله : مع منافاة إلخ : أن القياس المنتج المشتمل على الأمر الثاني أعني عموم موضوعية الأكبر كبر مع الاختلاف في الكيف إذا كان الأوسط منسوبا ومحمولا في كلتا مقدمتيه كما في الشكل الثاني، فحينئذ لابد في إنتاجه من شرط ثالث وهو منافاة نسبة وصف الأوسط المحمول إلى وصف الأكبر الموضوع في الكبرى لنسبة وصف الأوسط المحمول كذلك إلى ذات الأصغر الموضوع في الصغرى، يعني لابد أن تكون النسبتان المذكورتان مكيفتين بكيفيتين بحيث يمتنع اجتماع هاتين النسبتين في الصدق إذا كان الأوسط منسوبا إلخ إنما قيد بهذا؛ لئلا يتوهم اشتراط المنافاة المذكورة في الضروب الأربعة من الرابع الداخلة تحت قوله: "إما من عموم موضوعية الأكبر مع الاختلاف إلخ". إسماعيل) وهو منافاة إلخ: اعلم أن بين هذه المنافاة وبين ما ذكره من الشرطين بحسب الجهة في الشكل الثاني لازم، فعند تحقق هذه الملاقاة يتحقق الشرطان وبالعكس، فثبت الإشارة إلى ذينك الشرطين بقوله : مع منافاة إلخ. وصف الأوسط إلخ ولما كان المحمول عبارة عن الوصف والموضوع عن الذات قال: "وصف الأوسط"؛ لأنه محمول في كلتا المقدمتين في الشكل الثاني، وأيضاً قال: "وصف الأكبر"؛ لأنه محمول في الحقيقة وإن وقع موضوعا في كبرى هذا الشكل ولم يقل: وصف الأصغر بل قال: ذات الأصغر؛ لأنه موضوع في الحال والمآل أيضاً. النسبتان إلخ أي النسبة في الصغرى والنسبة في الكبرى المختلفتان في الإيجاب والسلب مكيفتين بكيفيتين أي بجهتين بحيث إلخ. مكيفتين: أي موجهتين بجهتين. مكيفتين بكيفيتين: كالدوام والفعل مثلا يعني كان نسبة الأوسط إلى وصف الأكبر مكيفة بدوام الإيجاب مثلا، ونسبة الأوسط إلى ذات الأصغر مكيفة بفعلية السلب، فالدوام أو الفعلية متنافيان لو وجدا في القضيتين المختلفتين بالإيجاب والسلب بعد اتحاد طرفيها نحو زيد قائم دائما، وزيد ليس بقائم بالفعل.
There remain the conditions of the second figure with respect to modality, so he indicated to them by his statement: 'with incompatibility etc.' His statement: 'with incompatibility etc.': That the productive syllogism which includes the second matter, namely the universality of the subject of the major in the major premise with difference in quality, when the middle term is related and predicated in both of its premises as in the second figure, then there is necessary for its production a third condition, namely the incompatibility of the relation of the description of the middle (which is predicated) to the description of the major (which is subject) in the major premise, with the relation of the description of the middle (similarly predicated) to the essence of the minor (which is subject) in the minor premise. That is, it is necessary that the two mentioned relations be qualified with two modalities so that the combination of these two relations in truth is impossible. 'When the middle term is related etc.': he only restricted with this in order that it not be imagined that the mentioned incompatibility is required in the four moods of the fourth that fall under his statement: 'Either from the universality of the subject of the major with difference etc.' (Ismail) 'And it is incompatibility etc.': Know that between this incompatibility and what he mentioned of the two conditions according to modality in the second figure there is a necessary relation; so when this meeting is realized, the two conditions are realized, and vice versa. So it is established that the indication to those two conditions is by his statement: 'with incompatibility etc.' 'The description of the middle etc.': Since the predicate is an expression for the description and the subject for the essence, he said 'description of the middle' because it is predicated in both premises in the second figure, and also he said 'description of the major' because it is predicate in reality although it occurs as subject in the major premise of this figure, and he did not say 'description of the minor' but said 'essence of the minor' because it is subject in the present state and the final result as well. 'The two relations etc.': That is, the relation in the minor premise and the relation in the major premise, which differ in affirmation and negation, qualified with two modalities, i.e., with two modes, such that etc. 'Qualified': that is, modalized with two modes. 'Qualified with two modalities': like perpetuity and actuality, for example. That is, the relation of the middle to the description of the major is modalized by perpetual affirmation, for example, and the relation of the middle to the essence of the minor is modalized by actual negation. So perpetuity and actuality are incompatible if they occur in two propositions differing in affirmation and negation after the unification of their terms, like: 'Zayd is always standing' and 'Zayd is not actually standing'.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's explanation of a third condition for the second figure when the second condition (universal major with difference in quality) is present. The condition is that the modes (modalities) of the two relations must be incompatible. Specifically, if the middle term is predicated in both premises, then the relation of the middle to the major (in the major premise) and the relation of the middle to the minor (in the minor premise) must be modalized so that they cannot both be true simultaneously. The commentator restricts this condition to cases where the middle is predicated in both premises, which is true only in the second figure (and not in the fourth figure moods that also have the second condition). He notes that this incompatibility is equivalent to the two modality conditions mentioned earlier for the second figure; they are mutually necessary and sufficient. He then clarifies why the author used 'description of the middle' and 'description of the major' but 'essence of the minor': because in the second figure, the middle is predicate in both premises and thus a description (attribute), the major is predicate in reality (though subject in the major premise), and the minor is always subject. Finally, he gives an example: if the middle's relation to the major is perpetually affirmative, then the relation of the middle to the minor must be actually negative, because perpetual affirmation and actual negation cannot both be true of the same subject. The example given is 'Zayd is always standing' and 'Zayd is not actually standing' – these cannot both be true, fulfilling the condition of incompatibility.

منافاة
جهة
وصف الأوسط
ذات الأصغر

C) Every intoxicant is forbidden; some drink is not an intoxicant.

The third condition for the second figure requires that the modalities of the middle's relation to the major and to the minor be incompatible—they cannot both be true together.

The Syllogism
