By necessity of negation: i.e., negating the middle term from the description of the major is apparent, for the judgment in the conditional (proposition) is by necessity of the predicate's relation to the subject's essence as long as the description exists, such as 'Every celestial sphere is mobile by possibility' and 'Nothing of the writer's finger is stationary as long as it is writing' — there is contradiction between the possibility of affirmation and the necessity of negation, (a proof exists) it is apparent. (Isma'il) It is apparent because necessity in the conditional is according to the description, so there is an apparent relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major. But in the necessary major, it is not apparent because necessity in it is according to essence, and we must establish a contradiction between the relation of the description of the middle to the essence of the minor by possibility of affirmation and the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rule of conditions for the four figures of the categorical syllogism. The author explains that for a valid syllogism (especially in the first figure), there must be a contradiction (munāfāt) between the two premises when considering their modal qualities—namely, necessity (ḍarūra), conditionality (mashrūṭa), and possibility (mumkina). The discussion focuses on the concept of 'necessity of negation' (ḍarūrat al-salab) regarding the middle term. The author argues that if the major premise is conditional (e.g., 'Every A is B as long as it is C'), the relation of the middle term's description to the major's description is necessary as long as the description holds. This is contrasted with a necessary major, where necessity pertains to the essence (dhāt) regardless of description. The commentary then demonstrates how contradiction arises when one premise is necessary and the other is possible, using examples like 'Every human is an animal by necessity' and 'Nothing of stone is an animal by absolute possibility.' The author also treats cases where the minor lacks perpetuity (dawām) or the major's negative does not convert, leading to no contradiction. The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
When Syllogism Conditions Fail: No Contradiction Case Study
Author explains conditions for valid syllogisms and shows that when either condition fails, no contradiction arises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify that when the minor premise lacks perpetuity and the major's negative lacks conversion, no contradiction occurs between the premises, and identify the most specific proposition types involved.

Core Idea
Absence of the two conditions does not invalidate a syllogism if no contradiction arises.

In syllogistic logic, two conditions are often required: the minor must be perpetual and the major's negative must convert. But what happens when these conditions are absent? The commentator al-Yazdi shows that even when they are absent, the syllogism may still be valid because the premises may not contradict each other. In this lesson, we will examine his analysis step by step, using examples from celestial phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two conditions for syllogistic validity
The first condition is that the minor premise must be perpetual (دائم), i.e., true for all times. The second is that the major premise's negative must convert (تنعكس سالبتها), meaning if the major is negative, its converse must also be true. Note these conditions.
These conditions are discussed in earlier sections of logic.
2
Identify the case: both conditions absent
Consider a syllogism where the minor is not perpetual and the major's negative does not convert. Al-Yazdi examines this scenario. The minor then falls under the 'conditional particular' (المشروطة الخاصة) – a proposition true as long as the description holds but not perpetually. The major falls under 'temporal' (الوقتية) – a proposition true only at a specific time.
Other possibilities like 'perpetual' or 'conventional' are excluded.
3
Examine the example provided
Al-Yazdi gives the following: Minor: 'No eclipsed thing is a moon necessarily as long as it is eclipsed, not perpetually.' Major: 'Every moon is luminous necessarily at the time of quadrature.' Note that the minor is conditional particular (depends on being eclipsed) and the major is temporal (only at quadrature).
The terms 'eclipsed thing' and 'moon' are linked by the middle term 'moon'.
4
Check for contradiction between the premises
Ask: do these two premises contradict? The minor says an eclipsed thing is not a moon (necessarily during eclipse). The major says every moon is luminous at quadrature. There is no contradiction because the time of eclipse (when the moon is not luminous) is different from the time of quadrature (when it is luminous). The contradiction is avoided.
Al-Yazdi states: 'لعل ذلك الوقت غير أوقات الوصف العنواني' – perhaps that time is not the times of the notional description.
5
Generalize the principle
If contradiction is removed between the most specific types (conditional particular and temporal), it is also removed between more general types. Al-Yazdi uses an analogy: if there is no contradiction between 'human' and 'writer', then there is no contradiction between 'animal' and 'walking'. This shows that the absence of the conditions does not automatically produce a contradiction.
The argument relies on the logical principle that if the specific are compatible, the general are also compatible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضابطة شرائط الأشكال الأربعة

وإما إنها دائرة مع الشرطين ،عدما أي كلما انتفى أحد الشرطين المذكورين لم يتحقق المنافاة المذكورة؛ فلأنه إذا لم تكن الصغرى مما يصدق عليه الدوام ولا الكبرى مما تنعكس سالبتها لم يكن في الصغريات أخص من المشروطة الخاصة ولا في الكبريات أخص من الوقتية، ولا منافاة بين ضرورة الإيجاب مثلا بحسب الوصف لا دائما وبين ضرورة السلب في وقت معين لا دائما؛ إذ لعل ذلك الوقت غير أوقات الوصف العنواني وإذا ارتفعت المنافاة بين الأخصين ارتفعت بين ما هو أعم منهما ضرورة، وكذا إذا لم تكن الكبرى ضرورية ولا مشروطة حين كون الصغرى ممكنة كان أخص الكبريات الدائمة والعرفية الخاصة والوقتية، ولا منافاة بين إمكان الإيجاب ودوام السلب

من المنخسف ولا في الكبريات فإن الكبرى تكون من القضايا التسع التي لا تنعكس سوالبها والأخص من كل منها هي الوقتية. إسماعيل) إذ لعل ذلك الوقت إلخ مثل: لا شيء من المنخسف بقمر بالضرورة ما دام منخسفا لا دائما، وكل قمر مضيء بالضرورة وقت التربيع، ولا منافاة بين النسبتين عند اتحاد الطرفين كما إذا قيل: لا شيء مضيء بالضرورة ما دام منخسفا لا دائما، وكل منخسف مضيء بالضرورة وقت التربيع؛ لأن وقت سلب الضوء عن ذات المنخسف وقت الانخساف الذي هو وصف عنواني، ووقت الضوء لذات المنخسف وقت التربيع غير وقت الانخساف وإذا ارتفعت إلخ نظيره إذا ارتفعت المنافاة بين الإنسان والكاتب فقد ارتفعت بين الحيوان والماشي أيضاً، فالحاصل أنه إذا ارتفعت المنافاة بين المشروطة الخاصة والعامة فارتفعت بين غيرهما أيضاً بالضرورة. (برهان ضرورة : فإن تحقق المنافاة بين الأعمين يستلزم تحققها بين الأخصين؛ إذ وجود الأعم عين وجود الأخص كما لا يخفى. إسماعيل) كان أخص الكبريات إلخ إشارة إلى انعدام الشرط الثاني للإنتاج وهو الممكنة إلخ؛ لأن الكبريات إما منعكسة السوالب أو لا، فعلى الأول لما صرحوا عدم الضرورية والمشروطة فلم يبق في الدائمتين إلا الدائمة وفي الوصفيتين إلا العرفيتان والعرفية الخاصة أخص منهما، وعلى الثاني ليس الأخص في تلك التسع إلا الوقتية. (برهان) ولا منافاة بين إمكان الإيجاب إلخ مثل كل ماش ساكن بالإمكان العام، ولا شيء من الفلك بساكن دائما، فالمنافاة منعدمة. (برهان)

ما دام الذات ولا بينه وبين دوام السلب بحسب الوصف لا دائما، ولا بينه وبين ضرورة السلب في وقت معين لا دائما، وكذا إذا لم تكن الصغرى ضرورية على تقدير كون الكبرى ممكنة كان أخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة أو الدائمة ولا منافاة بين إمكان الإيجاب وبين ضرورة السلب بحسب الوصف لادائما ولا بينه وبين دوام السلب ما دام الذات قطعا. وتحقيق هذا البحث

ولا بينه وبين دوام السلب أي بين إمكان الإيجاب وبين دوام السلب بحسب الوصف لا دائما، وهذا الدوام معنى العرفية الخاصة السالبة، مثل: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان وبالدوام لاشيء من الإنسان بكاتب ما دام إنسانا لادائما بين دوام السلب مثل كل كاتب ساكن الأصابع بالإمكان وبالدوام لا شيء من الراقم بساكن ما دام راقما لا دائما. (برهان ولا بينه أي بين إمكان الإيجاب وبين ضرورة السلب في وقت معين لادائما، وهذه الضرورة معنى الوقتية مثل كل قمر منخسف بالإمكان العام، وبالضرورة لا شيء من مضيء وقت التربيع لعدم المنافاة بين كل قمر منخسف بالإمكان العام وبالضرورة لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لادائما لتغاير الوقتين أي وقت الحيلولة ووقت التربيع. في وقت معين إشارة إلى كون الكبرى وقتية. المنخسف وكذا إذا لم تكن الصغرى أي كذا لم يوجد المنافاة عند عدم الشرط الثاني بأن لم تكن الصغرى ضرورية على تقدير كون الكبرى ممكنة؛ فإنه على هذا التقدير كان الواجب أن يكون الصغرى ضرورية فينتفي الشرط الثاني، وبانتفائه ينتفي المنافاة أيضاً؛ فإنه لا منافاة بين النسبتين المذكورتين في لا شيء من الساكن بكاتب بالدوام أو بالضرورة ما دام ساكنا لادائما، وكل ساكن كاتب بالإمكان؛ إذ لا منافاة بين إمكان إيجاب الكتابة للساكن بحسب الذات وبين ضرورته سلب الكتابة عن ذات الساكن بحسب الوصف أو دوامه ما دام الوصف. وبين ضرورة السلب إشارة إلى المشروطة الخاصة. ولا بينه وبين دوام السلب أي بين إمكان الإيجاب وبين دوام السلب بحسب الوصف لا دائما، وهذا الدوام معنى العرفية الخاصة السالبة، مثل كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان ولا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب ما دام إنسانا لا دائما. وتحقيق هذا البحث يعني شرح الضابطة على ما هو الحق في نفس الأمر وهو مبتدأ وقوله: "مما تفردت به" خبره. وحاصل الكلام أن في تحقيقها ما لم يشاركني أحد فيه وهذا التحقيق العجيب الوجيه الجميل أدق التحقيقات. ولعمري أن الشارح اليزدي أصدق قائل في هذا المقال، غفر له الله الكبير المتعال.
The rule of the conditions of the four figures. And [the statement] 'either it is restricted with the two conditions' i.e. whenever one of the two mentioned conditions is absent, the mentioned contradiction does not occur; because if the minor is not such that perpetuity applies to it, and the major is not such that its negative converts, then there is nothing in the minors more specific than the conditional particular, nor in the majors more specific than the temporal, and there is no contradiction between, for example, a necessity of affirmation according to description not perpetually and a necessity of negation at a specific time not perpetually; for perhaps that time is not the times of the notional description, and if the contradiction is removed between the two most specific [propositions], it is necessarily removed between what is more general than them. Similarly, if the major is neither necessary nor conditional while the minor is possible, then the most specific of the majors are the perpetual, the specific conventional, and the temporal, and there is no contradiction between the possibility of affirmation and the perpetuity of negation. From 'al-munkhasif' and in the majors, for the major is among the nine propositions whose negatives do not convert, and the most specific of each of them is the temporal. Ismail: 'for perhaps that time etc.' like: No eclipsed thing is a moon necessarily as long as it is eclipsed, not perpetually, and every moon is luminous necessarily at the time of quadrature, and there is no contradiction between the two relations when the terms are identical, as when it is said: Nothing luminous is [a moon] necessarily as long as it is eclipsed, not perpetually, and every eclipsed thing is luminous necessarily at the time of quadrature; because the time of negating light from the essence of the eclipsed is the time of eclipse which is a notional description, and the time of light for the essence of the eclipsed, the time of quadrature, is different from the time of eclipse. And 'if it is removed etc.' its analogue is: if the contradiction between 'human' and 'writer' is removed, then it is removed between 'animal' and 'walking' also. So the upshot is that if the contradiction between the conditional particular and the general is removed, it is necessarily removed between others as well. (Proof of necessity: for the verification of contradiction between the two more general [propositions] entails its verification between the two more specific; since the existence of the more general is identical to the existence of the more specific, as is not hidden. Ismail.) 'was the most specific of the majors etc.' is an indication of the absence of the second condition for production, i.e. the possible etc.; because the majors are either such that their negatives convert or not. In the first case, since they declared the absence of the necessary and the conditional, nothing remains among the perpetuals except the perpetual, and among the descriptives except the two conventionals, and the specific conventional is more specific than them. In the second case, the most specific among those nine is only the temporal. (Proof) And there is no contradiction between the possibility of affirmation etc. like: Every walker is stationary with general possibility, and nothing of the celestial sphere is stationary perpetually, so the contradiction is absent. (Proof) As long as the essence exists, and between it and the perpetuity of negation according to description not perpetually, and between it and the necessity of negation at a specific time not perpetually. Similarly, if the minor is not necessary on the assumption that the major is possible, then the most specific of the minors is the conditional particular or the perpetual, and there is no contradiction between the possibility of affirmation and the necessity of negation according to description not perpetually, nor between it and the perpetuity of negation as long as the essence exists, absolutely. And the verification of this research. And 'nor between it and the perpetuity of negation' i.e. between the possibility of affirmation and the perpetuity of negation according to description not perpetually, and this perpetuity is the meaning of the specific conventional negative, like: Every human is a writer with possibility, and perpetually nothing of human is a writer as long as human, not perpetually; or the perpetuity of negation like: Every writer has stationary fingers with possibility, and perpetually nothing of a scribe is stationary as long as scribe, not perpetually. (Proof) 'Nor between it' i.e. between the possibility of affirmation and the necessity of negation at a specific time not perpetually, and this necessity is the meaning of the temporal, like: Every moon is eclipsed with general possibility, and necessarily nothing of a luminous thing [is luminous] at the time of quadrature, because there is no contradiction between every moon is eclipsed with general possibility and necessarily nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature not perpetually, due to the difference of the two times, i.e. the time of opposition and the time of quadrature. 'at a specific time' indicates that the major is temporal. 'al-munkhasif' Similarly, if the minor is not necessary i.e. similarly the contradiction does not exist when the second condition is absent, i.e. when the minor is not necessary on the assumption that the major is possible; for on this assumption, the minor should have been necessary, so the second condition is negated, and with its negation the contradiction is also negated; for there is no contradiction between the two mentioned relations in 'Nothing stationary is a writer perpetually or necessarily as long as stationary, not perpetually, and every stationary is a writer with possibility'; for there is no contradiction between the possibility of affirming writing for the stationary according to essence and its necessity of negating writing from the essence of the stationary according to description or its perpetuity as long as the description. 'And between the necessity of negation' indicates the conditional particular. 'Nor between it and the perpetuity of negation' i.e. between the possibility of affirmation and the perpetuity of negation according to description not perpetually, and this perpetuity is the meaning of the specific conventional negative, like: Every human is a writer with possibility, and nothing of human is a writer as long as human, not perpetually. And the verification of this research means the explanation of the rule according to what is the truth in itself, and it is the subject, and his saying: 'what I alone have' is its predicate. And the sum of the speech is that in its verification there is what none has shared with me, and this wonderful, elegant, beautiful verification is the most precise of verifications. By my life, the commentator al-Yazdi is the most truthful speaker in this statement; may Allah the Great and Exalted forgive him.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the 'rule of the conditions for the four figures' of the categorical syllogism. The commentator, likely al-Yazdi, is explaining a subtle point about when the two conditions for a valid syllogism are absent and yet no contradiction arises between the premises, which would otherwise be expected. The two conditions are: (1) the minor premise must be such that perpetuity (الدوام) applies to it (i.e., it is a perpetual proposition), and (2) the major premise must be such that its negative converts (تنعكس سالبتها). The commentator goes through each case. First, if the minor is not perpetual and the major's negative does not convert, then the most specific type of minor is the 'conditional particular' (المشروطة الخاصة) and the most specific type of major is the 'temporal' (الوقتية). He gives an example using the moon's eclipse: 'No eclipsed thing is a moon necessarily as long as it is eclipsed, not perpetually' and 'Every moon is luminous necessarily at the time of quadrature.' There is no contradiction because the time of eclipse (when the moon is not luminous) and the time of quadrature (when it is luminous) are different. He then extends the argument: if contradiction is removed between the most specific types, it is also removed between more general types. He also discusses the second condition: when the major is neither necessary nor conditional while the minor is possible, the most specific majors are the perpetual, the specific conventional, and the temporal, and again no contradiction arises between possible affirmation and perpetual negation. Examples include 'Every walker is stationary with possibility' and 'Nothing of the celestial sphere is stationary perpetually.' The passage also includes an aside where the commentator praises his own unique contribution to this analysis, stating that no one has shared this verification with him.

مشروطة خاصة
وقتية
عرفية خاصة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the minor premise is not perpetual and the major premise's negative is not convertible, what are the most specific types of propositions for the minor and major respectively?
A) Perpetual (dā'ima) and temporal (waqtiyya)
B) Conditional particular (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) and temporal (waqtiyya)
C) Specific conventional ('urfiyya khāṣṣa) and perpetual (dā'ima)
D) Perpetual (dā'ima) and specific conventional ('urfiyya khāṣṣa)

Key Takeaway
When the two conditions (perpetuity of minor and convertibility of major's negative) are absent, the syllogism may still be valid if the premises are not contradictory, as shown by al-Yazdi's analysis of conditional particular and temporal propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the two premises: 'No human is a writer necessarily as long as human, not perpetually' (conditional particular) and 'Every human is a writer with possibility' (possible), check whether they contradict. Explain using al-Yazdi's reasoning that no contradiction exists because the times and conditions differ.
?
Construct an example from fiqh: choose a subject and predicate such that the minor is a conditional particular and the major is temporal, and demonstrate that no contradiction arises, thus the syllogism may be valid despite lacking the conditions.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Modal Conditions for Syllogistic Figures: 'Urfiya Khassa and Waqtia
Author verifies conditions for modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that prevent incompatibility (munafat) between possibility of affirmation and types of negation in modal syllogisms, and apply them to examples from kalam.

Core Idea
Modal propositions can coexist without contradiction when distinguished by essence, description, or time.

This passage from the sharh of Tahzib al-Mantiq delves into the subtle conditions governing validity in modal syllogisms. The author, building on al-Yazdi's work, explains when there is no incompatibility between the possibility of affirmation and various forms of negation (permanent or necessary). These conditions are crucial for constructing sound proofs in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where modal nuances often determine whether an argument holds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal propositions involved
Locate the two propositions being compared: one affirmative possible (e.g., 'every human is a writer potentially') and one negative with a modal qualifier (e.g., 'no human is a writer as long as he is human'). Look for 'bi-al-imkan' alongside 'dawam al-salb' or 'darurat al-salb'.
The modal qualifier may be 'permanence' or 'necessity' tied to a temporal condition.
2
Distinguish between essence and description
In the example, the possibility of writing pertains to the essence of human, while the permanence of negation is conditioned on the description 'as long as he is human'. This ascription to different aspects removes any contradiction.
The phrase 'according to description' (bi-hasab al-wasf) signals this distinction.
3
Recognize temporal distinction in waqtia
In the moon eclipse example, the possibility of eclipse is general (common possibility), while the necessary negation is at a specific time (quadrature). The times differ (opposition vs. quadrature), so no incompatibility arises.
The term 'waqt mu'ayyan' (specific time) is key.
4
Test the second condition: minor not necessary
When the minor premise is not necessary on the assumption that the major is possible, the second condition is absent and no incompatibility occurs. Example: 'nothing of the stationary is a writer permanently as long as stationary' and 'every stationary is a writer potentially' are compatible.
This scenario shows that possibility and necessity can coexist when considered under different respects.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider a kalam proof: 'The world is temporally originated (possible in essence). Nothing temporally originated is without a cause as long as it is originated (permanent negation according to description).' Check if there is incompatibility: the possibility of existence and the necessity of dependence do not conflict because they are based on different aspects (essence vs. description).
This demonstrates the practical use of these modal conditions in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضابطة شرائط الأشكال الأربعة
ما دام الذات ولا بينه وبين دوام السلب بحسب الوصف لا دائما، ولا بينه وبين ضرورة السلب في وقت معين لا دائما، وكذا إذا لم تكن الصغرى ضرورية على تقدير كون الكبرى ممكنة كان أخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة أو الدائمة ولا منافاة بين إمكان الإيجاب وبين ضرورة السلب بحسب الوصف لادائما ولا بينه وبين دوام السلب ما دام الذات قطعا. وتحقيق هذا البحث ولا بينه وبين دوام السلب أي بين إمكان الإيجاب وبين دوام السلب بحسب الوصف لا دائما، وهذا الدوام معنى العرفية الخاصة السالبة، مثل: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان وبالدوام لاشيء من الإنسان بكاتب ما دام إنسانا لادائما بين دوام السلب مثل كل كاتب ساكن الأصابع بالإمكان وبالدوام لا شيء من الراقم بساكن ما دام راقما لا دائما. (برهان ولا بينه أي بين إمكان الإيجاب وبين ضرورة السلب في وقت معين لادائما، وهذه الضرورة معنى الوقتية مثل كل قمر منخسف بالإمكان العام، وبالضرورة لا شيء من مضيء وقت التربيع لعدم المنافاة بين كل قمر منخسف بالإمكان العام وبالضرورة لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لادائما لتغاير الوقتين أي وقت الحيلولة ووقت التربيع.
في وقت معين إشارة إلى كون الكبرى وقتية.
المنخسف
وكذا إذا لم تكن الصغرى أي كذا لم يوجد المنافاة عند عدم الشرط الثاني بأن لم تكن الصغرى ضرورية على تقدير كون الكبرى ممكنة؛ فإنه على هذا التقدير كان الواجب أن تكون الصغرى ضرورية فينتفي الشرط الثاني، وبانتفائه ينتفي المنافاة أيضاً؛ فإنه لا منافاة بين النسبتين المذكورتين في لا شيء من الساكن بكاتب بالدوام أو بالضرورة ما دام ساكنا لادائما، وكل ساكن كاتب بالإمكان؛ إذ لا منافاة بين إمكان إيجاب الكتابة للساكن بحسب الذات وبين ضرورته سلب الكتابة عن ذات الساكن بحسب الوصف أو دوامه ما دام الوصف.
وبين ضرورة السلب إشارة إلى المشروطة الخاصة.
ولا بينه وبين دوام السلب أي بين إمكان الإيجاب وبين دوام السلب بحسب الوصف لا دائما، وهذا الدوام معنى العرفية الخاصة السالبة، مثل كل إنسان كاتب ،بالإمكان ولا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب ما دام إنسانا لا دائما. وتحقيق هذا البحث يعني شرح الضابطة على ما هو الحق في نفس الأمر وهو مبتدأ وقوله: "مما تفردت به" خبره. وحاصل الكلام أن في تحقيقها ما لم يشاركني أحد فيه وهذا التحقيق العجيب الوجيه الجميل أدق التحقيقات. ولعمري أن الشارح اليزدي أصدق قائل في هذا المقال، غفر له الله الكبير المتعال.
على هذا الوجه الوجيه مما تفردت به بعون الله الجليل والله يهدى من يشاء إلى سواء السبيل، وهو حسبي ونعم الوكيل
Rule of the conditions of the four figures: As long as the essence and no incompatibility between it and the permanence of negation according to description not always, and no incompatibility between it and the necessity of negation at a specific time not always, and likewise if the minor premise is not necessary on the assumption that the major is possible, it is the most specific of the minor premises of the particular conditioned or permanent, and there is no incompatibility between the possibility of affirmation and the necessity of negation according to description not always, nor between it and the permanence of negation as long as the essence is certainly. And the verification of this research: 'and no incompatibility between it and the permanence of negation' i.e., between the possibility of affirmation and the permanence of negation according to description not always, and this permanence is the meaning of the particular negative 'urfiya, such as: every human is a writer potentially and permanently, and no human is a writer as long as he is human not always? between the permanence of negation like every writer is still-fingered potentially and permanently, no writer? is still as long as he is a writer not always. (Proof: 'and no incompatibility between it' i.e., between the possibility of affirmation and the necessity of negation at a specific time not always, and this necessity is the meaning of the temporal (waqtia) such as: every moon is eclipsed with common possibility, and by necessity nothing is luminous at the time of quadrature due to lack of incompatibility between every moon is eclipsed with common possibility and necessarily nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature not always because of the difference of the two times, i.e., the time of opposition and the time of quadrature. 'At a specific time' indicates that the major is temporal. 'Eclipsed' [marginal]. And likewise if the minor is not [necessary] i.e., likewise incompatibility does not occur when the second condition is absent, namely that the minor is not necessary on the assumption that the major is possible; for in this assumption it would be necessary that the minor be necessary, so the second condition is negated, and with its negation incompatibility is also negated; for there is no incompatibility between the two mentioned relations in: nothing of the stationary is a writer permanently or necessarily as long as stationary not always, and every stationary is a writer potentially; since there is no incompatibility between the possibility of affirming writing for the stationary with respect to essence and the necessity of negating writing from the stationary's essence with respect to description or its permanence as long as the description. 'And the necessity of negation' refers to the particular conditioned. 'And no incompatibility between it and the permanence of negation' i.e., between the possibility of affirmation and the permanence of negation according to description not always, and this permanence is the meaning of the particular negative 'urfiya, such as: every human is a writer potentially and nothing of human is a writer as long as human not always. And the verification of this research means explaining the rule according to what is true in itself, and it is the subject, and his saying: 'what I have been alone in' is its predicate. And the gist of the speech is that in its verification there is what no one has participated in with me, and this wonderful, elegant, beautiful verification is the most precise of verifications. By my life, the commentator al-Yazdi is the most truthful speaker in this article, may the Great and Exalted God forgive him. In this elegant manner which I have been alone in by the help of the Mighty God, and God guides whom He wills to the straight path, and He is my sufficiency and best Trustee.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a detailed verification (tahqiq) of the conditions for the four figures of categorical syllogism, specifically regarding modal premises. The author, commenting on al-Yazdi, explains when there is no incompatibility (la munafat) between the possibility of affirmation and various types of negation: permanent negation (dawam al-salb) according to description, necessary negation (darurat al-salb) at a specific time, etc. He gives examples: 'every human is a writer potentially' and 'no human is a writer as long as he is human' (not always) — this illustrates the 'urfiya khassa negative. Another example involves the moon and eclipse, showing temporal necessity (waqtia). The author emphasizes that these conditions are subtle and that he is alone in this precise verification, praising al-Yazdi. The passage ends with a prayer and acknowledgment of divine help.

Key Terms

دوام السلب
ضرورة السلب
العرفية الخاصة
الوقتية

C) Every writer is still-fingered.
D) Nothing is both human and writer at the same time.

Key Takeaway
In modal syllogisms, possibility and negation can coexist without contradiction when the possibility concerns the essence and the negation is qualified by description or time.

Four Compositions of Conditional Syllogisms
Author defines conditional syllogism and lists four compositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (two conditionals, two disjunctives, categorical+conditional, categorical+disjunctive) and classify any given example.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism can mix conditionals, disjunctives, and categoricals in four distinct ways.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the matn's classification of conditional conjunctive syllogisms. The four compositions are presented with clear examples. Understanding these types is essential for recognizing valid argument forms in Islamic logic, where premises often mix conditional and categorical statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Types
From the passage, list the four types of compositions the author states: from two conditionals, from two disjunctives, from a categorical and a conditional, from a categorical and a disjunctive.
2
Extract the Examples for Each Type
For each type, locate the example provided. Note the logical structure: the premises and the conclusion. For instance, the first example: 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists; whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated; conclusion: whenever the sun rises, the world is illuminated.'
These examples use non-Islamic content, but the logical forms apply universally.
3
Check Logical Validity
Verify that in each example the conclusion necessarily follows from the premises. For two conditionals, the conclusion is a transitive chain; for two disjunctives, the conclusion combines options; for mixed types, the categorical premise is combined with the conditional/disjunctive.
4
Map to Islamic Examples
Translate each type into an Islamic science example. For instance, replace 'sun rising' with a Kalam argument: 'If the world is created, it has a Creator; if it has a Creator, He is eternal; therefore if the world is created, He is eternal.'
This shows the utility of these forms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Conditional Conjunctive Syllogism and Its Divisions
Chapter
The conditional of the conjunctive [syllogism] is either composed of two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive.
In this elegant manner which I have been alone in by the help of the Mighty God, and God guides whom He wills to the straight path, and He is my sufficiency and best Trustee: His saying: 'from two conditionals' like our saying: Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists, and whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated, yields: Whenever the sun is rising, the world is illuminated. His saying: 'or two disjunctives' like our saying: Always the number is either even or odd, and always the even is either the even of the even or the even of the odd, yields: Always the number is either even of the even, or even of the odd, or odd. His saying: 'or a categorical and a conditional' like: This thing is a human, and whenever this thing is a human it is an animal, yields: This is an animal; and like: Whenever this thing is a human it is an animal, and every animal is a body, yields: Whenever this thing is a human it is a body. His saying: 'or a categorical and a disjunctive' like: This is a number, and always the number is either even or odd, yields: This is either even or odd.
•
He stated explicitly
By the help of the Mighty God: meaning, do not think that I have been alone in this by myself without help and guidance from other than God; for God Almighty helped me in it, and guided me to the straight path that leads to Him, not other; because He is the Chosen One who guides whom He wills, and the need in any matter is to other than Him Exalted; because He is my sufficiency, and I entrust my affair to God; for He is the best Trustee. The conditional of the conjunctive: the conjunctive is that which is not composed purely of categoricals, whether it is composed purely of conditionals or of them and categoricals. Its primary divisions are five, as the author said: 'either composed of two conditionals, etc.' As for the secondary divisions, for each of the first two and the fifth there are three divisions, for the third two divisions, and for the fourth four divisions, and the detail is in the lengthy works. Or 'a categorical and a conditional' – the composite of the categorical and the second is conditional, or the reverse. So the first example in the commentary is an example of the first and the second, not the second? (Isma'il: 'like this number, etc.' is an example of when the categorical is placed before the disjunctive, and he left the example where the disjunctive is placed first, relying on natural disposition, like our saying: Always the number is either even or odd, and each of them falls under quantity, so the number falls under quantity. Isma'il).
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage introduces conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-sharti). The author, commenting on the matn, states that a conditional conjunctive syllogism can be composed in four ways: two conditionals (e.g., 'whenever sun rises, daytime exists; whenever daytime exists, world is illuminated'), two disjunctives (e.g., 'number is either even or odd; even is either even of even or even of odd'), a categorical with a conditional (e.g., 'this is human; whenever human, animal'), or a categorical with a disjunctive (e.g., 'this is number; number is either even or odd'). The commentary then adds metaphysical remarks about divine help, then explains the definition of 'conjunctive', and notes that the primary divisions are five (including pure conditional, etc.), with secondary divisions detailed elsewhere. A marginal note from Isma'il clarifies an example.

Five Types of Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Five primary divisions of compound conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five primary types of compound conditional syllogisms from a given pair of premises.

Core Idea
Five combinations: two conditionals, two disjunctives, or mixed with categoricals.

This passage from the Tahzeeb al-Mantiq presents the five primary divisions of compound conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-sharti). Understanding these types is essential for analyzing arguments in Islamic theology (kalam) and jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh) that mix conditional, disjunctive, and categorical statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types
For each of the two premises, determine whether it is a categorical (hamliyya), conditional (muttasila), or disjunctive (munfasila) proposition.
Categorical: 'Every A is B'. Conditional: 'If A then B'. Disjunctive: 'Either A or B'.
2
Match the combination
Based on the types, classify the syllogism into one of the five primary divisions: (1) two conditionals, (2) two disjunctives, (3) categorical and conditional, (4) categorical and disjunctive, (5) conditional and disjunctive.
The passage gives explicit examples for each.
3
Verify with examples
Compare your classification with the standard examples provided in the text to ensure accuracy.
For instance, 'If sun is rising then daytime exists; if daytime exists then world is illuminated' is two conditionals.
4
Distinguish from pure categoricals
Remember that a compound conditional syllogism is defined as one not composed solely of categorical premises. If both premises are categorical, it is a simple categorical syllogism, not one of these five types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرطي من الاقتراني إما أن يتركب من متصلتين أو منفصلتين أو حملية ومتصلة أو حملية ومنفصلة أو متصلة ومنفصلة. قوله من متصلتين كقولنا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، وكلما كان النهار موجودا فالعالم مضيء ينتج كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالعالم مضيء. قوله أو منفصلتين كقولنا دائما إما أن يكون العدد زوجا وإما أن يكون فردا، ودائما إما أن يكون الزوج زوج الزوج أو يكون زوج الفرد ينتج: دائما إما أن يكون العدد زوج الزوج أو يكون زوج الفرد أو يكون فردا. قوله أو حملية ومتصلة نحو هذا الشيء إنسان وكلما كان هذا الشيء إنساناً كان حيوانا ينتج: هذا حيوان ونحو كلما كان هذا الشيء إنسانا فهو حيوان وكل حيوان جسم ينتج كلما كان هذا الشيء إنسانا كان جسما. قوله: أو حملية ومنفصلة: نحو هذا عدد ودائما إما أن يكون العدد زوجا أو فردا ينتج فهذا إما أن يكون زوجا أو فردا. قوله أو متصلة ومنفصلة نحو كلما كان هذا ثلاثة فهو عدد ودائما إما أن يكون العدد زوجا أو يكون فردا ينتج: كلما كان هذا ثلاثة فإما أن يكون زوجا أو فردا. الاقتراني هو الذي لم يتركب من حمليات صرفة، سواء تركب من شرطيات صرفة أو منها ومن الحمليات وأقسامه الأولية خمسة كما به المصنف بقوله: "إما أن يتركب من متصلتين إلخ" وأما الأقسام الثانوية فلكل من الأولين والخامس ثلاثة أقسام وللثالث قسمان وللرابع أربعة أقسام، والتفصيل في المطولات.
The conditional from the conjunctive is either composed of two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. His statement: from two conditionals, like our saying: always, when the sun is rising, then daytime exists, and always, when daytime exists, then the world is illuminated, yields: always, when the sun is rising, then the world is illuminated. His statement: or from two disjunctives, like our saying: always, either the number is even or it is odd, and always, either the even is twice of an even or it is twice of an odd, yields: always, either the number is twice of an even or twice of an odd or odd. His statement: or a categorical and a conditional, like: this thing is a human, and always when this thing is a human, it is an animal, yields: this is an animal; and like: always when this thing is a human, it is an animal, and every animal is a body, yields: always when this thing is a human, it is a body. His statement: or a categorical and a disjunctive, like: this is a number, and always, either the number is even or odd, yields: so this is either even or odd. His statement: or a conditional and a disjunctive, like: always when this is three, it is a number, and always, either the number is even or it is odd, yields: always when this is three, then it is either even or odd. The conjunctive is what is not composed of pure categoricals, whether it is composed of pure conditionals or of them and categoricals, and its primary divisions are five, as the author has with his statement: 'either composed of two conditionals, etc.' As for the secondary divisions, for each of the first two and the fifth there are three divisions, for the third two divisions, and for the fourth four divisions, and the detail is in the longer works.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage defines the types of compound conditional syllogisms in conjunctive logic. The author first lists five possible compositions: (1) from two conditionals (الشرطيتين), (2) from two disjunctives (المنفصلتين), (3) from a categorical and a conditional, (4) from a categorical and a disjunctive, and (5) from a conditional and a disjunctive. Each is illustrated with an example. The example for two conditionals uses the classical case of 'if the sun is rising, then daytime exists' and 'if daytime exists, the world is illuminated', concluding that when the sun rises, the world is illuminated. For two disjunctives, the example involves parity of numbers: if a number is either even or odd, and the even is either twice an even or twice an odd, then the number is either twice an even, twice an odd, or odd. The mixed examples use 'human' and 'animal' for categorical-conditional, and 'number' and 'parity' for categorical-disjunctive. The fifth type is illustrated with 'three' being a number and numbers being either even or odd. After the examples, the author clarifies what 'conjunctive' (اقتراني) means: it is a syllogism not composed of pure categoricals, but entirely of conditionals or a mix. He notes that the primary divisions are five, while secondary divisions vary: the first two and the fifth each have three subdivisions, the third has two, and the fourth has four. The detailed discussion is deferred to longer works. This classification is foundational for understanding the scope of conditional reasoning in Islamic logic.

The Syllogism

The Four Figures in Conditional Syllogisms
Author maps the four figures to conditional syllogisms via middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which figure a compound conditional syllogism belongs to based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The four figures of syllogism apply to conditional syllogisms just as they do to categorical ones.

In this passage, the commentator shows that the familiar four figures of categorical syllogisms also govern compound conditional syllogisms. The key is the middle term (الحد الأوسط) — the shared part between the two premises. By understanding how the middle term's position determines the figure, you can analyze conditional arguments with the same precision as categorical ones. This knowledge is essential for advanced theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Middle Term
In any compound conditional syllogism, the two premises must share a common part. That shared part is the middle term (الحد الأوسط). Locate it in both premises.
The middle term can be a whole proposition or a part of a proposition, depending on the type of conditional.
2
Determine Subject/Predicate Roles
For each premise, decide whether the middle term is the subject (محكوم عليه) or the predicate (محكوم به). In conditional propositions, the 'subject' is the antecedent and the 'predicate' is the consequent. Apply this analogy.
The terms are transferred from categorical logic; the commentator uses them to describe parts of conditionals.
3
Map Position to Figure
Use the mapping given by the commentator: (1) if middle term is predicate in both → third figure; (2) if subject in both → second figure; (3) if subject in minor and predicate in major → first figure; (4) if predicate in minor and subject in major → fourth figure.
The first figure is the most natural and direct; the others require conversion or additional steps.
4
Recognize the Limits of This Compendium
The commentator notes that the full details (conditions, moods, conclusions) for each figure across the five divisions of compound conditionals are lengthy. This passage gives the core conceptual framework; for full treatment, consult longer works.
This is a common pedagogical move in Islamic logic texts: outline the structure, then refer to advanced sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وينعقد: يعني لابد في تلك الأقسام من اشتراك المقدمتين في جزء يكون هو الحد الأوسط، فإما أن يكون محكوما عليه في كلتا المقدمتين أو محكوما به فيهما أو محكوما به في الصغرى ومحكوما عليه في الكبرى أو بالعكس. فالأول هو الشكل الثالث والثاني هو الثاني والثالث هو الأول والرابع هو الرابع قوله: وفي تفصيلها أي في تفصيل الأشكال الأربعة في تلك الأقسام الخمسة بحسب الشرائط والضروب والنتائج طول لا يليق بالمختصرات، فليطلب من مطولات المتأخرين.
His statement: 'and it is formed': meaning in those divisions, there must be sharing of the two premises in a part that is the middle term. So it is either that which is predicated in both premises, or that which is subject in both, or that which is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, or the opposite. So the first is the third figure, the second is the second, the third is the first, and the fourth is the fourth. His statement: 'and in its detail' i.e., in detailing the four figures in those five divisions according to conditions, moods, and conclusions, it is lengthy and not suitable for compendiums, so let it be sought from the longer works of the later scholars.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage explains that the four figures of syllogism are valid in compound conditional syllogisms. The author clarifies that for any of the five divisions of compound conditionals discussed previously, the two premises must share a common part, called the middle term (الحد الأوسط). This middle term can occupy four possible positions: (1) it is the predicate in both premises (محكوما به في كلتا المقدمتين) – this corresponds to the third figure; (2) it is the subject in both premises (محكوما عليه في كلتا المقدمتين) – this is the second figure; (3) it is the subject in the minor premise and the predicate in the major (محكوما به في الصغرى ومحكوما عليه في الكبرى) – this is the first figure; (4) it is the predicate in the minor and subject in the major (بالعكس) – this is the fourth figure. The author then notes that detailing the conditions, moods, and conclusions for each of these figures across the five divisions is lengthy and beyond the scope of the compendium; those details should be sought in longer works. This passage emphasizes the systematic nature of conditional logic, mirroring the categorical syllogism's figures.

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط
محكوما عليه
محكوما به
شكل أول
شكل ثاني
شكل ثالث
شكل رابع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The four figures of syllogism, defined by the middle term's position, apply to compound conditional syllogisms just as they do to categorical ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Therefore, if the Quran is the word of God, its commands are binding.' Identify the middle term and determine which figure this syllogism belongs to.

The Structure of Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains conditional conjunctive syllogism and its structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a conditional conjunctive syllogism and locate the middle term.

Core Idea
The middle term is the shared part linking two conditional premises.

In this passage, the author explains the conditional conjunctive syllogism, which consists of two conditional premises (conjunctive or disjunctive) that share a common part called the middle term. You will learn how to recognize the structure of such syllogisms and pinpoint the middle term that connects the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Look for two conditional statements. They may be conjunctive (if...then) or disjunctive (either...or). In the example, the first premise is 'كلما كان هذا ثلاثة فهو عدد' (If this is three, it is a number) and the second is 'دائما إما أن يكون العدد زوجا أو يكون فردا' (Always either the number is even or odd).
2
Determine the type of each premise
A conjunctive premise has the form 'if A then B' (كلما A فـ B). A disjunctive premise has the form 'either A or B' (إما A أو B). Recognize which type each premise belongs to.
3
Find the common part (middle term)
The middle term is the part present in both premises. In the example, both premises mention 'العدد' (the number). This is the middle term, which ties the two premises together.
4
Check the conclusion
The conclusion is also a conditional statement that combines the antecedent of the first premise with the consequent of the second (or similar). Here, the conclusion is 'كلما كان هذا ثلاثة فإما أن يكون زوجا أو فردا' (If this is three, then it is either even or odd).
The conclusion must be a conditional that follows from the premises.

القياس الاقتراني الشرطي وأقسامه
أو متصلة ومنفصلة، وينعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة وفي تفصيلها طول
قوله: أو متصلة ومنفصلة نحو كلما كان هذا ثلاثة فهو عدد ودائما إما أن يكون العدد زوجا أو يكون فردا ينتج: كلما كان هذا ثلاثة فإما أن يكون زوجا أو فردا. قوله: وينعقد: يعني لابد في تلك الأقسام من اشتراك المقدمتين في جزء يكون هو الحد الأوسط، فإما أن يكون محكوما عليه في كلتا المقدمتين أو محكوما به فيهما أو محكوما به في الصغرى ومحكوما عليه في الكبرى أو بالعكس. فالأول هو الشكل الثالث والثاني هو الثاني والثالث هو الأول والرابع هو الرابع قوله: وفي تفصيلها أي في تفصيل الأشكال الأربعة في تلك الأقسام الخمسة بحسب الشرائط والضروب والنتائج طول لا يليق بالمختصرات، فليطلب من مطولات المتأخرين.
نحو كلما كان إلخ ونحو دائما إما أن يكون العدد زوجا أو فردا فهو كم منفصل، فكلما كان عددا كان كما منفصلا. إسماعيل) من اشتراك المقدمتين في جزء: أي يكون جزء واحد جزء المقدمة الأولى وهو بعينه جزء الثانية أيضاً، فهو الحد الأوسط. (برهان) في كلتا المقدمتين: نحو : إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالعالم مضيء، وكلما كانت الشمس طالعة، فالنهار موجود فقد يكون إذا كان العالم مضيئا فالنهار موجود. محكوما به نحو كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالعالم مضيء، وليس ألبتة إذا كان الليل موجوداً فالعالم مضيء، فليس ألبتة كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان الليل موجودا.
في الصغرى نحو كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا وكلما كان النهار موجودا كان العالم مضيئا، فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان العالم مضيئاً.
أو بالعكس أي محكوما عليه في الصغرى ومحكوما به في الكبرى نحو كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فكان النهار موجودا، وكلما كان العالم مضيئا كانت الشمس طالعة، فقد يكون إذا كان النهار موجودا كان العالم مضيئا .
The Conditional Conjunctive Syllogism and Its Divisions
Either conjunctive or disjunctive, and the four figures occur in it, and their detail is lengthy.
His saying: 'Either conjunctive or disjunctive,' e.g., 'Whenever this is three, it is a number' and 'Always either the number is even or odd' yields: 'Whenever this is three, then either it is even or odd.' His saying: 'And it occurs' means it is necessary in those divisions that the two premises share a part which is the middle term: either it is the subject in both premises, or the predicate in both, or the predicate in the minor and the subject in the major, or the opposite. The first is the third figure, the second is the second, the third is the first, and the fourth is the fourth. His saying: 'And in their detail' means in the detail of the four figures in those five divisions according to conditions, moods, and conclusions, there is length that is not suitable for abridgments, so let it be sought from the lengthy works of the later scholars.
E.g., 'Whenever...' etc., and e.g., 'Always either the number is even or odd' is a disjunctive quantity, so whenever it is a number, it is a disjunctive quantity. (Isma'il) 'Sharing of the two premises in a part' means that one part is a part of the first premise and it is the very same part of the second as well, so it is the middle term. (Burhan) 'In both premises' e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then the world is illuminated, and whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists' — it may be that if the world is illuminated, then daytime exists. 'Predicate in both' e.g., 'Whenever the sun is shining, the world is illuminated, and absolutely not if night exists then the world is illuminated' — so absolutely not whenever the sun is shining, night exists.
'In the minor' e.g., 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists, and whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated' — so whenever the sun is shining, the world is illuminated.
'Or the opposite' i.e., subject in the minor and predicate in the major, e.g., 'Whenever the sun is shining, then daytime exists, and whenever the world is illuminated, the sun is shining' — it may be that if daytime exists, the world is illuminated.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage explains the conditional conjunctive syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-sharti), which consists of two conditional premises, either conjunctive (mutasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). The author states that the four figures of the categorical syllogism also apply here, based on the position of the middle term. He illustrates with examples: a conjunctive premise like 'Whenever this is three, it is a number' and a disjunctive like 'Always either the number is even or odd', yielding a conclusion that is a disjunctive conditional. The middle term must be a part common to both premises, and the four figures arise from whether it is subject or predicate in each premise. The author then gives examples for each figure using Islamic astronomy examples (sun, day, world illuminated). He notes that the full detail is too lengthy for abridgments.

The Syllogism

The Four Figures of Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates the four figures with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of a conditional conjunctive syllogism based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The figure depends on whether the middle term is subject or predicate in each premise.

The author explains that the four figures found in categorical syllogisms also apply to conditional conjunctive syllogisms, determined by the position of the middle term. Using examples from astronomy, you will learn to classify each syllogism into its figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Find the part common to both premises. In the first figure example, the middle term is 'النهار موجود' (day exists) because it appears in both premises: 'كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا' and 'كلما كان النهار موجودا كان العالم مضيئا'.
2
Check the middle term's role in the minor premise
In the minor premise, determine if the middle term is subject (محكوم عليه) or predicate (محكوم به). Here, 'النهار موجود' is the consequent (predicate) of the conditional minor premise.
3
Check the middle term's role in the major premise
In the major premise, determine the role. 'النهار موجود' is the antecedent (subject) of the conditional major premise.
4
Match to the figure patterns
The author gives four patterns: (1) subject in both = third figure; (2) predicate in both = second; (3) predicate in minor and subject in major = first; (4) subject in minor and predicate in major = fourth. Since here it is predicate in minor and subject in major, this is the first figure.

The Syllogism

Definition and Composition of the Exceptional Syllogism
Author defines the exceptional syllogism and its composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of an exceptional syllogism and distinguish it from a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The exceptional syllogism states its conclusion explicitly in the premises.

The exceptional syllogism is a cornerstone of logical inference in Islamic thought. Unlike the categorical syllogism where the conclusion follows from the premises, the exceptional syllogism already contains the conclusion in its matter and form. Let's examine its definition and composition as presented by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
An exceptional syllogism always has two premises: one conditional (shartiyya) and one categorical (hamliyya). The conditional premise contains the if-then structure, while the categorical premise 'excepts' one part or its contradictory.
The categorical premise is not just any categorical statement; it must be related to the conditional.
2
Determine what the categorical premise does
The categorical premise 'excepts' (istithna) exactly one of the two parts of the conditional (muqaddam or tali) or its contradictory. This is why the syllogism is called 'exceptional' (istithna'i).
The exception can be the exact part or its negation.
3
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is the other part of the conditional or its contradictory. Thus, the conclusion is already mentioned in the conditional premise, but the categorical premise determines which part is affirmed or denied.
This differs from categorical syllogisms where the conclusion is new.
4
Apply the definition to an example
Example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists. The sun is shining (exception: affirming antecedent). Therefore, daytime exists (conclusion: consequent).' This fits the definition.
The example uses natural phenomena, but similar forms apply in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس الاستثنائي وأقسامه
فصل
الاستثنائي ينتج من المتصلة
قوله: الاستثنائي: القياس الاستثنائي وهو الذي تكون النتيجة مذكورة فيه بمادته وهيئته أبدا، مقدمة شرطية ومن مقدمة حملية يستثنى فيها عين أحد جزئي الشرطية أو نقيضه لينتج
يتركب من
عين الآخر أو نقيضه، فالاحتمالات المتصورة في إنتاج كل استثنائي أربعة وضع كل، ورفع كل لكن المنتج منها في كل قسم شيء. وتفصيله ما أفاده المصنف من أن الشرطية إن كانت متصلة
ينتج من المتصلة يعني أن القياس الاستثنائي إما اتصالي أو انفصالي لابد من مقدمتين: أحدهما شرطية والآخر حملية فإن كانت الشرطية متصلا يسمى اتصاليا، وإن كانت منفصلة يسمى انفصاليا. أما الاستثنائي المركب من الشرطية المتصلة فينتج دائما وقت وضع المقدم وضع التالي ووقت رفع التالي، ينتج رفع المقدم إنتاجا لازما في جميع المواد ؛ فإن النتيجة اللازمة له إحدى هاتين النتيجتين، وعند المعاندة بين المقدم والتالي أربع نتائج: عند وضع كل رفع الآخر وعند رفع الآخر وعند رفع كل وضع الآخر؛ ومن هذا البيان ينعطف بتقدير الوقت والمفعول محذوف وهو وضع التالي في المعطوف عليه ورفع المقدم في المعطوف أي ينتج القياس الاستثنائي من المتصلة عند وضع المقدم وضع التالي وعند رفع التالي رفع المقدم، ويمكن أن يكون قوله: وضع المقدم" منصوبا بنزع كلمة "من" أو مرفوعا؛ لكونه فاعلاً لقوله : "ينتج ويكون اللام في المقدم عوضا عن المضاف إليه وهو الضمير الراجع إلى الاستثنائي، وقس عليه قوله: رفع التالي"، ومن قال: "إنه حال" فقد سها حال الحان وفزع في البال. (تحفه)
الخافض، وهو
يتركب إلخ: نحو: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود لكن الشمس طالعة، فالأولى شرطية والثانية حملية يستثنى فيها عين المقدم ولو قلنا لكن النهار ليس بموجود فيستثنى فيها نقيض التالي فينتج في الأولى عين التالي وهو فالنهار موجود وفي الثاني ينتج نقيض المقدم هو الشمس ليست بطالعة. (برهان) يستثنى فيها صفة حملية أي القياس الاستثنائي يتركب من مقدمتين إحداهما شرطية والثانية حملية لكن لا مطلقا بل الحملية التي يكون المستثنى فيها عين أحد جزئي الشرطية أو نقيضه في كل قسم شيء: أي في وضع كل ينتج وضع المقدم وفي رفع كل ينتج رفع التالي، وبالجملة وضع المقدم والتالي قسم واحد، ورفع التالي أو المقدم قسم آخر، فالمنتج في الأول وضع المقدم فقط لا وضع التالي، وفي الثاني ينتج رفع التالي فقط لا رفع المقدم. (برهان) في كل قسم من القسمين أي وضع كل ورفع كل منقسمين إلى أربعة أقسام.
The Exceptional Syllogism and Its Divisions
Chapter
The exceptional syllogism yields from the conjunctive.
His saying: 'The exceptional' is the exceptional syllogism, which is that the conclusion is always mentioned in it in its matter and form. It is composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise in which exactly one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory is excepted to produce exactly the other part or its contradictory. Thus the conceivable possibilities in the production of every exceptional syllogism are four: affirming both, and denying both, but the productive among them in each division is something. And its detail is what the author expressed: that if the conditional is conjunctive,
it yields from the conjunctive, meaning that the exceptional syllogism is either conjunctive or disjunctive; it must have two premises: one conditional and the other categorical. If the conditional is conjunctive, it is called conjunctive exceptional; if disjunctive, it is called disjunctive exceptional. As for the exceptional composed of a conjunctive conditional, it always yields, at the time of affirming the antecedent, affirming the consequent, and at the time of denying the consequent, denying the antecedent, as a necessary production in all matters. For the necessary conclusion from it is one of these two conclusions. And when there is opposition between the antecedent and the consequent, there are four results: when affirming both, denying the other; and when denying the other; and when denying both, affirming the other. From this explanation, it turns with the estimation of time, and the object is omitted, which is affirming the consequent in the conjoined and denying the antecedent in the conjoined, i.e., the exceptional syllogism from the conjunctive yields when affirming the antecedent, affirming the consequent, and when denying the consequent, denying the antecedent. And it is possible that his saying 'affirming the antecedent' is accusative by removing the word 'min' or nominative because it is the subject of 'yuntij', and the lam in 'al-muqaddam' is a substitute for the genitive, which is the pronoun referring to the exceptional. And analogize to his saying: 'denying the consequent'. And whoever said that it is a circumstance has erred. (Tuhfa)
The lowering, and it is
'It is composed' etc.: e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists, but the sun is shining' — the first is conditional, the second is categorical in which exactly the antecedent is excepted. If we said 'but daytime does not exist', then the contradictory of the consequent is excepted, and it yields in the first the exact consequent, which is 'daytime exists', and in the second it yields the contradictory of the antecedent, which is 'the sun is not shining'. (Burhan) 'In it is excepted a categorical attribute' i.e., the exceptional syllogism is composed of two premises, one conditional and the other categorical, but not absolutely, rather the categorical in which exactly one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory is excepted. 'In each division something' i.e., in affirming both, it yields affirming the antecedent, and in denying both, it yields denying the consequent. In summary, affirming the antecedent and consequent is one division, and denying the consequent or antecedent is another division. So the productive in the first is only affirming the antecedent, not affirming the consequent, and in the second it yields only denying the consequent, not denying the antecedent. (Burhan) 'In each of the two divisions' i.e., affirming both and denying both are divided into four sections.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). It is composed of a conditional premise (either conjunctive or disjunctive) and a categorical premise that excepts either one of the parts of the conditional or its contradictory, leading to a conclusion that is the other part or its contradictory. The author explains that with a conjunctive conditional, the valid inferences are affirming the antecedent to get the consequent, and denying the consequent to get the antecedent. This is necessary in all matters. He discusses four possibilities: affirming both, denying both, and the two mixed cases, but only the two mentioned are productive. He gives an Islamic science example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; the sun is shining (affirming antecedent) therefore daytime exists.' Or '...but daytime does not exist (denying consequent) therefore the sun is not shining.' The commentary also covers grammatical and logical nuances of the text.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Valid Inferences from the Conjunctive Conditional
Author explains valid inferences from a conjunctive conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate the two productive forms of the conjunctive exceptional syllogism: affirming antecedent and denying consequent.

Core Idea
Only affirming antecedent and denying consequent are valid; the other two are fallacious.

Once you understand the structure, the next step is knowing which inferences are valid. The author explains that from a conjunctive conditional, only two forms are always productive: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and denying the consequent yields denying the antecedent. Let's explore why these are valid and the others are not.

Guided Path
1
List the four possible operations
From a conjunctive conditional ('If P then Q'), we can either affirm or deny the antecedent (P) or affirm or deny the consequent (Q). This gives four possible operations: affirming P, denying P, affirming Q, denying Q.
These correspond to the four conceivable possibilities mentioned in the passage.
2
Identify the two productive forms
The author states that only two are productive: affirming the antecedent produces affirming the consequent (wad' al-muqaddam yuntij wad' al-tali), and denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent (raf' al-tali yuntij raf' al-muqaddam). These correspond to modus ponens and modus tollens.
The other two (denying antecedent and affirming consequent) are invalid.
3
Explain why the invalid forms fail
Denying the antecedent (e.g., 'P is false') does not necessarily mean Q is false because Q could be true for other reasons. Affirming the consequent (e.g., 'Q is true') does not guarantee P is true because Q could be caused by something else.
The author calls these 'non-productive' (ghayr muntij) because they do not yield a necessary conclusion.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider: 'If the prayer is invalid, then the purification was defective.' (P→Q). Valid: 'The prayer is invalid (affirm P), therefore purification was defective (affirm Q).' Valid: 'Purification was not defective (deny Q), therefore prayer is not invalid (deny P).' Invalid: 'Purification was defective (affirm Q), therefore prayer is invalid.' Invalid: 'Prayer is not invalid (deny P), therefore purification was not defective.'
Use this example to practice distinguishing valid from invalid inferences.

القياس الاستثنائي وأقسامه

الاستثنائي ينتج من المتصلة
قوله: الاستثنائي: القياس الاستثنائي وهو الذي تكون النتيجة مذكورة فيه بمادته وهيئته أبدا، مقدمة شرطية ومن مقدمة حملية يستثنى فيها عين أحد جزئي الشرطية أو نقيضه لينتج
يتركب من
عين الآخر أو نقيضه، فالاحتمالات المتصورة في إنتاج كل استثنائي أربعة وضع كل، ورفع كل لكن المنتج منها في كل قسم شيء. وتفصيله ما أفاده المصنف من أن الشرطية إن كانت متصلة
ينتج من المتصلة يعني أن القياس الاستثنائي إما اتصالي أو انفصالي لابد من مقدمتين: أحدهما شرطية والآخر حملية فإن كانت الشرطية متصلا يسمى اتصاليا، وإن كانت منفصلة يسمى انفصاليا. أما الاستثنائي المركب من الشرطية المتصلة فينتج دائما وقت وضع المقدم وضع التالي ووقت رفع التالي، ينتج رفع المقدم إنتاجا لازما في جميع المواد ؛ فإن النتيجة اللازمة له إحدى هاتين النتيجتين، وعند المعاندة بين المقدم والتالي أربع نتائج: عند وضع كل رفع الآخر وعند رفع الآخر وعند رفع كل وضع الآخر؛ ومن هذا البيان ينعطف بتقدير الوقت والمفعول محذوف وهو وضع التالي في المعطوف عليه ورفع المقدم في المعطوف أي ينتج القياس الاستثنائي من المتصلة عند وضع المقدم وضع التالي وعند رفع التالي رفع المقدم، ويمكن أن يكون قوله: وضع المقدم" منصوبا بنزع كلمة "من" أو مرفوعا؛ لكونه فاعلاً لقوله : "ينتج ويكون اللام في المقدم عوضا عن المضاف إليه وهو الضمير الراجع إلى الاستثنائي، وقس عليه قوله: رفع التالي"، ومن قال: "إنه حال" فقد سها حال الحان وفزع في البال. (تحفه)
الخافض، وهو
يتركب إلخ: نحو: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود لكن الشمس طالعة، فالأولى شرطية والثانية حملية يستثنى فيها عين المقدم ولو قلنا لكن النهار ليس بموجود فيستثنى فيها نقيض التالي فينتج في الأولى عين التالي وهو فالنهار موجود وفي الثاني ينتج نقيض المقدم هو الشمس ليست بطالعة. The Exceptional Syllogism and Its Divisions
Chapter
The exceptional syllogism yields from the conjunctive.
His saying: 'The exceptional' is the exceptional syllogism, which is that the conclusion is always mentioned in it in its matter and form. It is composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise in which exactly one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory is excepted to produce exactly the other part or its contradictory. Thus the conceivable possibilities in the production of every exceptional syllogism are four: affirming both, and denying both, but the productive among them in each division is something. And its detail is what the author expressed: that if the conditional is conjunctive,
it yields from the conjunctive, meaning that the exceptional syllogism is either conjunctive or disjunctive; it must have two premises: one conditional and the other categorical. If the conditional is conjunctive, it is called conjunctive exceptional; if disjunctive, it is called disjunctive exceptional. As for the exceptional composed of a conjunctive conditional, it always yields, at the time of affirming the antecedent, affirming the consequent, and at the time of denying the consequent, denying the antecedent, as a necessary production in all matters. For the necessary conclusion from it is one of these two conclusions. And when there is opposition between the antecedent and the consequent, there are four results: when affirming both, denying the other; and when denying the other; and when denying both, affirming the other. From this explanation, it turns with the estimation of time, and the object is omitted, which is affirming the consequent in the conjoined and denying the antecedent in the conjoined, i.e., the exceptional syllogism from the conjunctive yields when affirming the antecedent, affirming the consequent, and when denying the consequent, denying the antecedent. And it is possible that his saying 'affirming the antecedent' is accusative by removing the word 'min' or nominative because it is the subject of 'yuntij', and the lam in 'al-muqaddam' is a substitute for the genitive, which is the pronoun referring to the exceptional. And analogize to his saying: 'denying the consequent'. And whoever said that it is a circumstance has erred. (Tuhfa)
The lowering, and it is
'It is composed' etc.: e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists, but the sun is shining' — the first is conditional, the second is categorical in which exactly the antecedent is excepted. If we said 'but daytime does not exist', then the contradictory of the consequent is excepted, and it yields in the first the exact consequent, which is 'daytime exists', and in the second it yields the contradictory of the antecedent, which is 'the sun is not shining'. (Burhan) 'In it is excepted a categorical attribute' i.e., the exceptional syllogism is composed of two premises, one conditional and the other categorical, but not absolutely, rather the categorical in which exactly one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory is excepted. 'In each division something' i.e., in affirming both, it yields affirming the antecedent, and in denying both, it yields denying the consequent. In summary, affirming the antecedent and consequent is one division, and denying the consequent or antecedent is another division. So the productive in the first is only affirming the antecedent, not affirming the consequent, and in the second it yields only denying the consequent, not denying the antecedent. (Burhan) 'In each of the two divisions' i.e., affirming both and denying both are divided into four sections.

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). It is composed of a conditional premise (either conjunctive or disjunctive) and a categorical premise that excepts either one of the parts of the conditional or its contradictory, leading to a conclusion that is the other part or its contradictory. The author explains that with a conjunctive conditional, the valid inferences are affirming the antecedent to get the consequent, and denying the consequent to get the antecedent. This is necessary in all matters. He discusses four possibilities: affirming both, denying both, and the two mixed cases, but only the two mentioned are productive. He gives an Islamic science example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; the sun is shining (affirming antecedent) therefore daytime exists.' Or '...but daytime does not exist (denying consequent) therefore the sun is not shining.' The commentary also covers grammatical and logical nuances of the text.

The Syllogism

Two Valid Rules of the Conditional Syllogism
Author states the two valid inferences of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two valid inference rules (affirming antecedent and denying consequent) to evaluate conditional syllogisms in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Only affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent yield certain conclusions.

In conditional syllogisms, not every combination of affirming or denying yields a valid inference. This lesson introduces the two valid moves that guarantee valid conclusions, as explained in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In the conditional, find the 'malzum' (antecedent) and 'lazim' (consequent). For example, in 'If the sale is valid, ownership transfers', the antecedent is 'sale valid' and consequent is 'ownership transfers'.
2
Apply modus ponens
If the antecedent is affirmed, conclude the consequent. The text says 'wad' al-muqaddam yuntij wad' al-tali' because the realization of the necessitating condition entails the necessary result.
3
Apply modus tollens
If the consequent is denied, conclude the denial of the antecedent. The text says 'raf' al-tali yuntij raf' al-muqaddam' because the non-existence of the necessary result entails the non-existence of the condition.
4
Verify luzumiyya
Ensure the conditional is implicative (luzumiyya), not accidental. The text states only luzumiyya conditionals are intended in this chapter.
