⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس الاستثنائي وأقسامه ينتج منها احتمالان: وضع المقدم ينتج وضع التالي؛ لاستلزام تحقق الملزوم تحقق اللازم، ورفع التالي ينتج رفع المقدم لاستلزام انتفاء اللازم انتفاء الملزوم، وأما وضع التالي فلا ينتج وضع المقدم، ولا رفع المقدم ينتج رفع التالي؛ لجواز كون اللازم أعم، فلا يلزم من تحققه تحقق الملزوم ولا من الملزوم انتفاء اللازم، وقد علمت من هذا أن المراد بالمتصلة في هذا الباب اللزومية. واعلم أيضاً: دليل المقدمتين: الأولى وضع التالي لا ينتج وضع المقدم؛ لأن المقدم ملزوم والتالي لازم واللازم قد يعم يلزم من تحقق الأعم كالسواد تحقق الملزوم الأخص كالحبشي؛ فإن الغراب أسود وليس بحبشي. والثانية رفع المقدم لا ينتج رفع التالي؛ لأن اللازم يعم والملزوم قد ينتفي كالحبشي ولا ينتفي اللازم كالسواد. (برهان) تحقق الملزوم: إذ تحقق الأعم لا يستلزم تحقق الأخص؛ لجواز تحققه في ضمن آخر كقولنا: كلما كان هذا إنسانا كان حيوانا، لكنه حيوان فلا يلزم منه كونه إنسانا؛ لجواز تحقق الحيوان في الفرس مثلا مع عدم وجود الإنسان. (معراج) الفهم: انتفاء اللازم لأن انتفاء الأخص لا يستلزم انتفاء الأعم، كقولنا: كلما كان هذا إنسانا كان حيوانا لكنه ليس بإنسان فلا يلزم منه كونه ليس بحيوان؛ لجواز أن يكون فرسا فكان حيوانا بلا شبهة. (معراج) من هذا: أي من هذا البيان بل من كلام المصنف أن المراد بالمتصلة في قوله: ينتج من المتصلة اللزومية لا الاتفاقية، إما من بيان الشارح فواضح، وإما من كلام المصنف؛ فلأن النتيجة لازمة للقياس، واللزوم إنما يتصور في المتصلة اللزومية لا الاتفاقية كما لا يخفى. وقيل إن الاستثنائي من المتصلة الاتفاقية لا يفيد أصلا، وقيل: إنه من المتصلة اللزومية ينتج لزومية ومن الاتفاقية اتفاقية. قال الشيخ: لا نسلم أن القياس الشرطي المركب من المتصلتين اللزوميتين ينتج لزومية بأن كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان عددا وكلما كان عدداً كان زوجا على هيئة الشكل الأول والنتيجة كاذبة أعني كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان زوجا، وأجاب بأن الصغرى اتفاقية. اللزومية: لا الاتفاقية؛ إذ لما لم يكن بين المقدمتين علاقة ومناسبة بل يكون الحكم بمحض الاتفاق فلا يلزم من وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر ولا من انتفائه انتفاء الآخر فلا يتركب القياس منهما، وكذا في العنادية. (برهان الدين) واعلم أيضاً إلخ: قيل: الأولى أن يذكر هذا الكلام أيضاً بعد التفصيل الذي بعده كما ذكره أن المراد من المتصلة اللزومية بعد التفصيل، وقيل: إن ذكر هذا الكلام بعد التفصيل ليس أولى من ذكره في هذا المقام، وذلك بوجوه، منها: أن المنفصلة قسيم للمتصلة، وذكر التقسيم الذي القسيم أنسب ومنها: أن الشارح من البلغاء وتبديل أسلوب البيان من البلاغة ومنها: أن الوجه الذي فيه إصلاح الكلام أولى من الوجه الذي فيه إفساده.
The exceptional syllogism and its divisions: two probabilities result from it: affirmation of the antecedent produces affirmation of the consequent, because the realization of the necessary condition entails the realization of the necessary consequent; and denial of the consequent produces denial of the antecedent, because the non-existence of the consequent entails the non-existence of the necessary condition. As for affirmation of the consequent, it does not produce affirmation of the antecedent, nor does denial of the antecedent produce denial of the consequent, because the consequent may be more general, so its realization does not entail the realization of the necessary condition, nor does the non-existence of the necessary condition entail the non-existence of the consequent. And you have learned from this that what is meant by 'connected conditional' in this chapter is the implicative one. And also know: proof of the two premises: the first, affirmation of the consequent does not produce affirmation of the antecedent, because the antecedent is the necessitating condition and the consequent is the necessary, and the necessary may be general; the realization of the general like blackness does not entail the realization of the specific necessary condition like Ethiopian, for the crow is black and not Ethiopian. And the second, denial of the antecedent does not produce denial of the consequent, because the necessary is general and the necessitating condition may be absent, like Ethiopian, while the necessary (blackness) is not absent. (Proof) realization of the necessary condition: because the realization of the general does not necessitate the realization of the specific, as it may be realized in something else, e.g., whenever this is a human it is an animal, but it is an animal, so it does not necessarily follow that it is a human, as the animal may be realized in a horse for example while the human does not exist. (Mi'raj) Understanding: the non-existence of the necessary, because the non-existence of the specific does not necessitate the non-existence of the general, e.g., whenever this is a human it is an animal, but it is not a human, so it does not necessarily follow that it is not an animal, because it could be a horse and thus an animal without doubt. (Mi'raj) From this: i.e., from this exposition, but rather from the author's statement, that what is meant by 'connected conditional' in his saying 'produces from the connected conditional' is the implicative, not the accidental. As for from the commentator's exposition it is clear; as for from the author's statement, because the conclusion is necessary for the syllogism, and necessity can only be conceived in the implicative connected conditional, not the accidental, as is not hidden. And it was said: the exceptional syllogism from the accidental connected conditional yields nothing at all; and it was said: from the implicative connected conditional it produces an implicative conclusion, and from the accidental it produces an accidental conclusion. Al-Shaykh said: we do not grant that the conditional syllogism composed of two implicative connected conditionals produces an implicative conclusion, on the basis that 'whenever two is odd it is a number, and whenever it is a number it is even' in the first figure, and the conclusion is false, namely 'whenever two is odd it is even'. And it was answered: the minor premise is accidental. The implicative: not the accidental; because when there is no relationship and suitability between the two premises, but rather the judgment is by mere coincidence, then the existence of one does not entail the existence of the other nor does its non-existence entail the other's non-existence, so the syllogism is not composed from them, and similarly in the [؟]. (Burhan al-Din) And also know etc.: It was said: it is more appropriate to mention this statement also after the detail that follows, as he mentioned that what is meant by 'connected conditional' is the implicative after the detail. And it was said: mentioning this statement after the detail is not more appropriate than mentioning it in this place, for several reasons: among them that the disjunctive is a counterpart to the connected, and mentioning the division in which the counterpart is more fitting; and among them that the commentator is among the eloquent, and changing the style of exposition is among eloquence; and among them that the view in which there is improvement of speech is more appropriate than the view in which there is corruption.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the connected conditional (muttasila) syllogism, a key part of conditional (istithna'i) reasoning. It begins by stating that the conditional syllogism yields two types of valid inference: affirming the antecedent leads to affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent leads to denying the antecedent. This is because the antecedent is the 'malzum' (necessitating condition) and the consequent is the 'lazim' (necessary result), so the realization of the condition necessitates the result, and the absence of the result necessitates the absence of the condition. Conversely, affirming the consequent does not yield a conclusion about the antecedent, nor does denying the antecedent yield a conclusion about the consequent, because the consequent may be more general (a'amm) than the antecedent, just as 'animal' is more general than 'human'. The text then explicitly states that the connected conditional intended in this chapter is the 'luzumiyya' (implicative) type, not the 'ittifaqiyya' (accidental) type, where the connection is merely coincidental. A detailed proof follows, using examples like 'blackness' (general) and 'Ethiopian' (specific) to illustrate that the general can exist without the specific. The commentary then discusses debates: some say accidental conditionals yield no conclusion, others say they yield accidental conclusions. A counterexample from 'al-Shaykh' involving the numbers (two is odd, all odd numbers are numbers, all numbers are even) is presented and answered by pointing out that the minor premise is accidental, not genuinely implicative. Finally, there is a textual note about the placement of the statement, arguing whether it was better placed before or after the upcoming discussion on disjunctives.

اللازم

Invalid Inferences: Affirming Consequent and Denying Antecedent
Author proves the invalidity of affirming consequent and denying antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are invalid in conditional arguments.

Core Idea
Affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent yields no necessary conclusion because the consequent may be more general.

The text explicitly warns that two common moves are invalid: affirming the consequent does not guarantee the antecedent, and denying the antecedent does not eliminate the consequent. This lesson unpacks the reasoning using examples like blackness/Ethiopian and human/animal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical structure
In a conditional, we have antecedent -> consequent. The antecedent is the 'malzum' (necessitating condition) and the consequent is the 'lazim' (necessary result).
2
See why affirming consequent fails
Affirming the consequent (e.g., 'it is black') does not prove the antecedent (e.g., 'they are Ethiopian') because the consequent can have other causes. The example: the crow is black but not Ethiopian.
The text says 'اللازم قد يعم' - the consequent may be more general.
3
See why denying antecedent fails
Denying the antecedent (e.g., 'it is not Ethiopian') does not disprove the consequent (e.g., 'blackness') because the consequent may still exist without that particular antecedent. The example: a non-Ethiopian person can still be black.
The text uses the same example in reverse.
4
Generalize with abstract examples
The text also uses 'If human then animal' and shows that affirming animal does not prove human, and denying human does not deny animal (could be a horse).
This illustrates the abstract pattern of a'amm wa akhass (general-specific).

القياس الاستثنائي وأقسامه ينتج منها احتمالان: وضع المقدم ينتج وضع التالي؛ لاستلزام تحقق الملزوم تحقق اللازم، ورفع التالي ينتج رفع المقدم لاستلزام انتفاء اللازم انتفاء الملزوم، وأما وضع التالي فلا ينتج وضع المقدم، ولا رفع المقدم ينتج رفع التالي؛ لجواز كون اللازم أعم، فلا يلزم من تحققه تحقق الملزوم ولا من الملزوم انتفاء اللازم، وقد علمت من هذا أن المراد بالمتصلة في هذا الباب اللزومية. واعلم أيضاً: دليل المقدمتين: الأولى وضع التالي لا ينتج وضع المقدم؛ لأن المقدم ملزوم والتالي لازم واللازم قد يعم يلزم من تحقق الأعم كالسواد تحقق الملزوم الأخص كالحبشي؛ فإن الغراب أسود وليس بحبشي. والثانية رفع المقدم لا ينتج رفع التالي؛ لأن اللازم يعم والملزوم قد ينتفي كالحبشي ولا ينتفي اللازم كالسواد. (برهان) تحقق الملزوم: إذ تحقق الأعم لا يستلزم تحقق الأخص؛ لجواز تحققه في ضمن آخر كقولنا: كلما كان هذا إنسانا كان حيوانا، لكنه حيوان فلا يلزم منه كونه إنسانا؛ لجواز تحقق الحيوان في الفرس مثلا مع عدم وجود الإنسان. (معراج) الفهم: انتفاء اللازم لأن انتفاء الأخص لا يستلزم انتفاء الأعم، كقولنا: كلما كان هذا إنسانا كان حيوانا لكنه ليس بإنسان فلا يلزم منه كونه ليس بحيوان؛ لجواز أن يكون فرسا فكان حيوانا بلا شبهة. (معراج) من هذا: أي من هذا البيان بل من كلام المصنف أن المراد بالمتصلة في قوله: ينتج من المتصلة اللزومية لا الاتفاقية، إما من بيان الشارح فواضح، وإما من كلام المصنف؛ فلأن النتيجة لازمة للقياس، واللزوم إنما يتصور في المتصلة اللزومية لا الاتفاقية كما لا يخفى. وقيل إن الاستثنائي من المتصلة الاتفاقية لا يفيد أصلا، وقيل: إنه من المتصلة اللزومية ينتج لزومية ومن الاتفاقية اتفاقية. قال الشيخ: لا نسلم أن القياس الشرطي المركب من المتصلتين اللزوميتين ينتج لزومية بأن كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان عددا وكلما كان عدداً كان زوجا على هيئة الشكل الأول والنتيجة كاذبة أعني كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان زوجا، وأجاب بأن الصغرى اتفاقية. اللزومية: لا الاتفاقية؛ إذ لما لم يكن بين المقدمتين علاقة ومناسبة بل يكون الحكم بمحض الاتفاق فلا يلزم من وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر ولا من انتفائه انتفاء الآخر فلا يتركب القياس منهما، وكذا في العنادية. (برهان الدين) واعلم أيضاً إلخ: قيل: الأولى أن يذكر هذا الكلام أيضاً بعد التفصيل الذي بعده كما ذكره أن المراد من المتصلة اللزومية بعد التفصيل، وقيل: إن ذكر هذا الكلام بعد التفصيل ليس أولى من ذكره في هذا المقام، وذلك بوجوه، منها: أن المنفصلة قسيم للمتصلة، وذكر التقسيم الذي القسيم أنسب ومنها: أن الشارح من البلغاء وتبديل أسلوب البيان من البلاغة ومنها: أن الوجه الذي فيه إصلاح الكلام أولى من الوجه الذي فيه إفساده.
The exceptional syllogism and its divisions: two probabilities result from it: affirmation of the antecedent produces affirmation of the consequent, because the realization of the necessary condition entails the realization of the necessary consequent; and denial of the consequent produces denial of the antecedent, because the non-existence of the consequent entails the non-existence of the necessary condition. As for affirmation of the consequent, it does not produce affirmation of the antecedent, nor does denial of the antecedent produce denial of the consequent, because the consequent may be more general, so its realization does not entail the realization of the necessary condition, nor does the non-existence of the necessary condition entail the non-existence of the consequent. And you have learned from this that what is meant by 'connected conditional' in this chapter is the implicative one. And also know: proof of the two premises: the first, affirmation of the consequent does not produce affirmation of the antecedent, because the antecedent is the necessitating condition and the consequent is the necessary, and the necessary may be general; the realization of the general like blackness does not entail the realization of the specific necessary condition like Ethiopian, for the crow is black and not Ethiopian. And the second, denial of the antecedent does not produce denial of the consequent, because the necessary is general and the necessitating condition may be absent, like Ethiopian, while the necessary (blackness) is not absent. (Proof) realization of the necessary condition: because the realization of the general does not necessitate the realization of the specific, as it may be realized in something else, e.g., whenever this is a human it is an animal, but it is an animal, so it does not necessarily follow that it is a human, as the animal may be realized in a horse for example while the human does not exist. (Mi'raj) Understanding: the non-existence of the necessary, because the non-existence of the specific does not necessitate the non-existence of the general, e.g., whenever this is a human it is an animal, but it is not a human, so it does not necessarily follow that it is not an animal, because it could be a horse and thus an animal without doubt. (Mi'raj) From this: i.e., from this exposition, but rather from the author's statement, that what is meant by 'connected conditional' in his saying 'produces from the connected conditional' is the implicative, not the accidental. As for from the commentator's exposition it is clear; as for from the author's statement, because the conclusion is necessary for the syllogism, and necessity can only be conceived in the implicative connected conditional, not the accidental, as is not hidden. And it was said: the exceptional syllogism from the accidental connected conditional yields nothing at all; and it was said: from the implicative connected conditional it produces an implicative conclusion, and from the accidental it produces an accidental conclusion. Al-Shaykh said: we do not grant that the conditional syllogism composed of two implicative connected conditionals produces an implicative conclusion, on the basis that 'whenever two is odd it is a number, and whenever it is a number it is even' in the first figure, and the conclusion is false, namely 'whenever two is odd it is even'. And it was answered: the minor premise is accidental. The implicative: not the accidental; because when there is no relationship and suitability between the two premises, but rather the judgment is by mere coincidence, then the existence of one does not entail the existence of the other nor does its non-existence entail the other's non-existence, so the syllogism is not composed from them, and similarly in the [؟]. (Burhan al-Din) And also know etc.: It was said: it is more appropriate to mention this statement also after the detail that follows, as he mentioned that what is meant by 'connected conditional' is the implicative after the detail. And it was said: mentioning this statement after the detail is not more appropriate than mentioning it in this place, for several reasons: among them that the disjunctive is a counterpart to the connected, and mentioning the division in which the counterpart is more fitting; and among them that the commentator is among the eloquent, and changing the style of exposition is among eloquence; and among them that the view in which there is improvement of speech is more appropriate than the view in which there is corruption.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the connected conditional (muttasila) syllogism, a key part of conditional (istithna'i) reasoning. It begins by stating that the conditional syllogism yields two types of valid inference: affirming the antecedent leads to affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent leads to denying the antecedent. This is because the antecedent is the 'malzum' (necessitating condition) and the consequent is the 'lazim' (necessary result), so the realization of the condition necessitates the result, and the absence of the result necessitates the absence of the condition. Conversely, affirming the consequent does not yield a conclusion about the antecedent, nor does denying the antecedent yield a conclusion about the consequent, because the consequent may be more general (a'amm) than the antecedent, just as 'animal' is more general than 'human'. The text then explicitly states that the connected conditional intended in this chapter is the 'luzumiyya' (implicative) type, not the 'ittifaqiyya' (accidental) type, where the connection is merely coincidental. A detailed proof follows, using examples like 'blackness' (general) and 'Ethiopian' (specific) to illustrate that the general can exist without the specific. The commentary then discusses debates: some say accidental conditionals yield no conclusion, others say they yield accidental conclusions. A counterexample from 'al-Shaykh' involving the numbers (two is odd, all odd numbers are numbers, all numbers are even) is presented and answered by pointing out that the minor premise is accidental, not genuinely implicative. Finally, there is a textual note about the placement of the statement, arguing whether it was better placed before or after the upcoming discussion on disjunctives.

اللازم
وضع التالي
رفع المقدم
الأعم
الأخص

Checkpoint
Given the conditional: 'If a person is sane, their testimony is valid.' Someone says: 'Their testimony is not valid, so they are not sane.' Is this valid?
A) Yes, modus tollens
B) No, because invalid testimony could result from other reasons
C) Yes, affirming the antecedent
D) No, it's affirming the consequent

Key Takeaway
Affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are fallacies because the consequent can be broader and have multiple causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the conditional: 'If a person has broken their fast intentionally, their fast is invalid.' Suppose you observe that a person's fast is invalid. Can you conclude they broke it intentionally? Why or why not? Use the concept of 'consequent being more general'.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Condition of Luzumiyya: Implicative Connection Required
Author clarifies that only luzumiyya conditionals are meant and discusses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between luzumiyya (implicative) and ittifaqiyya (accidental) conditionals and explain why only the former yields valid syllogisms.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism is only valid if the connection is necessary (luzumiyya), not coincidental (ittifaqiyya).

The passage explicitly states that the chapter intends only luzumiyya conditionals. An objection from al-Shaykh about the number example illustrates that even seemingly implicative conditionals may be accidental. This lesson clarifies the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of luzumiyya
Luzumiyya means the consequent follows necessarily from the antecedent, e.g., 'If it rained, the ground is wet' (by natural causation).
2
Understand ittifaqiyya (accidental)
Ittifaqiyya means the connection is coincidental, e.g., 'If Zayd is in the house, then it is daytime' – no necessary link.
Such conditionals yield no valid inference.
3
Analyze the objection by al-Shaykh
Al-Shaykh gives: 'If two is odd, it is a number; if it is a number, it is even' – the conclusion is false. The response: the minor premise ('if it is a number, it is even') is accidental (ittifaqiyya) because being a number does not necessarily imply being even.
This shows the need to check real relationship (munasaba).
4
Apply the condition to syllogisms
When constructing a conditional syllogism, ensure each conditional is luzumiyya. Otherwise, the syllogism yields no valid conclusion or an accidental one.
The text says: if there is no relationship, the syllogism cannot be composed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
Which of the following conditionals is luzumiyya (implicative)?
A) 'If Zayd is in the house, then it is daytime'
B) 'If a person is a scholar, then they are knowledgeable'
C) 'If the cat is on the mat, then it is raining'
D) 'If the door is closed, then the room is dark'

Key Takeaway
Only conditionals with a necessary connection (luzumiyya) can serve as premises in a valid conditional syllogism; accidental conditionals yield no logical conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following two conditional statements: (a) 'If the prayer is valid, then the person performed wudu.' (b) 'If the person performed wudu, then their clothes are clean.' Determine whether each conditional is luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya. If both were luzumiyya, would the conclusion 'If the prayer is valid, then their clothes are clean' follow necessarily? Why or why not?

Types of Disjunctive Conditionals and Their Valid Inferences
Author classifies disjunctive conditionals and explains valid inferences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a disjunctive conditional as exclusive (man'at al-jam'), inclusive (man'at al-khuluw), or true (haqiqiyya), and determine which of the four inference forms (affirming one/denying the other, etc.) are valid for each type.

Core Idea
The three types of disjunction – exclusive, inclusive, and true – each permit different patterns of valid inference.

In this passage, the author classifies disjunctive conditionals and explains which inferences are valid for each type. This is crucial for correctly applying disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic reasoning, such as determining legal categories or theological positions. We will walk through the definitions and examples to master these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive proposition
Locate the disjunctive conditional in the Arabic, usually signaled by 'إما' or 'أو'. Note the two parts (الجزئان). For example, 'إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجا أو فردا' (either this number is even or odd).
2
Determine the relationship between the parts
Check whether the parts can both be true (منع الجمع) and/or both be false (منع الخلو). If they cannot both be true, it is man'at al-jam' (exclusive). If they cannot both be false, it is man'at al-khuluw (inclusive). If both, it is haqiqiyya (true). The passage gives examples: tree/stone (exclusive), not-tree/not-stone (inclusive), even/odd (true).
For a fiqh example, 'This act is either wajib or haram' is haqiqiyya because it cannot be both nor neither.
3
Apply the valid inference patterns
For exclusive: affirming one part entails denying the other; denying one does not affirm the other. For inclusive: denying one entails affirming the other; affirming one does not deny the other. For true: all four forms (affirm one/deny the other, or deny one/affirm the other) are valid. Memorize: for haqiqiyya, 'في الصور الأربع النتائج الأربع'.
The passage shows: from 'it is even' you conclude 'it is not odd'; from 'it is not even' you conclude 'it is odd', etc.
4
Test the inference with an Islamic example
Take a real fiqh or kalam disjunction, e.g., 'The world is either temporally originated (hadith) or eternal (qadim).' Since a world cannot be both hadith and qadim, nor neither (in kalam), it is haqiqiyya. Given 'it is hadith', conclude 'it is not qadim'; given 'it is not hadith', conclude 'it is qadim'. Check which type allows which inference.

Source Text

التصديقات ١٨٨ قياس الخلف. أن المراد بالمنفصلة ههنا العنادية وإن كانت الشرطية منفصلة فمانعة الجمع ينتج من وضع كل جزء رفع الآخر؛ لامتناع اجتماعهما ولا ينتج من رفع كل وضع الآخر؛ لعدم امتناع الخلو عنهما ومانعة الخلو بالعكس. وأما الحقيقية فلما اشتملت على منع الجمع والخلو معاً ينتج في الصور الأربع النتائج الأربع. قوله: وضع المقدم ورفع التالي: نحو إن كان هذا إنسانا كان حيوانا لكنه إنسان فهو حيوان ولكنه ليس بحيوان فليس بإنسان. قوله ومن الحقيقية كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجا أو فردا لكنه زوج فليس بفرد لكنه فرد فليس بزوج لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج لكنه ليس بزوج فهو فرد. قوله كمانعة الجمع نحو: هذا إما شجر أو حجر لكنه شجر فليس بحجر فليس بشجر. قوله كمانعة الخلو: نحو هذا إما لا شجر أو لا حجر لكنه ليس بلا شجر فهو لا حجر لكنه ليس بلا حجر فهو لا شجر. قوله: وقد يختص إلخ: اعلم أنه قد يستدل على إثبات المدعى بأنه لولاه لصدق نقيضه؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين لكن نقيضه غير واقع فيكون هو واقعا كما مر غير مرة في مباحث العكوس والأقيسة. وهذا القسم من الاستدلال بالخلف يسمى لا حجر لكنه حجر على منع الجمع والخلو معاً: يعني أنها باعتبار اشتمالها على منع الجمع ينتج من وضع المقدم رفع التالي ومن وضع التالي رفع المقدم وباعتبار اشتمالها على منع الخلو ينتج من رفع المقدم وضع التالي ومن رفع التالي وضع المقدم. (جمال) النتائج الأربع وضع كل من المقدم والتالي رفع الآخر ورفع كل منهما وضع الآخر كما في المثال المذكور في الشرح؛ فإن قوله: لكنه زوج وضع المقدم فالنتيجة رفع التالي يعني قوله: ليس بفرد، وأيضاً قوله: لكنه فرد وضع التالي فالنتيجة رفع المقدم يعني قوله ليس بزوج وأيضاً قوله لكنه ليس بفرد رفع التالي فالنتيجة وضع المقدم يعني قوله فهو زوج، وقوله لكنه ليس بزوج رفع المقدم فالنتيجة وضع التالي يعني قوله: 'فهو فرد' وهكذا في مثال مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو. (برهان الدين)
The affirmations (tasdiqat) 188: Proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf). That the intended meaning of 'disjunctive' here is the oppositional (؟), even though the conditional is disjunctive. So the exclusive disjunctive (man'at al-jam') produces from the affirmation of each part the denial of the other, because of the impossibility of their coexistence, and does not produce from the denial of each the affirmation of the other, because of the lack of impossibility of their being both absent. And the inclusive disjunctive (man'at al-khuluw) is the opposite. As for the true disjunctive (al-haqiqiyya), because it includes both prevention of coexistence and prevention of absence together, it produces in the four forms the four results. His statement: 'affirmation of the antecedent and denial of the consequent'— e.g., if this is a human then it is an animal, but it is a human, so it is an animal, and if it is not an animal, then it is not a human. His statement: 'and from the true disjunctive like our saying: either this number is even or odd, but it is even, so it is not odd; but it is odd, so it is not even; but it is not odd, so it is even; but it is not even, so it is odd.' His statement: 'like the exclusive disjunctive (man'at al-jam')' e.g., this is either a tree or a stone, but it is a tree, so it is not a stone; but it is not a tree, so it is a stone. His statement: 'like the inclusive disjunctive (man'at al-khuluw)' e.g., this is either not a tree or not a stone, but it is not not a tree (i.e., it is a tree), so it is not a stone (i.e., it is a stone?); but it is not not a stone, so it is not a tree. His statement: 'and it may be specifically called...' Know that one may infer to establish the claim that were it not for it, its contradictory would be true, because of the impossibility of both contradictories being false, but its contradictory does not occur, so it occurs, as has passed many times in the investigations of conversions and syllogisms. And this type of inference by contradiction is called 'not a stone but it is a stone' according to the prevention of coexistence and absence together: meaning that by virtue of including prevention of coexistence, it produces from affirmation of the antecedent denial of the consequent and from affirmation of the consequent denial of the antecedent, and by virtue of including prevention of absence, it produces from denial of the antecedent affirmation of the consequent and from denial of the consequent affirmation of the antecedent. (Jamal) The four results: affirmation of each of the antecedent and consequent leads to denial of the other, and denial of each leads to affirmation of the other, as in the example mentioned in the commentary; for his statement 'but it is even' affirms the antecedent, so the result is denial of the consequent, meaning his statement 'it is not odd'; and also his statement 'but it is odd' affirms the consequent, so the result is denial of the antecedent, meaning his statement 'it is not even'; and his statement 'but it is not odd' denies the consequent, so the result is affirmation of the antecedent, meaning his statement 'so it is even'; and his statement 'but it is not even' denies the antecedent, so the result is affirmation of the consequent, meaning his statement 'so it is odd'. And similarly in the example of the exclusive and inclusive disjunctives. (Burhan al-Din)
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage classifies disjunctive conditionals (munfasila) and explains their valid inferences. It first states that the disjunctive intended here is the 'oppositional' (؟) type. It then distinguishes three types: (1) man'at al-jam' (exclusive disjunction), where the parts cannot both be true; affirming one part yields denial of the other, but denying one does not affirm the other because both could be false. (2) man'at al-khuluw (inclusive disjunction), where the parts cannot both be false; denying one yields affirmation of the other, but affirming one does not deny the other because both could be true. (3) al-haqiqiyya (true disjunction), which combines both restrictions (exactly one true); from any of the four forms (affirm antecedent, affirm consequent, deny antecedent, deny consequent) the opposite follows. Concrete examples are provided: for the true disjunctive, the number example (even/odd) illustrates all four results. For man'at al-jam', the example 'either tree or stone' is given, and for man'at al-khuluw, the example 'either not-tree or not-stone'. The passage then introduces qiyas al-khalf (proof by contradiction) as a special type of inference that establishes a claim by showing its contradictory leads to a falsehood. The commentary (Jamal and Burhan al-Din) further explicates the four results for the true disjunctive with the number example, showing how each premise position yields the corresponding conclusion.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A faqih states: 'Either this transaction is valid (sahih) or it is void (batil).' He then argues: 'It is not valid; therefore it is void.' Which type of disjunction is presupposed for this argument to be logically valid?
A) Exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam') only
B) Inclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw) only
C) True disjunction (haqiqiyya) only
D) Both inclusive and true disjunction

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunction – exclusive, inclusive, and true – each have distinct valid inference patterns; true disjunction allows all four forms of affirmation/denial to yield valid conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following disjunction from kalam: 'The world is either temporally originated (hadith) or eternal (qadim).' Determine which type it is (exclusive, inclusive, or true) and list all valid inferences that follow from affirming or denying each part.
?
A mufti argues: 'This food is either halal or haram. It is not halal, therefore it is haram.' Assuming the disjunction is haqiqiyya, is the argument valid? If the disjunction were exclusive, would it still be valid? Explain.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Proof by Contradiction (Qiyas al-Khalf)
Author explains proof by contradiction as a special case of disjunctive inference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a proof by contradiction based on the impossibility of both contradictories being false, and apply it to a theological or legal example.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction establishes a claim by showing its negation leads to an absurdity.

The author introduces qiyas al-khalf as a special inference method. It relies on the logical principle that a proposition and its negation cannot both be false. We will see how this method is used to prove a claim by showing that its contradictory leads to a falsehood. This tool is widely used in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proved (al-mudda'a)
Find the statement that the argument aims to establish. For example, 'The world is created' or 'This act is obligatory'. In the passage, the author refers to 'إثبات المدعى' (establishing the claim).
2
Assume the opposite (the contradictory)
The argument begins by assuming the negation of the claim. This is the 'khalf' (counterfactual). The text says: 'لولاه لصدق نقيضه' – were it not for the claim, its contradictory would be true.
3
Show that the assumption leads to a falsehood (batil)
From the assumption, derive a contradiction or an absurd consequence. This shows that the assumption cannot be true. The author says: 'لكن نقيضه غير واقع' – but its contradictory does not occur (i.e., is false).
4
Conclude that the original claim is true
Since the opposite is false and both cannot be false (due to 'استحالة ارتفاع النقيضين'), the original must be true. This is the conclusion of the proof by contradiction.
This principle is equivalent to an inclusive disjunction: the claim and its negation cannot both be false. Denying the negation affirms the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
A mutakallim wants to prove that 'the world is created' by contradiction. Which of the following best represents the structure of his proof?
A) Assume the world is created, then derive a contradiction.
B) Assume the world is eternal, then derive a contradiction.
C) Assume the world is eternal, then show it is impossible.
D) Show that 'the world is eternal' and 'the world is created' cannot both be false.

Key Takeaway
Proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) establishes a claim by showing its negation is false, relying on the principle that a claim and its negation cannot both be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a proof by contradiction for the statement 'The Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) is truthful'. Assume he would not be truthful, then show that this leads to a logical absurdity (e.g., the Quran being false, etc.) using a plausible premise.
?
A mutakallim argues: 'If the world is eternal, it would be impossible for it to have a beginning, but we observe it has a beginning; therefore the world is not eternal.' Is this a valid proof by contradiction? Analyse its structure.

The Syllogism

Definition and Purpose of Qiyas al-Khalf
Defines qiyas al-khalf and its logical structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify qiyas al-khalf and explain its purpose of proving a proposition by refuting its opposite.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves a claim by showing its denial leads to absurdity.

In this passage, the author defines 'qiyas al-khalf' (argument by contradiction), a powerful tool in Islamic logic used to establish a desired conclusion indirectly. Instead of proving the conclusion directly, you assume its opposite and derive an absurdity, thereby confirming the original. Understanding this method is essential for engaging with complex theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of qiyas al-khalf
Find where the author explicitly defines it. In the beginning: 'قياس الخلف: وضع المقدم ورفع التالي...' and later 'وقد يختص باسم قياس الخلف، وهو ما يقصد به إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه'. Mark that passage.
The definition includes both the structure and the purpose.
2
Extract the essential purpose
The purpose is 'إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه' (establishing the desired by refuting its opposite). This is the core of qiyas al-khalf.
3
Note the key structural claim
The author states that qiyas al-khalf is not a single syllogism but resolves into two: a conditional conjunctive and a connected hypothetical. This will be explored in later lessons.
This claim is crucial for understanding its logical composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قياس الخلف: وضع المقدم ورفع التالي ومن الحقيقية وضع كل كمانعة الجمع ورفعه كمانعة الخلو، وقد يختص باسم قياس الخلف، وهو ما يقصد به إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه أن المراد بالمنفصلة ههنا العنادية وإن كانت الشرطية منفصلة فمانعة الجمع ينتج من وضع كل جزء رفع الآخر؛ لامتناع اجتماعهما ولا ينتج من رفع كل وضع الآخر؛ لعدم امتناع الخلو عنهما ومانعة الخلو بالعكس. وأما الحقيقية فلما اشتملت على منع الجمع والخلو معاً ينتج في الصور الأربع النتائج الأربع. قوله : وضع المقدم ورفع التالي: نحو إن كان هذا إنسانا كان حيواناً لكنه إنسان فهو حيوان ولكنه ليس بحيوان فليس بإنسان قوله ومن الحقيقية كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجا أو فردا لكنه زوج فليس بفرد لكنه فرد فليس بزوج لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج لكنه ليس بزوج فهو فرد قوله كمانعة الجمع نحو: هذا إما شجر أو حجر لكنه شجر فليس بحجر فليس بشجر. قوله كمانعة الخلو: نحو هذا إما لا شجر أو لا حجر لكنه ليس بلا شجر فهو لكنه ليس بلا حجر فهو لا شجر قوله : وقد يختص إلخ : اعلم أنه قد يستدل على إثبات المدعى بأنه لولاه لصدق نقيضه؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين لكن نقيضه غير واقع فيكون هو واقعاً كما مر غير مرة في مباحث العكوس والأقيسة. وهذا القسم من الاستدلال بالخلف يسمى لا حجر لكنه حجر على منع الجمع والخلو معاً: يعني أنها باعتبار اشتمالها على منع الجمع ينتج من وضع المقدم رفع التالي ومن وضع التالي رفع المقدم وباعتبار اشتمالها على منع الخلو ينتج من رفع المقدم وضع التالي ومن رفع التالي وضع المقدم. (جمال) النتائج الأربع وضع كل من المقدم والتالي رفع الآخر ورفع كل منهما وضع الآخر كما في المثال المذكور في الشرح؛ فإن قوله: لكنه زوج وضع المقدم فالنتيجة رفع التالي يعني قوله : ليس بفرد، وأيضاً قوله: لكنه فرد وضع التالي فالنتيجة رفع المقدم يعني قوله ليس بزوج وأيضاً قوله لكنه ليس بفرد رفع التالي فالنتيجة وضع المقدم يعني قوله فهو زوج، وقوله لكنه ليس بزوج رفع المقدم فالنتيجة وضع التالي يعني قوله: "فهو فرد" وهكذا في مثال مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو. (برهان الدين) ومرجعه إلى استثنائي واقتراني. إما لأنه ينجر إلى الخلف أي المحال على تقدير صدق نقيض المطلوب أو لأنه ينتقل منه إلى المطلوب خلفه أي من ورائه الذي هو نقيضه، وهذا ليس قياساً واحداً؛ بل ينحل إلى قياسين: أحدهما: من اقتراني شرطي، والآخر استثنائي متصل يستثنى فيه نقيض التالي، هكذا لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت نقيض، وكلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال ينتج: لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت المحال لكن المحال ليس بثابت، فيلزم ثبوت المطلوب؛ لكونه نقيض المقدم، ثم قد يفتقر بيان الشرطية يعني قولنا: كلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال إلى دليل، فتكثر القياسات.
Qiyas al-Khalf: positing the antecedent and negating the consequent, and from the real exclusive-disjunctive, positing each as in mutually-preventive and negating it as in co-vacuous. And it may be specifically named 'qiyas al-khalf', which is that by which one intends to establish the desired proposition by refuting its opposite. That the intended meaning of the disjunctive here is the adversative, even if the conditional is disjunctive. So the mutually-preventive yields from positing each part the negation of the other, due to the impossibility of their conjunction, and it does not yield from negating each the positing of the other, due to the lack of impossibility of their vacuity. And the co-vacuous is the opposite. As for the real [exclusive-disjunctive], since it includes both prevention of conjunction and vacuity together, it yields in the four forms the four results. His statement: 'positing the antecedent and negating the consequent', e.g., 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is a human, so it is an animal; and but it is not an animal, so it is not a human.' His statement: 'and from the real', e.g., our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd; but it is even, so it is not odd; but it is odd, so it is not even; but it is not odd, so it is even; but it is not even, so it is odd.' His statement: 'as in mutually-preventive', e.g., 'This is either a tree or a stone; but it is a tree, so it is not a stone; but it is not a tree, so it is a stone.' His statement: 'as in co-vacuous', e.g., 'This is either not a tree or not a stone; but it is not not a tree, so it is not a stone; but it is not not a stone, so it is not a tree.' His statement: 'and it may be specifically [named] etc.': Know that one may argue for establishing the claim by [showing that] were it not for it, its opposite would be true, due to the impossibility of the two opposites being raised; but its opposite does not occur, so it is the one that occurs, as has passed multiple times in the discussions of inverses and syllogisms. This section of argument by khalf is called... 'but it is a stone' [example] on prevention of conjunction and vacuity together: meaning that, by virtue of its inclusion of prevention of conjunction, it yields from positing the antecedent the negation of the consequent and from positing the consequent the negation of the antecedent; and by virtue of its inclusion of prevention of vacuity, it yields from negating the antecedent the positing of the consequent and from negating the consequent the positing of the antecedent. (Jamal) The four results: positing each of the antecedent and consequent yields the negation of the other, and negating each yields the positing of the other, as in the example mentioned in the commentary; for his statement: 'but it is even' is positing the antecedent, so the result is negating the consequent, meaning his statement: 'it is not odd'; and also his statement: 'but it is odd' is positing the consequent, so the result is negating the antecedent, meaning his statement: 'it is not even'; and his statement: 'but it is not odd' is negating the consequent, so the result is positing the antecedent, meaning his statement: 'so it is even'; and his statement: 'but it is not even' is negating the antecedent, so the result is positing the consequent, meaning his statement: 'so it is odd'. And similarly in the example of mutually-preventive and co-vacuous. (Burhan al-Din) And its reference is to hypothetical-syllogism and conjunctive-syllogism. Either because it leads to khalf, i.e., absurdity, on the assumption of the truth of the opposite of the desired proposition, or because one moves from it to the desired proposition behind it, i.e., from its rear which is its opposite. And this is not a single syllogism; rather, it resolves into two syllogisms: one of them is a conditional conjunctive, and the other is a connected hypothetical in which the opposite of the consequent is excepted. Thus: if the desired proposition were not established, its opposite would be established; and whenever its opposite is established, absurdity is established. This yields: if the desired proposition were not established, absurdity would be established; but absurdity is not established; so the establishment of the desired proposition follows, because it is the opposite of the antecedent. Then explaining the conditional, i.e., our saying: 'whenever its opposite is established, absurdity is established', may require a proof, so the syllogisms multiply.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines 'Qiyas al-Khalf' (argument by contradiction) and explains its formal structure. The author begins by giving the general definition: it is a method of establishing a desired proposition by refuting its opposite, using either a conditional (muttasil) or a disjunctive (munfasil) premise. He then distinguishes three types of disjunctives: mutually-preventive (mani'at al-jam'), co-vacuous (mani'at al-khuluw), and the real exclusive-disjunctive (haqiqiyya) which combines both. For each, he describes the valid inferences: from positing one part to negating the other, and vice versa. Examples are provided: for conditional, 'If it is human, it is animal'; for the real disjunctive, 'Either even or odd'; for mutually-preventive, 'Either tree or stone'; for co-vacuous, 'Either not tree or not stone'. The marginal gloss by Jamal clarifies the four inference patterns using the even/odd example. Then the text explains the crucial point: qiyas al-khalf is not a single syllogism but reduces to two—a conditional conjunctive and a connected hypothetical where the opposite of the consequent is excepted. The reasoning is: if the claim were false, its opposite would be true, and that opposite leads to absurdity; since absurdity is impossible, the original claim must be true. The author notes that the conditional premise (that the opposite leads to absurdity) may itself require proof, leading to a chain of syllogisms. The marginal notes by Burhan al-Din and Ismail discuss the vocalization of 'khalf' (with fatha or damma) and its meanings. Overall, the passage provides a comprehensive treatment of argument by contradiction, its logical components, and its resolution into simpler syllogistic forms.

خلف

B) To prove a proposition by refuting its opposite.
C) To prove a proposition by analogy.
D) To prove a proposition by induction.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf establishes a proposition by showing that its negation leads to an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Types of Disjunctives in Qiyas al-Khalf
Explains types of disjunctives and their inference rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive propositions used in qiyas al-khalf and determine the valid inferences for each.

Core Idea
The real exclusive-disjunctive yields all four inferences, while the others only yield two.

The passage explains three types of disjunctives that can be used in qiyas al-khalf: mutually-preventive (mani'at al-jam'), co-vacuous (mani'at al-khuluw), and the real exclusive-disjunctive (haqiqiyya). Each has specific inference patterns. Understanding these is critical for constructing valid arguments by contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of disjunctives
Locate the Arabic terms: مانعة الجمع (mutually-preventive), مانعة الخلو (co-vacuous), and حقيقية (real exclusive-disjunctive). They are introduced early in the passage.
These are types of disjunctive premises.
2
Extract the inference pattern for each type
For مانعة الجمع: from positing one part, negate the other; not from negating one to posit the other. For مانعة الخلو: vice versa. For الحقيقية: both positive and negative inferences work, giving four results.
The passage states: 'مانعة الجمع ينتج من وضع كل جزء رفع الآخر... ومانعة الخلو بالعكس. وأما الحقيقية... ينتج في الصور الأربع النتائج الأربع.'
3
Apply the pattern to the examples
The author gives examples: tree/stone for مانعة الجمع, not tree/not stone for مانعة الخلو, even/odd for الحقيقية. Verify that the inferences follow the rules.
Work through the examples in the translation to see the four cases for الحقيقية.

Commentary
This passage defines 'Qiyas al-Khalf' (argument by contradiction) and explains its formal structure. The author begins by giving the general definition: it is a method of establishing a desired proposition by refuting its opposite, using either a conditional (muttasil) or a disjunctive (munfasil) premise. He then distinguishes three types of disjunctives: mutually-preventive (mani'at al-jam'), co-vacuous (mani'at al-khuluw), and the real exclusive-disjunctive (haqiqiyya) which combines both. Examples are provided: for conditional, 'If it is human, it is animal'; for the real disjunctive, 'Either even or odd'; for mutually-preventive, 'Either tree or stone'; for co-vacuous, 'Either not tree or not stone'. The marginal gloss by Jamal clarifies the four inference patterns using the even/odd example. Then the text explains the crucial point: qiyas al-khalf is not a single syllogism but reduces to two—a conditional conjunctive and a connected hypothetical where the opposite of the consequent is excepted. The reasoning is: if the claim were false, its opposite would be true, and that opposite leads to absurdity; since absurdity is impossible, the original claim must be true. The author notes that the conditional premise (that the opposite leads to absurdity) may itself require proof, leading to a chain of syllogisms. The marginal notes by Burhan al-Din and Ismail discuss the vocalization of 'khalf' (with fatha or damma) and its meanings. Overall, the passage provides a comprehensive treatment of argument by contradiction, its logical components, and its resolution into simpler syllogistic forms.

The Syllogism

Reduction of Qiyas al-Khalf to Two Syllogisms
Shows decomposition of qiyas al-khalf into two syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct how qiyas al-khalf resolves into a conditional conjunctive and a connected hypothetical syllogism.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf is not a single syllogism but a compound of two: a conditional conjunctive and a connected hypothetical.

The passage makes a key structural claim: qiyas al-khalf is not a single argument but decomposes into two simpler syllogisms. The first is a conditional conjunctive (اقتراني شرطي) and the second is a connected hypothetical (استثنائي متصل). Understanding this decomposition is essential for analyzing the logical form of indirect proofs.

Guided Path
1
Locate the statement of reduction
Find where the author says 'ومرجعه إلى استثنائي واقتراني' and 'ينحل إلى قياسين'. This is near the end of the passage.
The marginal note by Burhan al-Din is key.
2
Identify the two syllogisms
The first is a conditional conjunctive that establishes: if the opposite of the desired proposition were true, then absurdity follows. The second is a connected hypothetical that excepts the consequent (absurdity is false) to conclude the desired proposition.
The text gives the formal version: 'لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت نقيض، وكلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال ينتج: لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت المحال لكن المحال ليس بثابت، فيلزم ثبوت المطلوب'.
3
Trace the reasoning with an example
Use the even/odd example from earlier. Suppose the desired proposition is 'the number is even'. Its opposite is 'the number is odd'. Show that if it were odd, absurdity follows. Then the second syllogism: 'If odd, then absurd; but absurd is false; so not odd, i.e., even.'
This demonstrates the reduction concretely.
4
Note the optional expansion
The author notes that the conditional premise (that the opposite leads to absurdity) may itself require proof, leading to a chain of syllogisms.
This shows the complexity can increase.

قياس الخلف: وضع المقدم ورفع التالي ومن الحقيقية وضع كل كمانعة الجمع ورفعه كمانعة الخلو، وقد يختص باسم قياس الخلف، وهو ما يقصد به إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه أن المراد بالمنفصلة ههنا العنادية وإن كانت الشرطية منفصلة فمانعة الجمع ينتج من وضع كل جزء رفع الآخر؛ لامتناع اجتماعهما ولا ينتج من رفع كل وضع الآخر؛ لعدم امتناع الخلو عنهما ومانعة الخلو بالعكس. وأما الحقيقية فلما اشتملت على منع الجمع والخلو معاً ينتج في الصور الأربع النتائج الأربع. قوله : وضع المقدم ورفع التالي: نحو إن كان هذا إنسانا كان حيواناً لكنه إنسان فهو حيوان ولكنه ليس بحيوان فليس بإنسان قوله ومن الحقيقية كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجا أو فردا لكنه زوج فليس بفرد لكنه فرد فليس بزوج لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج لكنه ليس بزوج فهو فرد قوله كمانعة الجمع نحو: هذا إما شجر أو حجر لكنه شجر فليس بحجر فليس بشجر. قوله كمانعة الخلو: نحو هذا إما لا شجر أو لا حجر لكنه ليس بلا شجر فهو لكنه ليس بلا حجر فهو لا شجر قوله : وقد يختص إلخ : اعلم أنه قد يستدل على إثبات المدعى بأنه لولاه لصدق نقيضه؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين لكن نقيضه غير واقع فيكون هو واقعاً كما مر غير مرة في مباحث العكوس والأقيسة. وهذا القسم من الاستدلال بالخلف يسمى لا حجر لكنه حجر على منع الجمع والخلو معاً: يعني أنها باعتبار اشتمالها على منع الجمع ينتج من وضع المقدم رفع التالي ومن وضع التالي رفع المقدم وباعتبار اشتمالها على منع الخلو ينتج من رفع المقدم وضع التالي ومن رفع التالي وضع المقدم. (جمال) النتائج الأربع وضع كل من المقدم والتالي رفع الآخر ورفع كل منهما وضع الآخر كما في المثال المذكور في الشرح؛ فإن قوله: لكنه زوج وضع المقدم فالنتيجة رفع التالي يعني قوله : ليس بفرد، وأيضاً قوله: لكنه فرد وضع التالي فالنتيجة رفع المقدم يعني قوله ليس بزوج وأيضاً قوله لكنه ليس بفرد رفع التالي فالنتيجة وضع المقدم يعني قوله فهو زوج، وقوله لكنه ليس بزوج رفع المقدم فالنتيجة وضع التالي يعني قوله: "فهو فرد" وهكذا في مثال مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو. (برهان الدين) ومرجعه إلى استثنائي واقتراني. إما لأنه ينجر إلى الخلف أي المحال على تقدير صدق نقيض المطلوب أو لأنه ينتقل منه إلى المطلوب خلفه أي من ورائه الذي هو نقيضه، وهذا ليس قياساً واحداً؛ بل ينحل إلى قياسين: أحدهما: من اقتراني شرطي، والآخر استثنائي متصل يستثنى فيه نقيض التالي، هكذا لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت نقيض، وكلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال ينتج: لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت المحال لكن المحال ليس بثابت، فيلزم ثبوت المطلوب؛ لكونه نقيض المقدم، ثم قد يفتقر بيان الشرطية يعني قولنا: كلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال إلى دليل، فتكثر القياسات.
Qiyas al-Khalf: positing the antecedent and negating the consequent, and from the real exclusive-disjunctive, positing each as in mutually-preventive and negating it as in co-vacuous. And it may be specifically named 'qiyas al-khalf', which is that by which one intends to establish the desired proposition by refuting its opposite. That the intended meaning of the disjunctive here is the adversative, even if the conditional is disjunctive. So the mutually-preventive yields from positing each part the negation of the other, due to the impossibility of their conjunction, and it does not yield from negating each the positing of the other, due to the lack of impossibility of their vacuity. And the co-vacuous is the opposite. As for the real [exclusive-disjunctive], since it includes both prevention of conjunction and vacuity together, it yields in the four forms the four results. His statement: 'positing the antecedent and negating the consequent', e.g., 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is a human, so it is an animal; and but it is not an animal, so it is not a human.' His statement: 'and from the real', e.g., our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd; but it is even, so it is not odd; but it is odd, so it is not even; but it is not odd, so it is even; but it is not even, so it is odd.' His statement: 'as in mutually-preventive', e.g., 'This is either a tree or a stone; but it is a tree, so it is not a stone; but it is not a tree, so it is a stone.' His statement: 'as in co-vacuous', e.g., 'This is either not a tree or not a stone; but it is not not a tree, so it is not a stone; but it is not not a stone, so it is not a tree.' His statement: 'and it may be specifically [named] etc.': Know that one may argue for establishing the claim by [showing that] were it not for it, its opposite would be true, due to the impossibility of the two opposites being raised; but its opposite does not occur, so it is the one that occurs, as has passed multiple times in the discussions of inverses and syllogisms. This section of argument by khalf is called... 'but it is a stone' [example] on prevention of conjunction and vacuity together: meaning that, by virtue of its inclusion of prevention of conjunction, it yields from positing the antecedent the negation of the consequent and from positing the consequent the negation of the antecedent; and by virtue of its inclusion of prevention of vacuity, it yields from negating the antecedent the positing of the consequent and from negating the consequent the positing of the antecedent. (Jamal) The four results: positing each of the antecedent and consequent yields the negation of the other, and negating each yields the positing of the other, as in the example mentioned in the commentary; for his statement: 'but it is even' is positing the antecedent, so the result is negating the consequent, meaning his statement: 'it is not odd'; and also his statement: 'but it is odd' is positing the consequent, so the result is negating the antecedent, meaning his statement: 'it is not even'; and his statement: 'but it is not odd' is negating the consequent, so the result is positing the antecedent, meaning his statement: 'so it is even'; and his statement: 'but it is not even' is negating the antecedent, so the result is positing the consequent, meaning his statement: 'so it is odd'. And similarly in the example of mutually-preventive and co-vacuous. (Burhan al-Din) And its reference is to hypothetical-syllogism and conjunctive-syllogism. Either because it leads to khalf, i.e., absurdity, on the assumption of the truth of the opposite of the desired proposition, or because one moves from it to the desired proposition behind it, i.e., from its rear which is its opposite. And this is not a single syllogism; rather, it resolves into two syllogisms: one of them is a conditional conjunctive, and the other is a connected hypothetical in which the opposite of the consequent is excepted. Thus: if the desired proposition were not established, its opposite would be established; and whenever its opposite is established, absurdity is established. This yields: if the desired proposition were not established, absurdity would be established; but absurdity is not established; so the establishment of the desired proposition follows, because it is the opposite of the antecedent. Then explaining the conditional, i.e., our saying: 'whenever its opposite is established, absurdity is established', may require a proof, so the syllogisms multiply.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines 'Qiyas al-Khalf' (argument by contradiction) and explains its formal structure. The author begins by giving the general definition: it is a method of establishing a desired proposition by refuting its opposite, using either a conditional (muttasil) or a disjunctive (munfasil) premise. He then distinguishes three types of disjunctives: mutually-preventive (mani'at al-jam'), co-vacuous (mani'at al-khuluw), and the real exclusive-disjunctive (haqiqiyya) which combines both. For each, he describes the valid inferences: from positing one part to negating the other, and vice versa. Examples are provided: for conditional, 'If it is human, it is animal'; for the real disjunctive, 'Either even or odd'; for mutually-preventive, 'Either tree or stone'; for co-vacuous, 'Either not tree or not stone'. The marginal gloss by Jamal clarifies the four inference patterns using the even/odd example. Then the text explains the crucial point: qiyas al-khalf is not a single syllogism but reduces to two—a conditional conjunctive and a connected hypothetical where the opposite of the consequent is excepted. The reasoning is: if the claim were false, its opposite would be true, and that opposite leads to absurdity; since absurdity is impossible, the original claim must be true. The author notes that the conditional premise (that the opposite leads to absurdity) may itself require proof, leading to a chain of syllogisms. The marginal notes by Burhan al-Din and Ismail discuss the vocalization of 'khalf' (with fatha or damma) and its meanings. Overall, the passage provides a comprehensive treatment of argument by contradiction, its logical components, and its resolution into simpler syllogistic forms.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Qiyas al-Khalf: Structure and Reduction
Author defines qiyas al-khalf and explains its reduction to conditional and categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of qiyas al-khalf and reduce it to a conditional and a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves a claim by showing its negation leads to impossibility.

Qiyas al-khalf (syllogism by contradiction) is a powerful logical tool used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. It does not stand alone but decomposes into a conditional and a categorical syllogism. Understanding this reduction helps you analyze reductio ad absurdum arguments and verify their validity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'khalf'
Note that 'khalf' can mean 'impossible' (with damm) or 'behind' (with fatha). This ambiguity is resolved by context: qiyas al-khalf leads to an impossibility or moves from the contradictory to the conclusion.
The vowel shift is important for correct interpretation.
2
Identify the two-syllogism structure
Qiyas al-khalf is not a single syllogism. It resolves into a conditional categorical (iqtirani sharṭi) and a conjunctive conditional (istithna'i muttasil) that excepts the contradictory of the consequent.
The conditional categorical contains a conditional premise, and the istithna'i uses modus tollens.
3
Trace the argument pattern
Assume the negation of the desired conclusion. Derive an impossibility. Conclude that the original claim holds because its contradictory leads to absurdity. Formally: If not P then Q, and Q leads to impossible, so not P is false, therefore P.
This is the classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Recognize when additional proof is needed
The conditional premise 'whenever the contradictory holds, the impossible holds' may itself require proof. This can lead to a chain of syllogisms.
The author notes that the reduction only states the minimum; more syllogisms may be added.

قياس الخلف
ومرجعه إلى استثنائي واقتراني.
إما لأنه ينجر إلى الخلف أي المحال على تقدير صدق نقيض المطلوب أو لأنه ينتقل منه إلى المطلوب خلفه أي من ورائه الذي هو نقيضه، وهذا ليس قياساً واحداً؛ بل ينحل إلى قياسين: أحدهما: من اقتراني شرطي، والآخر استثنائي متصل يستثنى فيه نقيض التالي، هكذا لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت نقيض، وكلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال ينتج: لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت المحال لكن المحال ليس بثابت، فيلزم ثبوت المطلوب؛ لكونه نقيض المقدم، ثم قد يفتقر بيان الشرطية يعني قولنا: كلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال إلى دليل، فتكثر القياسات. كذا قال المصنف في شرح الأصول، فقوله: ومرجعه
إما لأنه
ينجر إلخ هذا التوجيه ظاهر من كلام الشيخ، والتوجيه الثاني من كلام المحقق الطوسي، والظاهر أن
الخلف على الأول بالضم وعلى الثاني بالفتح كما لا يخفى. (إسماعيل الخلف: بالضم البطلان وبالفتح الوراء. أو لأنه ينتقل منه هذا على تصور الخلف بالفتح وأما إذا ثبت أن الخلف بالضم بمعنى البطلان والوراء أو بالفتح كذلك فالأمر واضح أي من ورائه أي ظهره والوراء في الفارسية "پشت" ونقيض الشيء كأنه وراءه. هذا إذا كان الخلف بفتح الخاء؛ فإن الخلف بالفتح بمعنى الوراء وبالضم المحال والباطل. (برهان) بل ينحل: يعني أن القياس الخلف يكون مؤلفا من اقتراني شرطي من متصلتين ومن استثنائي مشتمل على لزومية في نتيجة الشرطي لاستثناء نقيض التالي. هكذا لو لم يثبت إلخ كما قلنا: لو لم يصدق قولنا: بعض الحيوان إنسان لصدق لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان وكلما صدق هذا ثبت المحمول فينتج: لو لم يصدق بعض الحيوان إنسان ثبت المحال فجعلناه شرطية وقلنا لكن المحال ليس بثابت فالنتيجة بعض الإنسان حيوان صادق وهو المدعي. (برهان) ثم قد يفتقر بيان الشرطية التي وقعت كبرى تصويره إنا ندعى أن السالبة تنعكس كنفسها، أي لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر ينعكس إلى لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان؛ لأنه لو لم يثبت المطلوب أي العكس المذكور لثبت نقيضه أعني بعض الحجر إنسان وكلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال، فلو لم يثبت المطلوب المذكور ثبت المحال لكن المحال ليس بثابت فالمطلوب ثابت لكن الكبرى أعني كلما ثبت المحال نظري فنتيجته إلى قياس آخر بأنا نضم النقيض إلى الأصل ونقول: بعض الحجر إنسان ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر ينتج بعض الحجر ليس بحجر وهو سلب الشيء عن نفسه. ومرجعه إلخ جواب عما قيل: إن مرجع الاستثنائي والاقتراني غير صحيح، وحاصل الجواب: أن غرض المصنف بيان ما لابد منه في كل قياس خلف لا نفي الزيادة على ما ذكر
إلى استثنائي واقتراني معناه أن هذا القدر مما لابد منه في كل قياس خلف، وقد يزيد عليه، فافهم.
Qiyas al-Khalf and its reduction to a conditional and a categorical. Either because it leads to absurdity i.e. the impossible on the assumption of the truth of the contradictory of the desired conclusion, or because one moves from it to the desired conclusion behind it i.e. from its rear which is its contradictory. And this is not a single syllogism; rather it resolves into two syllogisms: one of them is a conditional categorical, and the other is a conjunctive conditional in which the consequent's contradictory is excepted. Thus: if the desired conclusion does not hold, its contradictory holds, and whenever its contradictory holds, the impossible holds, resulting: if the desired conclusion does not hold, the impossible holds, but the impossible does not hold, so the holding of the desired conclusion follows because it is the contradictory of the antecedent. Then the clarification of the conditional, meaning our statement: 'whenever its contradictory holds, the impossible holds' may need a proof, so syllogisms multiply. Thus said the author in Sharh al-Usul. So his statement 'and its reduction'... this explanation is apparent from the words of the Shaykh, and the second explanation is from the words of al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi. And it appears that 'khalf' in the first meaning is with damm and in the second with fath, as is not hidden. (Isma'il: 'khalf' with damm means falsehood, with fath means behind. Or because one moves from it... this is on the understanding of 'khalf' with fath; but if it is established that 'khalf' with damm means falsehood and behind, or with fath likewise, then the matter is clear: i.e. from its rear i.e. its back, and 'behind' in Persian is 'pusht', and the contradictory of a thing is as if behind it. This is when 'khalf' is with fatha of the kha'; for 'khalf' with fatha means behind, and with damm means the impossible and false.) (Burhan) Rather it resolves: meaning that the qiyas al-khalf is composed of a conditional categorical of two conjunctives and a conditional containing a consequentiality in the result of the conditional for excepting the contradictory of the consequent. Thus: if it is not established etc. as we said: if our statement 'some animal is human' is not true, then 'no animal is human' is true, and whenever this is true the predicate is established, resulting: if 'some animal is human' is not true, the impossible is established; so we make it a conditional and say: but the impossible is not established, so the result 'some human is animal' is true, and it is the claim. (Burhan) Then the clarification of the conditional that occurred as the major may need a proof: we claim that the negative converts to itself, i.e. 'no human is stone' converts to 'no stone is human'; because if the desired conclusion i.e. the mentioned conversion is not established, its contradictory i.e. 'some stone is human' is established, and whenever its contradictory is established the impossible is established; so if the mentioned desired conclusion is not established the impossible is established, but the impossible is not established, so the desired conclusion is established. But the major i.e. 'whenever the impossible is established' is theoretical, so its result requires another syllogism: we combine the contradictory with the original and say: 'some stone is human' and 'no human is stone' yields 'some stone is not stone', and that is negating the thing about itself. And its reduction etc. is an answer to what was said: that the reduction to conditional and categorical is incorrect. The summary of the answer is that the author's intention is to state what is necessary in every qiyas al-khalf, not to negate the addition to what was mentioned. 'To conditional and categorical' means that this amount is what is necessary in every qiyas al-khalf, and it may increase beyond it, so understand.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage defines and explains qiyas al-khalf (the syllogism by contradiction/reductio). The author begins by stating that qiyas al-khalf reduces to a conditional (istithna'i) and a categorical (iqtrani) syllogism. Two explanations are given for the term 'khalf': it either leads to absurdity (the impossible) or it moves from the contradictory to the desired conclusion. Crucially, the author clarifies that this is not a single syllogism but decomposes into two: a conditional categorical and a conjunctive conditional that excepts the consequent's contradictory. An example is given: if the desired conclusion 'some animal is human' is not true, then 'no animal is human' is true, leading to an impossibility; thus the original must be true. The author also discusses how the conditional premise may itself require proof, leading to additional syllogisms. The passage then examines objections and further clarifies that the reduction is not meant to exclude possible additional elements but to identify the core structure. Finally, it is noted that the word 'khalf' has two meanings depending on vowelization: with damm (khulf) meaning falsehood, with fath (khalaf) meaning behind or rear, and this ambiguity is resolved in the commentary.

Key Terms
قياس الخلف (qiyas al-khalf)
استثنائي (istithna'i)
اقتراني (iqtirani)
خلف (khalf/khulf)

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is a compound syllogism that reduces to a conditional and a categorical, proving a claim by deriving an impossibility from its negation.

The Five Arts of Syllogism Based on Matter
Classifies qiyas into five arts based on material effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any syllogism into one of the five arts (demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, sophistry) based on the epistemic effect of its premises.

Core Idea
The five arts are classes of syllogism defined by how premises affect the mind: certainty, opinion, imagination, or deception.

This passage expands on how qiyas is divided not only by form but also by matter into five arts. Understanding this classification helps you identify whether an argument is intended to produce certain knowledge, persuasive opinion, imaginative imagery, or false conviction. The commentary provides a systematic key: first, check if the premises produce assent (tasdiq) or imagination (takhayyul); then, within assent, distinguish certainty from opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the effect of the premises
Does the syllogism produce assent (tasdiq) or imagination (takhayyul)? If the latter, it belongs to poetry (shi'r). If the former, proceed.
Assent is belief in the truth of a proposition; imagination is a mental image without belief.
2
If assent, determine certainty vs opinion
Does the syllogism yield certainty (jazzm) or opinion (zann)? Certainty is indubitable; opinion is preponderant belief.
Certainty does not admit of the opposite.
3
If certain, check for certainty type
If the premises are certain and true, it is demonstration (burhan). If not certain but accepted by the public or conceded by the opponent, it is dialectic (jadal). Otherwise, it is sophistry (mughalata).
Demonstration yields yaqini knowledge; dialectic forces concession; sophistry deceives.
4
If opinion, assign to rhetoric
Any syllogism that yields opinion (not certainty) and is not imaginal belongs to rhetoric (khataba).
Rhetoric aims at persuasion.
5
Assign imagination to poetry
If the premises produce imagination (takhayyul) rather than assent, the syllogism is poetry (shi'r).
Poetic premises are often figurative, e.g., 'Wine is a flowing ruby.'

فصل
قوله: القياس إلخ : القياس كما ينقسم باعتبار الهيئة والصورة إلى الاستثنائي والاقتراني بأقسامها فكذلك ينقسم باعتبار المادة إلى الصناعات الخمس أعني البرهان والجدل والخطابة والشعر والمغالطة، وقد يسمى سفسطة أيضاً؛ لأن مقدماته إما أن تفيد تصديقا أو تأثيرا آخر غير التصديق أعني التخييل، والثاني الشعر والأول إما أن يفيد ظنا أو جزما، فالأول الخطابة، والثاني إن أفاد جزما يقينيا فهو البرهان، وإلا فإن اعتبر فيه عموم الاعتراف من العامة أو التسليم من الخصم فهو الجدل، وإلا فهو المغالطة. واعلم أن المغالطة إن استعملت في مقابلة الحكيم يسمى سفسطة.
القياس: هذا بيان الصناعات الخمس وهي أقسام الدليل باعتبار مادته كما أن الأقسام السابقة أقسام باعتبار صورته. (أبو الفتح فكذلك ينقسم إلخ إن قلت لم قدم مباحث الصورة على مباحث المادة؛ مع أن العكس أنسب؛ إذ المادة مقدمة على الصورة؛ لكونها معروضة للصورة :قلت لأن الصورة أشرف من المادة؛ فإن الشيء من الصورة بالفعل ومن المادة بالقوة فللصورة تقدم بالشرف على المادة؛ لأن القياس ينتج على تقدير تسليم المقدمات إذا كانت الصورة صحيحة وإن كانت المادة فاسدة كما هو الظاهر في قولنا زيد حجر، وكل حجر ناطق، فزيد ناطق بخلاف ما إذا كانت الصورة فاسدة؛ فإنه حينئذٍ لا ينتج وإن كانت المادة صحيحة كما إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الحيوان صاهل. إسماعيل) لأن مقدماته إلخ وجه ضبط الصناعات الخمس وانقسام القياس إليها بحسب المادة. والثاني الشعر مثل: الخمر ياقوتية سيالة، والعسل مرة مهوّعة، وهذه المقدمات التي تفيد التخييل كثيرا ما يأخذه الشعراء في أشعارهم. إما أن يفيد ظنا إلخ الظن هو الطرف الراجح والجزم ما لا يحتمل النقيض الجدل: هو الخصومة، وفي الاصطلاح قياس مؤلف من قضايا مشهورة أو مسلمة لإنتاج قول آخر. والجدلي قد يكون سائلا، وغاية سعيه إلزام الخصم وإقحام من هو قاصر من إدراك مقدمات البرهان، وقد يكون بجيبا، وغرضه أن لا يطرح مطرح الإلزام. (تحفه) فهو المغالطة المغالطة في الاصطلاح قياس فاسد، إما من جهة المادة أو من جهة الصورة أو من جهتهما معا يفيد التصديق الجز مي أو الظني الغير المطابق للواقع. واعلم إلخ حاصلة المغالطة قسمان: الأول: ما يغلط للحكيم فهو سفسطة في الاصطلاح، والثاني لغيره فهو مشاغبة والمغالطة قياس فاسد صورة أو معنى. (برهان)
يسمى
He said: 'al-qiyas' etc.: Just as qiyas is divided according to form and figure into exceptional and conjunctive with their divisions, so too it is divided according to matter into the five arts: demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, and sophistry, which is also called sophism. Because its premises either effect assent or another effect other than assent, i.e., imagination. The second is poetry. The first either yields opinion or certainty. The first (yielding opinion) is rhetoric. The second (yielding certainty) if it yields certain certainty is demonstration. Otherwise, if it involves general acceptance by the common people or concession by the opponent, it is dialectic. Otherwise, it is sophistry. Know that sophistry if used against a sage is called sophism. Qiyas: This is an explanation of the five arts, which are divisions of the proof according to matter, just as previous divisions were according to form. (Abu al-Fath) Thus it divides etc. If you say: Why did he present the topics of form before the topics of matter, when the opposite would be more appropriate because matter is prior to form since it is the subject of form? I say: Because form is nobler than matter; for a thing is in act by form and in potentiality by matter. So form has precedence in nobility over matter. Because qiyas produces a conclusion on the condition of the premises being conceded, if the form is valid even if the matter is corrupt, as is obvious in our saying: 'Zayd is a stone, and every stone is articulate, so Zayd is articulate.' Unlike when the form is corrupt, then it does not produce a conclusion even if the matter is correct, as if we say: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a neigher.' (Isma'il) Because its premises etc. This is a way of regulating the five arts and the division of qiyas into them according to matter. The second is poetry, like: 'Wine is a flowing ruby, honey is a nauseating bile.' These premises that produce imagination are often taken by poets in their poems. Either it yields opinion etc.: Opinion is the preponderant side, and certainty is what does not admit of the opposite. Dialectic: It is disputation, and in terminology it is a syllogism composed of well-known or conceded propositions to produce another statement. The dialectician may be a questioner, his aim being to force the opponent and to corner one who is incapable of grasping the premises of demonstration; or he may be an answerer, his purpose being not to be placed in a position of being forced. (Tuhfah) So it is sophistry: Sophistry in terminology is a corrupt syllogism, either from the aspect of matter or from the aspect of form or from both together, yielding absolute or probable assent not corresponding to reality. Know etc.: The outcome is that sophistry is of two types: first, what is used to deceive the sage, called sophism in terminology; second, what is used for others, called مشاغبة (disturbance). And sophistry is a corrupt syllogism in form or meaning. (Burhan) It is called.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a sharh expanding on the classification of qiyas (syllogism) into five arts based on the material (premises). It begins by noting that qiyas is divided by form into conjunctive and exceptional, and by matter into the five arts: burhan (demonstration), jadal (dialectic), khataba (rhetoric), shi'r (poetry), and mughalata (sophistry). The division is based on the effect of premises: they either produce assent (tasdiq) or some other effect like imagination (takhayyul). Poetry produces imagination; the rest produce assent. Assent is either opinion (zann) or certainty (jazzm). Rhetoric yields opinion; demonstration yields certain certainty; dialectic yields concession or common acceptance; sophistry yields false certainty or opinion. The text then addresses an objection: why discuss form before matter? The answer is that form is nobler because it actualizes potentiality, and a syllogism can produce a conclusion with correct form even if matter is false (example: Zayd is a stone, all stones are articulate → Zayd is articulate), but not with correct matter and corrupt form (example: all humans are animals, some animals neigh). The commentary continues with definitions of each art: poetry uses imaginative premises; opinion is preponderant belief; certainty is indubitable; dialectic uses well-known or conceded premises; sophistry is a corrupt syllogism that deceives. The passage ends with a note that sophistry against a sage is called safsata. This is an expansion-probe because the commentator clarifies and justifies the classification and order.

Key Terms

الجدل
الخطابة
الشعر
المغالطة
الصورة
المادة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the five arts yields certain knowledge?
A) Dialectic
B) Rhetoric
C) Demonstration
D) Poetry

Key Takeaway
The five arts classify syllogisms by the epistemic effect of their premises: certain knowledge, opinion, imagination, or deception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following syllogism into one of the five arts and explain your reasoning: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (certain premise from Quran). Nabidh is an intoxicant (disputed premise). Therefore nabidh is forbidden.'
?
Identify which art is being used when a poet says: 'The wine is a flowing ruby, so drink it.' Does this syllogism produce assent or imagination?
