The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Why Form is Nobler than Matter in Syllogistic
Explains why form is studied before matter in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical priority of form over matter using the commentator's argument and examples from the Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Form actualizes the syllogism; valid form yields a conclusion even from false premises.

The commentator anticipates an objection: why discuss form before matter when matter is the subject of form? His answer reveals a key principle: form is nobler because it brings the syllogism to actuality. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for evaluating arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says: matter is prior to form because matter is the subject (ma'rud) of form. So logically, matter should be discussed first.
This is a natural assumption from a hylomorphic perspective.
2
Grasp the response: form is nobler
The commentator replies that form is nobler (ashraf) because a thing is in act (bil-fi'l) by form and in potentiality (bil-quwwa) by matter. So form has precedence in nobility.
Actuality is more perfect than potentiality.
3
Analyze the examples
Example 1 (valid form, false matter): 'Zayd is a stone, every stone is articulate → Zayd is articulate.' The conclusion follows, though premised are false. Example 2 (corrupt form, true matter): 'Every human is animal, some animal neighs → ?' No valid conclusion.
This shows that form determines the syllogism's ability to produce a conclusion.
4
Connect to Islamic argumentation
In kalam, the validity of the huduth argument for Allah's existence depends on its form (first figure). Even if opponents question the premises, the form itself is sound and logically compelling.
Form is what makes the argument valid regardless of premise truth.
5
Evaluate the commentator's reasoning
The commentator uses a teleological reasoning: form actualizes the potential of matter. This is not just a pedagogical choice but reflects the nature of syllogistic.
This argument is internally valid and consistent with Aristotelian hylomorphism.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Classifying Syllogisms by Material: The Five Arts
Author defines the five arts of Qiyas and distinguishes them by their material.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any syllogism into one of the five arts (demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, sophistry) based on the epistemic quality of its premises.

Core Idea
The five arts of syllogism are defined not by form, but by the certainty or type of assent their premises produce.

In logic, the material of a syllogism — the nature of its premises — determines its art. Just as a carpenter uses wood or metal, a logician uses certain premises for demonstration, famous premises for dialectic, and so on. This lesson will guide you through the classification tree that puts every syllogism into its proper art.

Guided Path
1
Identify the effect of the premises
Determine whether the syllogism's premises produce assent (tasdiq) or imagination (takhyeel). This is the first branching point.
If it produces imagination, it belongs to poetry (shi'r).
2
If assent, check the degree of belief
Among syllogisms that produce assent, decide whether they yield opinion (zann) or certainty (jazm). Opinion leads to rhetoric, certainty to the remaining arts.
3
For certainty, examine the premises
If the syllogism yields certainty, check whether all its premises are certain (yaqiniyya). If yes, it is demonstration (burhan). If not, proceed.
Demonstration requires all premises to be certain.
4
If not all certain, look for acceptance
Among syllogisms that yield certainty but do not have all certain premises, check if the premises are widely accepted by the public (mashhoorat) or conceded by the opponent (musallamah). If yes, it is dialectic (jadal). If no, it is sophistry (mughtalata).
Dialectic relies on famous or accepted premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل قوله: القياس كما ينقسم باعتبار الهيئة والصورة إلى الاستثنائي والاقتراني بأقسامها فكذلك ينقسم باعتبار المادة إلى الصناعات الخمس أعني البرهان والجدل والخطابة والشعر والمغالطة، وقد يسمى سفسطة أيضاً؛ لأن مقدماته إما أن تفيد تصديقا أو تأثيرا آخر غير التصديق أعني التخييل، والثاني الشعر والأول إما أن يفيد ظنا أو جزما، فالأول الخطابة، والثاني إن أفاد جزما يقينيا فهو البرهان، وإلا فإن اعتبر فيه عموم الاعتراف من العامة أو التسليم من الخصم فهو الجدل، وإلا فهو المغالطة. واعلم أن المغالطة إن استعملت في مقابلة الحكيم سميت سفسطة، وإن استعملت في مقابلة غير الحكيم فهو مشاغبة، واعلم أيضاً أنه يعتبر في البرهان أن يكون مقدماته بأسرها يقينية بخلاف غيره من الأقسام مثلا: يكفى في كون القياس مغالطة أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه وهمية وإن كانت الأخرى يقينية نعم يجب أن لا يكون فيها ما هو أدون منها كالشعريات وإلا يلحق بالأدون، فالمؤلف من مقدمة مشهورة أخرى مخيلة لا يسمى جدليا بل شعريا فاعرفه
Chapter. His saying: 'Qiyas' etc. Qiyas, just as it is divided with respect to form and figure into conditional and conjunctive with their subdivisions, similarly it is divided with respect to matter into the five arts: demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, and sophistry, and it is also called sophistry; because its premises either yield assent or another effect other than assent, namely imagination; the second is poetry; and the first either yields opinion or certainty; the first is rhetoric; and the second if it yields certain certainty, it is demonstration; otherwise, if general recognition from the public or submission from the opponent is taken into account, it is dialectic; otherwise it is sophistry. Know that sophistry if used against the sage is called sophistry [safastah], and if used against someone other than the sage it is called wrangling. And know also that in demonstration it is required that all its premises be certain, unlike other divisions. For example, in a sophistical syllogism, it suffices that one of its premises is suppositional even if the other is certain. However, it must not contain anything inferior to those, like poetic premises, otherwise it would be subsumed under the inferior. Thus a syllogism composed of a famous premise and an imaginative one is not called dialectical but poetic. So understand.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage divides Qiyas into five arts based on the nature of their premises (material), contrasting with the earlier division based on form. The author begins by stating that Qiyas can be divided by form into conditional and conjunctive, and by matter into demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, and sophistry. The classification proceeds by asking what effect the premises produce: either assent (tasdiq) or imagination (takhyeel). Poetry produces imagination. Among those producing assent, some yield opinion (zann) — that is rhetoric — and others yield certainty (jazm). Certainty that is certain (yaqini) is demonstration; otherwise, if the premises are widely accepted (mashhoorat) or conceded by the opponent (musallamah), it is dialectic; if neither, it is sophistry. The author further distinguishes sophistry: when used against a sage (hakim), it is called safastah; when used against others, it is mushaghbah. A key point: demonstration requires all premises to be certain, while sophistry can have one certain and one suppositional premise. However, if a syllogism contains premises inferior to those of its category (e.g., poetic in dialectic), it is reclassified to the inferior art. Thus a mix of famous and imaginative premises becomes poetic, not dialectical.

Key Terms
قياس
برهان
جدل
خطابة
شعر
مغالطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a demonstrative syllogism (burhan) from a dialectical one (jadal)?
A) Burhan yields certainty; jadal yields opinion.
B) Burhan has all certain premises; jadal has famous or accepted premises.
C) Burhan is for experts; jadal is for the public.
D) Burhan uses categorical syllogisms; jadal uses conditional ones.

Key Takeaway
The classification into five arts depends solely on the material of the premises, not on the form of the syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following syllogism: 'Every created thing has a cause; the world is created; therefore the world has a cause.' If both premises are certain, what art is it? (Answer: Demonstration)
?
Classify: 'All reasonable people agree that stealing is bad; therefore you should not steal.' If the premise is widely accepted (mashhoor), what art is it? (Answer: Dialectic)

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Sophistry and Its Subtypes: Safastah and Mushaghbah
Author defines the five arts of Qiyas and distinguishes them by their material.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sophistry from other arts and identify its subtypes based on the target audience.

Core Idea
Sophistry is the art of false reasoning; when aimed at the sage it is safastah, when aimed at others it is mushaghbah.

Among the five arts, sophistry is unique because it does not aim at truth but at deception. The author further divides it into two types depending on the intended victim. This lesson will help you recognize sophistical arguments and understand the moral and intellectual implications of their use.

Guided Path
1
Identify a non-demonstrative, non-dialectical, non-rhetorical, non-poetic syllogism
First, ensure the syllogism does not meet the criteria for the other four arts: it does not produce certainty from certain premises (burhan), nor is it based on famous/accepted premises (jadal), nor does it yield opinion (rhetoric) or imagination (poetry).
Sophistry often uses a mix of certain and suppositional premises.
2
Determine the intended effect
Sophistry aims to deceive, producing either a false certainty or a false opinion. This distinguishes it from genuine reasoning.
3
Identify the target audience
If the sophistical argument is used against a sage (hakim), it is called safastah (pure sophistry). If used against a non-sage (e.g., a layperson or student), it is called mushaghbah (wrangling).
The same argument can be classified differently based on the target.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل قوله: القياس كما ينقسم باعتبار الهيئة والصورة إلى الاستثنائي والاقتراني بأقسامها فكذلك ينقسم باعتبار المادة إلى الصناعات الخمس أعني البرهان والجدل والخطابة والشعر والمغالطة، وقد يسمى سفسطة أيضاً؛ لأن مقدماته إما أن تفيد تصديقا أو تأثيرا آخر غير التصديق أعني التخييل، والثاني الشعر والأول إما أن يفيد ظنا أو جزما، فالأول الخطابة، والثاني إن أفاد جزما يقينيا فهو البرهان، وإلا فإن اعتبر فيه عموم الاعتراف من العامة أو التسليم من الخصم فهو الجدل، وإلا فهو المغالطة. واعلم أن المغالطة إن استعملت في مقابلة الحكيم سميت سفسطة، وإن استعملت في مقابلة غير الحكيم فهو مشاغبة، واعلم أيضاً أنه يعتبر في البرهان أن يكون مقدماته بأسرها يقينية بخلاف غيره من الأقسام مثلا: يكفى في كون القياس مغالطة أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه وهمية وإن كانت الأخرى يقينية نعم يجب أن لا يكون فيها ما هو أدون منها كالشعريات وإلا يلحق بالأدون، فالمؤلف من مقدمة مشهورة أخرى مخيلة لا يسمى جدليا بل شعريا فاعرفه
Chapter. His saying: 'Qiyas' etc. Qiyas, just as it is divided with respect to form and figure into conditional and conjunctive with their subdivisions, similarly it is divided with respect to matter into the five arts: demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, and sophistry, and it is also called sophistry; because its premises either yield assent or another effect other than assent, namely imagination; the second is poetry; and the first either yields opinion or certainty; the first is rhetoric; and the second if it yields certain certainty, it is demonstration; otherwise, if general recognition from the public or submission from the opponent is taken into account, it is dialectic; otherwise it is sophistry. Know that sophistry if used against the sage is called sophistry [safastah], and if used against someone other than the sage it is called wrangling. And know also that in demonstration it is required that all its premises be certain, unlike other divisions. For example, in a sophistical syllogism, it suffices that one of its premises is suppositional even if the other is certain. However, it must not contain anything inferior to those, like poetic premises, otherwise it would be subsumed under the inferior. Thus a syllogism composed of a famous premise and an imaginative one is not called dialectical but poetic. So understand.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage divides Qiyas into five arts based on the nature of their premises (material), contrasting with the earlier division based on form. The author begins by stating that Qiyas can be divided by form into conditional and conjunctive, and by matter into demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, and sophistry. The classification proceeds by asking what effect the premises produce: either assent (tasdiq) or imagination (takhyeel). Poetry produces imagination. Among those producing assent, some yield opinion (zann) — that is rhetoric — and others yield certainty (jazm). Certainty that is certain (yaqini) is demonstration; otherwise, if the premises are widely accepted (mashhoorat) or conceded by the opponent (musallamah), it is dialectic; if neither, it is sophistry. The author further distinguishes sophistry: when used against a sage (hakim), it is called safastah; when used against others, it is mushaghbah. A key point: demonstration requires all premises to be certain, while sophistry can have one certain and one suppositional premise. However, if a syllogism contains premises inferior to those of its category (e.g., poetic in dialectic), it is reclassified to the inferior art. Thus a mix of famous and imaginative premises becomes poetic, not dialectical.

Key Terms
مغالطة
سفسطة
مشاغبة
حكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes safastah from mushaghbah?
A) The type of syllogism used
B) The target audience
C) The number of premises
D) The form of the syllogism

Key Takeaway
The classification of sophistry into safastah and mushaghbah depends on the dignity of the target, not on the argument itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Suppose an argument uses a premise that is only suppositional (wahmi) to conclude something false. If this argument is used against a philosopher (hakim), is it safastah or mushaghbah? (Answer: Safastah)
?
If the same argument is used against a layperson, what is it called? (Answer: Mushaghbah)
